\heb חֹק
\pos noun, m.
\incpos noun, f.
\gloss law
\sub חקה ,חֻקָּה ,הקק
\intro From the verb HQQ, which means to inscribe on a hard surface, or to decree, it refers to an authoritarian decree, or law, most often with the sense of permanence:1. a permanent and binding law, an enduring custom, or a daily task prescribed by God or by a person in authority.3. a fixed and permanent allotment, or provision for designated individuals decreed by God or by a person in authority.4. a person's lot in life as decreed by God, whether it is fate or the fulfilment of a promise.5. the natural order and boundaries of the universe as set by God.7. Decisions, plans, and resolutions.The most frequent context of this word is the Mosaic Law, especially in reference to laws and special allotments for the priests.
\comp All of the senses of XOQ involve 1) the domain of authoritarian action or decree, and 2) the sense of permanence or regularity. In the first sense, the recipient of the XOQ is required to perform certain actions. In the second sense, the beneficiary of the XOQ is the one who receives an allotment that is designated for him specifically. The third and fourth senses consist of an authoritarian action on the part of God that concern the lot of humans (sense 3), and the limits and laws of nature (sense 4). The sense of permanence is re-inforced in a number of instances by the qualifying word 'OLAM (lasting) as in ----, and also in reference to natural boundaries and laws set by God.  When referring to legislation, the term XOQ is used to highlight the permanence of the laws. In the case of prescribed duties for servants (Sense 1, context h), however, the sense of permanence seems to be less prevalent; here XOQ seems to focus more on the sense of a fixed set of tasks for the day.
\dfnotes 1. The feminine form of XOQ, XUQQAH semantically overlaps with XOQ; however there is a different distribution of usage. a. XOQ is found more in reference to non-specified laws, while XUQQAH seems to be preferred in the context of specific laws. However, both terms can be found in both contexts. b. XOQ refers to the permanence of laws, while XUQQAH seems to focus more on the feature of repetition of laws and customs.  Thus, XUQQAH is used more frequently to refer to customs and traditions of other peoples.c. In the context of natural laws, XOQ refers more to the limits and order of nature, while XUQQAH refers to the repetitious acts of nature. 2. See MISHPAT, ''EDUT, MITSWAH, TORAH
\level2 1
\meaning A fixed, permanent requirement for a group of people to act in a certain way. This requirement can be prescribed by God, a person, or a community who have power and authority over this people.
\frame Legislation, Customs
\domain Permanence
\level3 a
\context A specific law given by God, such as Passover
\framec Legislation
\domainc Deity; Covenant
\keyref $!>!1""
\eval Depending on the passage, translations vary: NJPS uses 'law', occasionally 'decree'. NIV, NRSV and others use 'statute', occasionally 'decree', 'regulation', 'rules', etc.. It appears that XOQ does not refer to a particular type of law, but is rather the expression of an aspect of a regulation - that of a binding and permanent prescription. The most generic term for this sense in English is 'law'.

\level3 b
\context A generic term for laws given by God through Moses
\framec Legislation
\domainc Deity: Covenant
\syntagcc Always in the plural form. In Deuteronomy, it is a generic reference for a previously mentioned list of legislative terms.
\keyref #!*!+%!
\trans In contexts where the the term is clearly generic, a generic term such as 'law' would be appropriate.

\level3 c
\context A unspecified type of law.  It is often found in a list with other legal terms; in the poetic context it is frequently placed in parallel construction with these same legal terms. While it is difficult to ascertain precise meaning for this word is these contexts, there are nonetheless some interesting patterns. a. frequent pairing with MISHPAT(IM) especially in Deuteronomy and Ezekiel; b. This pairing is, in several passages, associated in a part-whole relationship with TORAH, i.e. XOQ and MISHPATIM are considered to be the primary ingredients of the TORAH, the body of laws given by God through Moses. c. In Psalms 119, the verb LIMED (learn, teach) is more frequently associated with XOQ than with the other legal terms found in the Psalm. See the collocational references below for more patterns. It should be noted that for Psalms 119, I have only noted collocations found in the same verse as XOQ.
\framec Legislation
\domainc Covenant; Praise; Learning/Teaching; Meditation

\heb חֻקָּה
\pos ncf
\intro The general sense for HUQQAH is : A customary activity that is established or required by an authority. It is applied to the following contexts:1. God establishing the customary activity of natural celestial cycles which provide night and day, and seasons.2. God requiring the people of Israel to do specific customary activities, especially religious rituals3. Nations and kings establishing customary activities which are to be practiced by their people. This can include both good and bad practices, such as idolatry.
\level2 1
\meaning Established customary activity by God, of natural celestial cycles and seasons
\frame Divine order
\domain Universe; Celestial movements

\heb חקק
\strong B3411
\alt חוּקֹק
\pos verb
\gloss inscribe, engrave
\freq 19
\sub חקה ,(חֻקָּה)‏ חֹק
\intro The concrete meaning of |hחקק|h* is : To use a tool to draw, inscribe or carve out on a surface. There are three primary extended meanings :1. To set in place the sky and the foundations of the earth,2. To inscribe in one's memory, and3. To decree a law, or lead a people.The third meaning has futher extended uses:1. A military or political ruler2. A divine lawgiver, i.e. God3. A ruler's staffThis ruler's staff is also used to express the sense of leadership, or headship over a people, as in the ruler's staff being alotted to Judah.
\comp |hחקק|h* carries first and foremost the sense of - to make a permanent mark:1. make a permanent mark on a surface2. make a permanent mark in one's memory3. make a permanent mark in creating the universe The extension into the Legal Frame involves the addition of the authority domain, and can be seen in two ways : 1. Laws and decrees are seen as permanent and binding2. Laws, in the Middle Eastern context were written down, often engraved in stone. Thus it is conceivable that the act of inscribing a law on a surface was extended to mean simply to decree a law. From the sense - to decree a law - the participle form of the verb can have two senses:1. As Agent, meaning lawmaker, or governor2. As a concrete symbol of authority, meaning ruler's staffThe ruler's staff then also becomes a figurative expression meaning leadership. In a very literal sense, then, the participle form of the verb is used to define a leader as an inscriber of laws, and the one who holds the ruler's staff is the one who has the authority to inscribe laws.
\level2 1
\meaning To inscribe, engrave, write, draw, carve on a surface
\frame Mark
\gram The prepositions that can accompany the verbs with this meaning are |hעל|h* (on) and |hב|h* (in). One writes |ion|i* a surface, but one draws |iin|i* red, and one carves |iin |i*the rock.
\eventfr Agent: HumanPatient: The engraved markingLocation: The surface on which the marking was made
\level3 a
\context To write words
\framec Writing
\keyref 2!3!7'"
\keyverse If only my words were written down ; If only there were |binscribed|b* in a record! (KTBH)
\eval Most translations use a subjunctive form - |iOh, that my words were written down |i*(NJPS). This is correct from a grammatical and literary point of view, but it may not be the most natural speech. One translation collapses the parallelism into one statement : |iI wish my words could be written down (CEV)|i*. This is also acceptable, depending on what is considered poetic in the receptor language.
\ref 2!3!7'" 7!>!('!

This is also a possible interpretation (see Use Comments above). However, it might be preferable to remain closer to the original meaning of the verb, especially in the context of this verse, which describes God's different roles in relation to humans - as our judge, who decrees laws for us, and who is (after all) our king.

\dat 2005-04-27 user; 2006-01-19 20:28:04 Anne

\heb צוה
\pos v
\gloss command
\freq 496
\sub צִיּוּן ,צַו ,מִצְוָה
\intro 1. The verb |hצוה|h* is used to express any directive given by anyone in a superior, authority position to anyone under his authority as a means of imposing one's will on the other person and requiring them to do something. This meaning has various translations in English: |icommand, decree, instruct, give orders, tell someone to do something, charge.|i* It is used both in the context of rules and regulations, and laws, as well as in the context of orders for a specific one-time activity. There are also different authority relationships for which the |hצוה|h* may be used: divine-human, kings-subjects, parent-child, master-servant, etc.  When more than one type of relationship is involved between two people, the authority role may change depending on the context. For example, in Esther, Mordecai, as uncle, |icommands|i* Esther not to reveal her origins to anyone (Est .2:20). Later, it is Esther, as queen who |iorders|i* Mordecai to observe a fast (Est.4:17). So though she does not have authority over Mordecai as niece, she does have authority over him as queen, and thus can command him.2. One extension of this basic meaning is : |ito give authority and command|i* another person to do a specific task. One specific example of this is when Moses |icommissioned |i*Joshua to take his position as leader of the Israelites as they enter Canaan. This sense is also used in the context of |igiving final instructions |i*to one's household before dying, as well as |itransmitting a message and/or commands through an intermediary|i*. 3. A second extension of the meaning is used to express God's acts of power and creation. The verb means: |ito decree an event and to make that event happen, i.e. to cause something to happen|i*. Examples of this use can be found in God |icausing |i*acts in all of his creation, the skies, the earth, the animals, and even causing people to act a certain way, especially causing enemies to destroy the Israelites. He can also |icommand|i* blessings, loyalty His covenant, salvation, and victory.|i |i*.
\hebraist (Can TSIVAH mean promise? permit? There are a few contexts where these words are used by certain translations. Something to examine more closely. In these cases, it seems that the context guides the interpretation more than does the basic sense of the word. There are not enough instances that would allow for the validation of this sense. On the other hand, both of these words can be seen as sharing the domain of Authority. See below for more discussion.)
\level2 1
\meaning A generic term meaning to command, order, prescribe, decree, charge, with the power of authority to someone under that authority
\frame Prescription
\domain Authority; Directive; Speech; Communication
\eventfr Agent:Any person or deity who has some kind of authority over another person
\level3 a
\context To command, order, prescribe with the power of authority to someone under that authority
\ref "!B!@%! %!<!!,! )!-!-+!
\paradigc 
\level3 b
\context Man is not allowed to give orders to God!
\keyref 7!M!++!
\keyverse The Lord, the holy God of Israel, the one who shapes the future, says: “You have no right to question me about my children or to |btell me what I ought to do|b*! (GNT)
\eval This is a very interesting translation. Instead of literally translating the rhetorical questions and the Hebraic expression |ithe work of my hands|i*, as do almost all other translations (NJPS, NLT, NIV, CEV, REB, NRS - |iWill you question me about my children, or command me concerning the work of my hands?|i*),  GNT transforms the sentence into a very powerful exclamation, which correctly interprets the sense of the rhetorical question.
\ref 7!M!++!
\paradigc 
\level2 2
\meaning To communicate a regulatory prescription by someone with authority to someone under that authority
\frame Regulation
\domain Authority, Prescription; Directive; Speech; Communication
\gram The two most frequent uses of are : 1) as part of a speech margin : e.g. X |icommanded|i* (Y) (and said) and 2) within a relative clause in the expression : e.g. as I have |icommanded|i* you.
\level3 a
\context God communicates regulatory prescriptions either directly to people or through Moses
\domainc Covenant, Legislation
\eventfrc Agent: God
\syntagcc Most of the regulations which are decreed concern Mosaic law. They also include the law given by God to Adam and Eve, as well as limited time regulations, for example: laws regulating activities during the time in the wilderness, and laws applying to specific individuals. Regulatory prescriptions include regulations on any matter, from ritual obligations to moral and social laws, as well as the general command/exhortation to obey all of God's laws.

\keyref "!!!6!!
\keyverse Finally Pharaoh |bissued an order|b* to all his people which said: "Take every newborn Hebrew boy and throw him into the Nile, but let all the girls live." (KTBH)
\eval Translations use a varied number of terms. |iCommand|i* and |iorder|i* are the most frequent, but the following are also used by some translations on occasion: |iinstruct, tell.|i* Care should be taken not to weaken the sense of prescription, however. For example NLT rendering of 2SA.18:12 as converted the command into a request, by using |isay|i* in the speech margin, and including the word, |iplease |i*in the quotation: |iWe heard the king say to you...,'For my sake, please don't harm young Absalom.'"|i*

\keyref (!"!)'!
\keyverse ... I have |bwarned|b* the young men not to bother you...(NLT, CEV)
\eval |iWarn |i*is an equivalent translation for : |igive orders not to|i*. Implicit in this translation is the notion that retribution could be forthcoming if the order is not obeyed. It should be noted, though, that the term |iwarn|i* carries with it the sense of exhortation. It is debatable as to whether this is the case here. Other translations say : |iordered ...not to (NJPS, GNT, RSV)|i* and, |itold...not to (NIV, REB), |i*which may be more acceptable.

\level3 d
\context An elder family member gives orders to a younger family member
\domainc Family
\syntagcc In GEN.50:16, within the family context, Joseph's authority position as prime minister no longer counts. Joseph's older brothers give him orders. The same is the case for Esther. See EST.2:20 Meaning 2 (c). In GEN.27:8, Rebecca as mother gives orders to her son.
\keyref !!;!('!
\keyverse Now son, listen carefully and do what I |btell|b* you (NIV)
\eval The most frequent English terms in this category are : t|iell someone to do something,|i* and i|instruct someone to do something,|i* which reflects a less formal manner of expressing the same concept. Implicit with this translation is the notion that the orders given by a family member may not be as imposing as a royal or divine decree, for example. However, it should be borne in mind that in Hebrew culture, hierachical family relationships were taken very seriously, and the orders of elder family members were binding.Therefore care should be taken to choose an appropriate term that expresses the prescriptive nature of this directive.

\paradigc Passive
\pgloss to be commissioned to command
\refsc !!M!3"! "!B!B0!
\level2 5
\meaning To ordain or decree and cause an action or event to take place. God is both the agent of the decree and the causer of the action that He has decreed.
\frame Divine action
\domain Deity; Authority: Power; Speech
\eventfr Agent: God
\syntagc This meaning is found primarily in poetry and prophecy, used to emphasize God's greatness, power, authority, and role as creator. God make things happen and does not have to contend with a human who may or may not comply to his order. As a result, the sense of communication seems to be less salient here, while the sense of causation predominates.
\level3 a
\context God is the causer of all events and actions
\keyref 9!#!E'!
\keyverse Who can speak and have it happen if the Lord has not |bdecreed |b*it?(NIV)
\eval There are a variety of translations for this passage.  The NIV seems to be the closest to the basic meaning. There are two that carry the sense of approval or permission : |iNo one can do anything without the Lord's approval (CEV); Can anything happen without the Lord's permission? |i*These two renderings seem to include a certain theological interpretation that certain things happen but that they cannot be God's doing. As a result, it is assumed that in this case, |hצוה|h* can be interpreted as |iallow |i*or |ipermit.|i* However, the following verse makes it very clear that the writer is saying that both good and bad events come from God, thus re-inforcing the sense of |idecree|i* or |icausing to happen |i*in verse 37.
\ref 9!#!E'!
\paradigc 
\level3 b
\context Idols are not the causers of events
\domainc Idolatry
\keyref 7!P!%,!
\keyverse And so I predicted your future long ago, announcing events before they took place, to keep you from claiming that your idols and images |bmade them happen|b*.(GNT)
\eval This is a very natural way to express the sense of causing an event to happen. Other translations use the more traditional words of |iordain (NJPS, NIV, REB), |i*and |icommand (NLT, NRS).|i*
\ref 7!P!%,!

\domainc Creation

\eval All translations consulted agree that the word |icommand|i* can be used here.

\context God causes animals to do a specific activity

\ref +!1!$%! .!'!-&! >!)!#-!

\level3 e

\domainc Good attributes; Covenant
\keyref 3"K!#'!
\keyverse ...For there the Lord |bbestows|b* his blessing, even life forevermore. (NIV)
\eval Here translations use a variety of words which are acceptable depending on the language register intended: |iordain (NJPS, NRS), pronounce (NLT), bestow (NIV, REB). |i*The one rendering that may be debatable is |ipromise (GNT, CEV)|i*, Though the context may invite the sense of |ipromise|i*, it could seem slightly weaker in that a |ipromise|i* something that has not happened yet, whereas |iordain a blessing|i* brings the sense of making it happen.

\paradigc 
\level3 f

\framec Punishment
\domainc Enemy
\keyref >!&!+#!
\keyverse For the Lord will |bcommand|b*, And the great house shall be smashed to bits, And the little house to splinters. (NJPS)
\eval The word |icommand|i* is used by all translations consulted, though it is used in the figurative sense here.
\ref *!1!.,! 7!*!&&! 8!B!6"!|nc|n* 8!O!'$! 9!!!1'! 9!"!1(! >!&!+#! >!)!$&! >!)!)#! B!!!.!!
\paradigc 
\not (a) One translation uses the word 'promise' here, possibly because the action commanded was seen to be an act of God: ... your servants had heard that the Lord your God had promised His servant Moses to give you the whole land and to wipe out all the inhabitants of the country on your account.(NJPS) Other translations have retained the sense of command e.g.: ... Your servants were clearly told how the Lord your God had commanded his servant Moses to give you the whole land...(NIV).  This rendering, though more literal, is problematic also, because Moses could not have given something he did not have. NET Bible proposes an innovative solution, which seems a little more natural: how the Lord your God commanded Moses his servant to assign you the whole land.  
\notfr (a) NOTE
\notsp (a) NOTE
\notpt (a) NOTE
\notxx (a) NOTE
\not (b) These passages are all in the context of Moses passing on the authority of leadership to Joshua. While English translations generally agree that NUM.27:19 and 23 should be translated as 'commission', there is not a consensus for DEU. 3:21, 28; 31:14,23; and JOS. 1:9. A close study of these contexts shows that the sense of investing the authority for leadership is intended. In Numbers, God instructs Moses to commission Joshua in a public ceremony. In Deuteronomy 3, Moses reminds the people of that ceremony, and in Deuteronomy 31, it is God who directly invests the spiritual authority in a private meeting in the Tent of the Meeting, between God, Joshua, and Moses. In the grammatical structure of all these instances, 'Joshua' is always the direct object.
\notfr (b) NOTE
\notsp (b) NOTE
\notpt (b) NOTE
\notxx (b) NOTE
\not (c) Most translations retain the sense of 'give the command' here. However, it would seem that the sense here is 'God causing the armies to fight.'
\notfr (c) NOTE
\notsp (c) NOTE
\notpt (c) NOTE
\notxx (c) NOTE

\heb עוֹלָם
\level1 1
\pos ncm
\sub עלם
\comp Overwhelmingly used as an attribute (nominal or verbal); only once used referentially. It does not have intrinsic domains. Reference may be to the whole of a span of time; the start of a span of time, though, has a certain prominence, and may be, particularly in past time, an alternative referent.
\addinfo note collocation with a second 'olam or with 'ed; also make a list under each contextual meaning of doublets ('from 'olam to 'olam') which have been included
\level2 1
\meaning a long or unlimited span of time
\frame long stretches of time
\domain none
\level3 a
\context an unlimited span of time stretching into the future
\domainc various: four frequent collocations are 'covenant' (God's covenant with Israel), 'law', 'possession' and 'love' (God's love for Israel); others are 'the Davidic throne'
\syntagcc attributed of divine activities and purposes with respect to humankind, in particular with respect to the covenant community, and of appropriate activities of humankind, in particular the covenant community, towards God; also of rights, responsibilities and relationships within the covenant community. Note that the the span of time may start in the past.

\dat 2006-01-18 12:01:05 jbe

\heb צלח
\pos v
\gloss be successful
\freq 63
\intro Since understanding of how ‘success’ comes about is culturally conditioned, the translation of |hצלח|h* (‘be successful’) is likely to differ from language to language. In some it may be best expressed by the condition for success - ‘ability’ in some way; in others it may be best expressed by the outcome, such as an expression which covers ‘victory’. Since in scripture success (or lack of success) is divinely influenced, and also influenced by moral choices, the words chosen to translate ‘success’ should be able to carry these notions, rather than, for instance, to imply the possession of magical power.The frame of ‘success’ with respect to people requires: (i) a person who experiences or achieves the success; (ii) one of more events in which this person is successful (iii); a measure or way of distinguishing success from failure. While the first of these elements is always expressed in the Hebrew text, the other two may be unexpressed, to be inferred from the context. The extent to which this is acceptable in the language into which translation is being made will have to be determined; one or both of these elements may need to be made more explicit.It is likely that the concept of ‘success’ will need to be translated differently in one or more of the several domains that occur in scripture: Personal Welfare, Warfare (where ‘victory’ may be an appropriate translation), Land/Nation, Venture, God’s Purposes, Justice, Objects (see below). There is some overlapping of these domains (see comments at 5.1, SemanticNetworks.)In the domain of ‘objects’ (which extends ‘success’ beyond the frame used by English speakers), a different word may need to be used to translate ‘success’ than is used for the other domains. This composite domain of ‘objects’ collects together three instances: ‘belt’, ‘vine’ and ‘wood of vine’ (in the NIV and NLT, for instance, ‘thrive’ is used in the context of ‘vine’.)The grammatical subject of the Hebrew verb expressing ‘success’ may be the person or people seeking success, the activity in which success takes place or is being sought (e.g. Num 14:41), or an object used by the person or people seeking success (e.g. Isa 54:17).In scripture, the frame of ‘success’ is found in a positive sub-frame (obeying God results in success, e.g. Jos 1:8), a negative sub-frame (disobeying God results in failure, e.g. Deu 28:29), and in what could be called a contrary sub-frame (evil men sometimes are successful, e.g. Psa 37:7), which gains its force of meaning from the normality of the other two sub-frames.
\hebraist The verb |hצלח|h* may occur either in the qal form or the hiphil form. The hiphil form may or may not have a causative meaning. Whether the hiphil form takes on causative meaning or not is determined by the context.One issue that can be discussed is whether there is a distinction between the qal and the hiphil forms of this verb. Of the 55 usages of this Hebrew verb which refer to ‘success’ in the canonical Old Testament, the qal form is used 15 times and the hiphil form 40 times. Of these 40 uses, in some 30 of these instances the hiphil form is clearly used without causative meaning, that is to say only one agent is expressed; there are only about 10 instances of its use with clear causative meaning, that is to say two agents being expressed, one causing the other to be successful. All instances where the expressed grammatical subject is a thing, that is non-human, involve use of the qal form.One might surmise from this that the qal form is used where the subject is seen as less agentive. In other words that the hiphil form, even when only one agent is involved, is more agentive in some way (that is to say, the agent is seen more as causing himself to be successful.) We have labelled this role as agent/patient since the participant is always involved both in causing his success in some way and in experiencing it. However it does not seem possible to prove whether this distinction of agency (qal = less agentive, hiphil = more agentive, even when not causative) is correct. Western versions do not attempt to make such a distinction in their translations. In languages where degree of agency is marked, such a distinction might appear in translation. However, since the distinction cannot be proven, it is probably best in such languages to read agency out of the context rather than depend on a qal/hiphil distinction which cannot be substantiated.In two cases - Jdg 18:5 and Psa 1:3 - the hiphil form could potentially be analysed as causative (with God as causing agent), or with the activity (‘our road’, ‘all that he does’) as the grammatical subject. A causative analysis does not seem plausible in either case. In the first case the verb is clearly marked as having a feminine subject. In both cases (as additional evidence) the Septuagint translators understood the activity to be the subject.
\altint With respect to the plural verb ‘success’ in Jer 5:28, one question concerns who should be taken as the grammatical subject, whether the ‘wicked men’ of verse 26 or the ‘fatherless’ of this verse. Most modern translations render the clause as a purpose clause (c.f. Waltke and O’Connor 1990:650.) If the subject is taken as ‘wicked men’ it could be rendered (RSV) ‘they [wicked men] judge not with justice the cause of the fatherless, to make it prosper’, or (NIV) ‘they do not plead the case of the fatherless to win it’. If the subject is taken as the ‘fatherless’ it could be rendered: ‘that they [the fatherless] may have success’.Tawil (Tawil 1976) argues from a related language that the verb in this context should be understood as ‘provide/render justice’ and, claiming implicit negation from the context, translates the verse (with ‘wicked men’ as subject): ‘they judge not the cause, the cause of the orphan they do not justly render’.Although these three interpretations differ in detail, they are concordant as regards the resultant meaning. Tawil’s interpretation is more radical, since it involves the detail of an extended meaning for |hצלח|h* (‘provide/render justice’) and the need to adopt an implied negative; the resultant meaning in the context of the preceding and following clause is, nevertheless, the same.A fourth more literal possibility is represented by the Segond Révisé translation, but is more disjointed: ‘ils ne défendent pas la cause, la cause de l'orphelin, et ils prospèrent; ils ne font pas droit aux pauvres’/’they do not defend the cause, the cause of the orphan, and they prosper; they do not defend the right of the poor’. This disjointedness suggests that an original speaker/hearer would have understood the expression in a more meaningful way, however we lack sufficient access to original speaker/hearer knowledge to know what that would have been.If a grammatical subject or object has to be supplied, a compromise is to use ‘cause/case’ as the subject or object and render the clause as a purpose clause, as in the first two options presented above.
\dfnotes The domains of success that appear in Scripture overlap to some extent. The personal success or welfare of the king, for instance, will affect the welfare of the land or nation.The frame of ‘success’ requires: (i) a person (or, in the Hebrew frame, a thing) who achieves and experiences the success; (ii) one of more events in which this person is successful (iii); a measure or way of distinguishing success from failure. (This last could also be thought of as the ideal version of (ii), that is to say a successful version of the event or events.) The first of these elements will be expressed in the Hebrew text, but the other two may be unexpressed, to be inferred from the context. The extent to which this is acceptable in the language into which translation is being done will have to be determined; one or both of these elements may need to be made more explicit. There may also be other participants, specifically agents; such as a ‘causing agent’ who causes the events to be successful (always Divine); and in one case (Jer 2:37) a ‘secondary’ or ‘helping agent’.In scripture, the frame of ‘success’ is found in a positive form (obeying God results in success, e.g. Jos 1:8), a negative form (disobeying God results in failure, e.g. Deu 28:29), and in what could be called a contrary form (evil men are sometimes successful, e.g. Psa 37:7), which gains its force of its meaning from the normality of the other two forms.The inclusion of objects in the frame of ‘successfulness’ for the Hebrew language means that this frame is conceptually larger than the corresponding frame in English. It includes notions of ‘effectiveness’ and ‘productiveness’ (and these could be considered supplementary names for this frame.)
\level2 1
\meaning to achieve a designated or inferred event or state, or series of events or states
\frame Successfulness
\eventfr Causing Agent, if expressed: DivineAgent/Patient: Humanor Patient: Event or ObjectSecondary Agent: HumanTarget, if expressed: EventWe have considered the (active non-causative) grammatical subject to be both an agent and a patient (except in the case of things non-human, where it is only a patient), since he or she both works for the event to succeed and experiences the success or failure of the event. When the verb has causative meaning a causing agent will be present as grammatical subject as well as an agent/patient as grammatical object (or, in the case of two instances in Nehemiah, as indirect object marked by |hל|h*.)
\level3 a
\context have personal success
\domainc Personal Welfare

The NLT combines both as ‘succeed’. (In cultures where the order of events is important, ‘success’ may need to precede ‘prosperity’.) “Then you will be prosperous and successful.” (NIV Jos 1:8) “Only then will you succeed” (NLT Jos 1:8)

\level3 d
\context be successful of a king or people in a land
\domainc Land/NationThis domain of use will overlap with the domain of Personal/Welfare, particularly in the case of a king. However we have kept it separate since it involves a communal experience of success. The success of the king does not affect just himself; it also affects his people, the society, their destiny, and the land itself. Similarly for the success of a people in the land.
\eventfrc Causing Agent, if expressed: DivineAgent: HumansPatient: Humans, Society, Land

\heb אנף
\strong 00599
\pos v
\gloss to be angry
\freq 14
\sub אַף
\intro 1. The only distinction in meaning is between God being angry with someone, Meaning 1a, and someone being angry with someone else, Meaning 1b.2. God’s anger is always righteous. It is directed against a person or people who are wrong. It should not be translated by words which can only mean sinful anger.
\hebraist The Qal and Hithpael are used without obvious difference in meaning. The former, normally, and the latter, consistently, express the target with |hבּ|h*.
\comp 1 Focus: response; response: anger	1a Experiencer: God	1b Experiencer: human
\dfnotes See notes for other synonyms
\level2 1
\meaning To experience a state of extreme displeasure and an associated excitement of the mind
\frame Anger
\stem Qal and Hithpael
\eventfr Experiencer:     God (or once the Davidic king)Target:              human = individual or the nation of IsraelStimulus:          eventConsequence:  event
\level3 a
\context To experience rightly a state of extreme displeasure and an associated excitement of the mind
\domainc Apostasy and Judgement
\eventfrc Experiencer     = subject:  GodTarget              = |hבּ|h* or implicit:  human = individual or the nation of IsraelStimulus          = consecutive or implicit (or once |hעַל|h*, once |hבִּגְלַל|h*, once |hכִּי|h*):  eventConsequence = consecutive or infinitive or implicit:  event
\keyref +!+!)!"
\keyverse Yahweh |bwas angry |b*with Solomon because his heart had turned away from Yahweh, the God of Israel, who had appeared to him twice (KTBH)
\eval Any of the expressions 'be/become angry with Solomon' used by most translations would be a good model. CEV departs from this by leaving 'Solomon' out of the anger clause, 'this made the Lord very angry' and thereby losing the interpersonal aspect of the anger. This should be avoided.

\domainc Kingship, Rebellion

\keyref 3!"!,""
\keyverse Pay homage to the kingly son, lest |bhe be angry|b*... (KTBH)
\eval Most (RSV, NRS, REB, NJB, NJV) accept one of the alternative emendations|nb|n*, though all but REB appear to read |hיאנף|h* and render it 'lest he be angry' (NIV, RSV, NJB), 'or he will be angry' (NRS), 'lest he be angered' (NJV). TEV and CEV appear to have conflated the two references to anger in the verse.NIV (also ESV, NAS) capitalises 'Son' indicating a Messianic interpretation. TEV and CEV appear to accept the same interpretation as KTBH. NLT is a good model, "Submit to God's royal son, or he will become angry". It makes explicit the king's role as God's adopted son.
\ref 3!"!,"!|nb|n*
\paradigc Similar
\colc אַף
\pgloss anger
\refsc 3!"!,""
\paradigc Similar
\colc בער
\pgloss [anger] to burn
\refsc 3!"!,""
\not (a) Unusually, the target and the agent of the stimulus are different.
\not (b) Text critical, exegetical and hermeneutical questions abound in this verse. KTBH takes the view that |hבַּר|h*, although apparently Aramaic rather than Hebrew, does mean 'son' and is coreferential with 'my son' of v. 7. The psalm is taken to be a coronation hymn, in which David or the Davidic king is presented as being adopted by Yahweh (v. 7). The surrounding nations are envisaged as being in the rebellion against the king and refusing to kiss him, that is to pay homage to him.Alternative suggestions are based on change of vowels, change of consonants or redivision of vv. 11-12. For a discussion see Bratcher and Reyburn (UBS 1991), who reject the interpretation given here, and Craigie (WBC 1983) who defends it, noting that 'the use of |hבר|h* here avoids the dissonance of |hבן פן|h*'.A further question is whether the son in view is the Messiah, and thus whether 'Son' should be capitalised. In the original context of the psalm, the referent was probably the then King of Israel or Judah, and the psalm was not overtly prophetic, so the Experiencer was human and 'son' should probably not be capitalised.
\ver 0
\dat 2005-11-01 cjs

\heb אַף
\strong 00639
\pos Pc
\pos ncm
\gloss anger, nose, nostril, also
\freq 410
\sub אנף
\intro 1. There are two Hebrew words with the same form. One of them is a noun meaning either ‘anger’ or ‘nose, nostril, face’. The other is a particle meaning ‘also, even, indeed’.2. When the word means ‘anger’, there is a further distinction in meaning between God being angry with someone, Meaning 1a, and someone being angry with someone else, Meaning 1b. 3. God’s anger is always righteous. It is directed against a person or people who are wrong. It should not be translated by words which can only mean sinful anger.
\hebraist There is unanimous agreement that the particle |hאַף|h* and the noun are homonyms (BDB, HALOT, DCH, TDOT, NIDOTTE, TWOT). Equally there is agreement that the two meanings of the noun, 'nose, nostril' and 'anger', are related. The original meaning of the root |hאנף|h* and the precise nature of the relationship between the meanings of |hאַף|h*, however, are less certain.In some cases, the connection becomes explicit where other Hebrew words for 'anger' collocate with |hאַף|h* used in the sense 'nose'. TDOT cites, Ezk 38:18 and Psa 18:8-9, noting that 'in the OT the nose plays a certain role in the description of anger' (1.351). Similarly in Isa 65:5, 'these people are smoke in my nose' implying that they are a source of irritation and anger.One suggestion is that |hאנף|h* may originally have meant 'to snort' and thus form a link between 'nose' and 'anger' but this is not universally accepted (TDOT 1.351).
\comp 1 Focus: response; response: anger	1a Experiencer: God	1b Experiencer: human2 Meaning: nose, nostril or face3 Meaning: also, even, indeed
\dfnotes For notes see other synonyms. In addition:It may be helpful to distinguish positive and negative frames for divine anger:For the positive frame, the domain related to the stimulus event is apostasy, and that related to the consequence frame is judgement.For the negative frame 'turning from anger' the domain related to the stimulus event is repentance, and that related to the consequence frame is mercy.The negative frame is often expressed by a construction with |hשׁוב|h*, either with the verb in the Qal and |hאַף|h* as subject, often with a |hמִן|h* phrase of the former target, (x18), or with the verb in the Hiphil and God as subject (x5).Alternatively, it may be expressed by the Hiphil of |hנוח|h* with |hחֵמָה|h* as object, 'God gave rest to his anger'.
\level2 1
\meaning A state of extreme displeasure and an associated excitement of the mind
\frame Anger
\eventfr Experiencer:      God or humansTarget:               humanStimulus:           eventConsequence:  event
\level3 a
\context A right state of extreme displeasure and an associated excitement of the mind
\domainc Apostasy and Judgement, Repentance and Restoration, Opposition to Israel, Eschatological judgement
\eventfrc Experiencer     = genitive:                                           GodTarget              = |hעַל |h*or|h בּ|h* or implicit:                          human = an individual or a nation, Israel or anotherStimulus          = consecutive or implicit:                    eventConsequence = consecutive or infinitive or implicit:  event
\keyref 3!"!%$!
\keyverse Then he speaks to them in his |banger|b*, and in his fury he terrifies them. (KTBH)
\eval Most translations use two of the available English synonyms for the two Hebrew synonyms in the parallelism - 'anger, wrath' (NIV), 'anger, fury' (TEV), 'anger, rage' (NJB, NJV) or 'wrath, fury' (RSV, NRS). These are all good models, though, perhaps, since |hאַף|h* is the most common of the Hebrew synonyms, and 'anger' is the most common in English, 'anger' might be the best choice. REB renders the prepositional phrase 'in his anger' by the adverb 'angrily' which may also be followed. CEV renders the verse rather freely, 'The Lord becomes furious and threatens them'.

\eval Most translations render |hאַף|h* by 'anger' and use another of the available English synonyms for |hחֵמָה|h* in the parallel colon - 'anger, wrath' (NIV, RSV, NRS), 'anger, fury' (NJV), 'anger, rage' (NJB) or 'wrath, fury' (RSV, NRS). This may be appropriate in some languages. TEV, 'don't give in to ... anger' conflates the two Hebrew synonyms into one, 'anger', which may be appropriate if parallelism is being removed consistently.

\not (a) Unusually, the target is more extensive than the agent of the stimulus, suggesting corporate culpability.
\not (b) Most consider this to refer to God's anger, but Reyburn notes that the phrase 'in his anger' or 'by his nostril' may suggest the picture of a hot blast of breath through the nostril (UBS 1992).
\not (c) If |hמִקְנֶה|h* with the sense 'cattle' is retained, then |hאַף|h* is probably the particle rather than the noun. However, the Targum appears to have understood |hמִקְנֶה|h* as derived from the verb |hקנא|h*  'to be jealous', so a translation 'the wrath of indignation' is possible (Hartley, NICOT 1988, 477 n.17). Both HALOT and BDB recommend repointing as the Hiphil participle |hמַקְנֶה|h*. For other possible emendations of this difficult verse, see Pope (AB 1965, 238).
\not (d) Several Hebrew mss, supported by LXX and Tg, read |hחֶרֶב|h* 'sword' for |hחָרֹון|h* 'burning [anger]', in which case there is no collocating synonym. See |hחָרֹון|h*.
\not (e) Presumably an expression of anger or other emotion.
\not (f) |hרוּחַ אַפֹּו|h* should probably be translated as an anthropomorphism, 'the breath of his nose/nostrils' even though God's anger is also in the background.
\not (g) The idiom here, if indeed the text is correct, is unclear. LXX appears to have read |hאפס|h* for |hאפים|h*, '... although he loved Hannah ...'. Klein follows this reading (WBC, 1983). The Peshitta understood the phrase |hמָנָה אַחַת אפַּיִם|h* to mean 'a double portion', perhaps on the basis of the dual form. This understanding has been defended by KD on the grounds of a shift from |hאַף|h* to  'face' to 'person' and since it is dual, one portion for two persons. This has been followed by NIV and NRS. Other emendations have been proposed, see AB and UBS.Whatever reading is accepted, anger is not in view.
\not (h) The sense here is of smoke in (or 'from' see Anderson, WBC on 2Sa 22:9) God's nose, though his anger is also in view, as the preceding verse shows.

\heb זעם
\strong 02194
\pos v
\gloss to curse
\freq 12
\sub זַעַם
\intro 1. The verb means ‘to speak in anger’ or ‘to curse’, while the noun from the same root focuses mostly on the anger, itself.2. The only distinction in meaning is between God speaking in anger to someone, Meaning 1a, and someone God speaking in anger to someone else, Meaning 1b. 3. God’s anger is always righteous. It is directed against a person or people who are wrong. It should not be translated by words which can only mean sinful anger.
\hebraist The relationship between this noun and its cognate verb requires specific comment. The noun collocates frequently with words for 'anger', whereas the verb does not. In Num 23:7-8 and Pro 24:24, the verb collocates instead with words meaning 'to curse'. The relationship then may be that, for the verb, the meaning shifts from 'to be angry' > 'to speak in anger' > 'to curse', whereas for the noun, the meaning 'anger' dominates. Most instances of the verb and the noun, though not all, fit this pattern.
\comp 1 Focus: spoken response; response: angry speech, cursing	1a Speaker: God	1b Speaker: human

\meaning To pronounce a curse on someone out of a state of extreme displeasure and an associated excitement of the mind
\frame Cursing
\stem Qal and (x1) Niphal
\eventfr Experiencer:  God or humanTarget:           human
\level3 a
\context To pronounce a curse on someone out of a right state of extreme displeasure and an associated excitement of the mind
\framec Anger
\domainc Apostasy, Opposition to Israel
\eventfrc Speaker = subject or genitive or implicit:  GodTarget    = object or implicit:                      human
\keyref $!7!()!
\keyverse How can I curse those whom God has not cursed or how can I denounce those whom Yahweh |bhas |b*not |bdenounced|b* (KTBH)
\eval The problem here is the parallelism between |hקבב|h* and |hזעם|h*. Except for NJV 'damn', all render the former well by 'curse' and are left with a problem for the latter. The most common is 'denounce' (NIV, RSV, NRS, REB, NJB), which is not quite right but may be the best solution.

\heb זַעַם
\strong 02195
\pos ncm
\gloss anger
\freq 22
\sub זעם
\intro 1. The main distinction in meaning is between a focus on anger itself, Meaning 1, and  a focus on speaking in anger or cursing, Meaning 2. Compare this with the cognate verb which focuses more on the speaking.2. There is a further distinction between God being angry with someone, Meaning 1a, and someone being angry with someone else, Meaning 1b. 3. God’s anger is always righteous. It is directed against a person or people who are wrong. It should not be translated by words which can only mean sinful anger.
\hebraist The relationship between this noun and its cognate verb requires specific comment. The noun collocates frequently with words for 'anger', whereas the verb does not. In Num 23:7-8 and Pro 24:24, the verb collocates instead with words meaning 'to curse'. The relationship then may be that, for the verb, the meaning shifts from 'to be angry' > 'to speak in anger' > 'to curse', whereas for the noun, the meaning 'anger' dominates. Most instances of the verb and the noun, though not all, fit this pattern.

\eval Most translations use 'anger' for the |hאַף|h* in the second colon, and one of the available English synonyms for |hזַעַם|h* in the first: 'indignation' (RSV, NRS, REB), 'wrath' (NIV, NJV), 'fury' (NJB). This seems a better model to follow than TEV which reverses this 'anger, indignation' (CEV is similar), because it seems better to match the more common and more general words in the translation.

\not (b) Even though Jeremiah is the actual Experiencer, he is sharing in Yahweh's anger, cf Jer 6:11 'I am full of the anger of Yahweh'.
\not (c) The association with |hלָשֹׁון|h* and the occurrence of |hלַעַג|h* in the next clause suggests speech, probably in anger, against God or his prophets. Angry speech is also more consistent with the main use of the verb for cursing.

\heb זעף
\strong 02196
\pos v
\gloss to be angry, to rage, to be dejected
\freq 6
\sub זָעֵף, זַעַף, זַלְעָפָה
\intro 1. The main distinction in meaning is between someone being angry with someone else, Meaning 1, and someone being in less than normal physical or emotional health, and showing it, Meaning 3.2. The one instance of Meaning 2 is like Meaning 1, but refers to the violence of the sea, behaving as if it were angry.
\hebraist Most of the instances of |hזעף|h* and its cognates occur in contexts where the meaning 'anger' is appropriate. A few, particularly Gen 40:6 and Dan 1:10, cannot have that meaning, leading some to propose two homonyms. |hזעף|h* I 'to rage, be angry' is well attested in cognate languages, but the existence of |hזעף|h* II is debated. HALOT, TDOT and NIDOTTE all defend homonym II by citing an Arabic root|i ḍaʿif|i* 'look wretched, pitiful, dejected' though TDOT notes that |iḍ |i*does not correspond to Hebrew |hז|h*.BDB maintains a single root with a central meaning 'be out of humour' which develops in two directions 'be dejected' and 'be enraged' corresponding to the two main meanings presented here.
\comp 1 Meaning: to be angry	1a Experiencer: human2 Meaning: to act violently	2a Force: the sea3 Meaning: to be in less than normal health	3a Experiencer: human
\level2 1
\meaning To experience a state of extreme displeasure and an associated excitement of the mind
\frame Anger
\stem Qal
\eventfr Experiencer:  humanTarget:           human or GodStimulus:       event
\level3 a
\context To experience a state of extreme displeasure and an associated excitement of the mind
\domainc Folly, Rebuke
\eventfrc Experiencer = subject or genitive or implicit:  human or the heart, by metonymy for the personTarget          = |hעִם|h* or |hעַל|h* or implicit:                 human or GodStimulus      = implicit:                                     event
\keyref 4!3!#&!
\keyverse A person's folly ruins his life, but it is against Yahweh that his heart |brages|b*. (KTBH).
\eval Most translations render |hזעף|h* with '[his heart] rages' (NIV, RSV, NRS, REB, NJB, NJV). This is a good model to follow. TEV and CEV use 'blame', thereby making explicit the implicit step in the experience which might go 'experience ruin' > 'blame someone' > 'get angry with that someone'. This shift may be necessary if the 'blame' step is not obvious.
\ref .!:!3!! .!:!3&! 4!3!#&!
\paradigc 
\level2 2
\meaning To act violently, of the sea or a storm
\frame Meteorology
\stem Qal
\eventfr Force:  Sea
\level3 a
\context To act violently, of the sea
\domainc Sea
\eventfrc Force = genitive subject:  Sea
\keyref @!!!/'!
\keyverse They picked up Jonah and they threw him into the sea and the sea ceased its |braging|b*. (KTBH)
\eval Some translations follow the structure of the Hebrew closely: 'and the sea ceased from its raging' (RSV, NRS), 'and the sea stopped raging' (NJB, NJV). Others recognise that the raging is a property of the sea and translate 'the raging sea' (NIV), the raging of the sea' (REB). 'Rage' is a good rendering because the English and Hebrew match, though this may not be possible in other languages. TEV conveys the meaning without the word 'rage': '[the sea] calmed down' (CEV similar). Depending on translation style, any of these would be a good model to follow.
\ref @!!!/'!
\paradigc Opposite
\colc עמד
\pgloss to stand still
\refsc @!!!/'!
\level2 3
\meaning To be in less than normal health, either physical or emotional, and to show it
\frame Health
\eventfr Experiencer = subject: human
\syntagc The fact that Joseph saw, and that Nebuchadnezzar would have seen, that something was wrong suggests that the word refers to something visible. Joseph's reference to the men's faces being |hרָעִים|h* 'sad' also supports the element 'and to show it' in the definition.
\level3 a
\context To be in less than normal health, either physical or emotional, and to show it
\domainc Food, Dreams
\eventfrc Experiencer: human
\keyref !!H!&(!
\keyverse Joseph came to them in the morning and he saw them; they |bwere dejected|b* (KTBH).
\eval The precise nuance of this meaning of the term is hard to establish and eight translations render in six different ways, 'dejected' (NIV), 'troubled' (RSV, NRS), 'dispirited' (REB), 'upset' (TEV, CEV), 'gloomy' (NJB), 'distraught' (NJV). All fit the context well and all are consistent with the evidence for meaning. It would probably be best, however, to choose a translation equivalent which indicates that whatever was wrong was visible.

\colc פָּנִים רָעִים
\pgloss faces [are] dejected
\refsc !!H!&(!

\heb זָעֵף
\strong 02198
\pos a
\gloss angry
\freq 2
\sub זעף
\intro Both instances of the word refer to someone being angry with someone else.
\altint The majority view (HALOT, TDOT, NIDOTTE) is that the adjective is derived from |hזעף|h* I and has the sense 'angry'. It is worth noting, however, that |hזעף|h* II, with the sense 'out of humour, dejected' would also fit. In particular, the clause 'and he turned his face [away]' later in 1Ki 21:4 is consistent with dejection.
\comp 1 Focus: response; response: anger	1a Experiencer: human
\level2 1
\meaning In a state of extreme displeasure and an associated excitement of the mind
\frame Anger
\eventfr Experiencer:  humanTarget:           humanStimulus:       event
\level3 a
\context In a state of extreme displeasure and an associated excitement of the mind
\domainc Prophecy, Commercial Transaction
\eventfrc Experiencer = implicit:            humanTarget          = implicit:            humanStimulus      = |hעַל|h* or implicit:  event
\keyref +!4!K%"
\keyverse He went home sullen and |bangry |b*and he came to Samaria. (KTBH)
\eval The rarity and difficulty of both words |hסַר|na|n*|h* and |hזָעֵף|h* is reflected in the variety of translations for the pair: 'sullen and angry' (NIV, RSV), 'resentful and sullen' (RSV, NRS), 'worried and depressed' (TEV), 'angry and depressed' (CEV), 'gloomy and out of temper' (NJB), 'dispirited and sullen' (NJV). All of them fit the context as descriptions of Ahab's likely mood, but probably some term for 'anger' should be used.
\ref +!4!K%! +!5!$%!
\paradigc Similar
\colc סַר|na|n*
\pgloss stubborn, resentful, sullen, ill-humoured
\refsc +!4!K%" +!5!$%"
\not (a) This word is restricted to the same contexts as |hזָעֵף|h* in 1Ki 20:43; 21:4, 5.

\heb זַעַף
\strong 02197
\pos ncm
\gloss anger
\freq 5
\sub זעף
\intro 1. The only distinction in meaning is between God being angry with someone, Meaning 1a, and someone being angry with someone else, Meaning 1b.2. God’s anger is always righteous. It is directed against a person or people who are wrong. It should not be translated by words which can only mean sinful anger.
\hebraist The noun seems to be related to homonym I of the verb, not homonym II. See the discussion under |hזעף|h*.

\heb חֵמָה
\level1 1
\strong 02535
\pos ncf
\gloss anger
\freq 125
\sub יחם
\intro 1. Most instances of the word refer to anger or to the heat of anger, Meaning 1.2. When the word means ‘anger’, there is a further distinction in meaning between God being angry with someone, Meaning 1a, and someone being angry with someone else, Meaning 1b.3. God’s anger is always righteous. It is directed against a person or people who are wrong. It should not be translated by words which can only mean sinful anger.4. In a few instances, the word has other meanings, perhaps related to heat. One is ‘poison, venom’, Meaning 2a, and another is fever from wine, Meaning 2b.
\hebraist |hחֵמָה|h* is generally agreed to be derived from |hיחם|h*, though |hחמם|h* and |hיחם|h* are closely associated roots and there is even some doubt over which of the two roots gives rise to some of the Qal forms.|hחמם|h* means 'to be hot' in various senses, and |hיחם|h* also means 'to be' hot', but in a more limited sexual and reproductive sense. This sexual and reproductive sense, however, is not attested for |hחֵמָה|h*. Its most common sense is 'anger', though it occurs a few times in the senses 'poison, venom' and 'fever'. As TDOT notes, 'the notion of "being hot" ... probably furnishes the point of departure for all three meanings'.
\comp 1 Focus: response; response: anger	1a Experiencer: God	1b Experiencer: human2 Other meanings	2a Meaning: venom, poison	2b Meaning: fever from wine
\level2 1
\meaning A state of extreme displeasure and an associated excitement of the mind
\frame Anger
\eventfr Experiencer:  God or humanTarget:           humanStimulus:       event
\level3 a
\context A right state of extreme displeasure and an associated excitement of the mind
\domainc Apostasy and Judgement, Repentance and Restoration, Opposition to Israel, Eschatological judgement
\eventfrc Experiencer = genitive or implicit:  GodTarget          = |hבּ|h* or |hעַל|h* or implicit:  humanStimulus      = |hעַל אֲשֶׁר|h* or implicit:  event
\keyref 3!&!"$!
\keyverse O LORD, do not rebuke me in your anger or discipline me in your |bwrath|b*. (NIV)
\eval A problem here is the two words for 'anger' in the parallel cola. Most use two English synonyms: 'anger, wrath' (NIV, RSV, NRS, REB), 'anger, fury' (NJV) or 'anger, heat of wrath' (NJB). Use of the neutral 'anger' for the common |hאַף|h* is natural, but care is necessary for the other word to avoid wrong connotations. 'Wrath' is old-fashioned and used only for God's wrath. For this reason, it may be the best choice. 'Fury' conveys intensity and is appropriate for a temporary anger, which is not a settled attitude. [Murray asks - is this our job?]CEV collapses the parallel cola and needs only one word 'anger'; in this this case, this does not lead to loss of meaning.

\keyref 4!/!!#!
\keyverse A gentle answer turns away |brage |b*but a hurtful word rouses anger. (KTBH)
\eval Again, the problem here is the two words for 'anger' in the parallel cola. Most use the same two English synonyms: 'wrath, anger' (NIV, RSV, NRS, NJB, NJV). For human anger, 'wrath' has a distinctly old-fashioned sound, so perhaps an alternative such as 'rage' should be used.TEV and CEV retain the parallelism, but avoid the problem, by using a pronoun in the second colon: 'A gentle answer quietens anger but a harsh one stirs it up' (TEV). This seems a good example to follow if a language lacks a suitable synonym.

\not (a) The text is difficult. Many emend |hחֵמָה|h* to |hהֵמָּה|h* 'these things'. NICOT accepts this as the simplest way to make sense of the text, but WBC retains MT and translates the colon 'for [yours is] an anger worthy of the sword'.If MT is retained, the anger is probably divine and the verse should be understood, 'for wrath will bring punishment by the sword' (NIV).
\not (b) There is a minority view (REB, TDOT) which sees this as an instance of the '[heat of] wine', probably under the influence of Driver (cited by Watts, WBC). Watts (WBC), Oswalt (NICOT) and Young all reject this meaning and take |hחֵמָה|h* to have its normal meaning 'anger'.
\not (c) Even though Jeremiah is the actual Experiencer, he is sharing in Yahweh's anger, cf Jer 15:17 'you filled me with anger'.
\not (d) 'Anger' is difficult in this context. Many either delete or emend eg NICOT. If MT is retained, NRS is a good model to follow, 'Beware that wrath does not entice you into scoffing', though 'anger' might be better than 'wrath' unless God's anger is in view.
\not (e) There is considerable ambiguity here. For fuller discussion see NICOT. KTBH retains MT, follows HALOT in reading the noun |hחֵמָה|h* rather than |hחֵמֶת|h*, understands |hחֵמָה|h* in its normal sense 'anger' and takes the Experiencer as the addressee, the Babylonians.

\heb חרה
\level1 1
\strong 02734
\pos v
\inc חָרוֹן
\incpos ncm
\inc חֳרִי
\incpos ncm
\gloss to burn [with anger]
\freq 93
\intro 1. Most instances of the word refer to burning with anger, Meaning 1.2. When the word means ‘anger’, There is a further distinction in meaning between God being angry with someone, Meaning 1a, and someone being angry with someone else, Meaning 1b.3. God’s anger is always righteous. It is directed against a person or people who are wrong. It should not be translated by words which can only mean sinful anger.4. In a few instances, the word has other meanings. One is ‘to be worried or distressed’, Meaning 2a; another is ‘to compete’, Meaning 2b, and a third is ‘zealously’, Meaning 2c.
\hebraist Other ANE languages have a cognate root which has the meaning 'to burn' in a literal sense. This suggests that the literal meaning may also have been present in Hebrew, but in the HB the root only occurs in metaphorical senses, particularly 'to burn with anger'. It does however, collocate with literal fire, both in narrative (Num 11:1) and in poetry (2Sa 22:8-9, Psa 18:8-9).Other verbs |hעבר|h* 'to burn' (Psa 2:12, Isa 30.27) and |hעשׁן|h* 'to smoke' (Deu 29:19, Psa 74:1) also take |hאַף|h* (and other words for anger) as subject, though less often than |hחרה|h* and their literal meaning is more prominent.  |hחרה|h* is unique, however, in that the literal sense is unattested.Of the two main constructions of the Qal,|na|n* one makes the anger explicit, 'someone's anger burned', and the other is an impersonal construction in which the anger is implicit, 'it burned to someone' = 'someone was angry'. The latter construction is also attested in Hebrew for other emotions.|nb|n* There appears, however, to be little or no difference between the two constructions. The fuller expression is used for most instances of Yahweh's anger, but not consistently. The shorter expression is more common for human anger, but both occur.
\evalev HALOT proposes a second homonym, attested only at Isa 24:6. The Westminster Hebrew Institute, in their morphological database, however, follow the majority of authorities (BDB, NIDOTTE, gkc, Even-Shoshan) against HALOT and considers the form |hחָרוּ|h*, found at Isa 24:6, to be derived from the root |hחרר|h*.
\comp 1 Focus: response; response: anger	1a Experiencer: God	1b Experiencer: human2 Other meanings	2a Meaning: to be worried or distressed	2b Meaning: to compete	2c Meaning: zealously
\dfnotes See other synonyms for notes. In addition:1. 'Then David was angry because the Lord's wrath had broken out against Uzzah' (2Sa 6:8, NIV, similarly REB) is an informative mistranslation. The Hebrew makes Yahweh the subject of the verb |hפרץ|h*, but NIV makes Yahweh's anger into the subject, thereby demoting Yahweh in terms of the actor-undergoer hierarchy, and presenting him as being driven by his own anger. Others follow Hebrew more closely, making Yahweh the agent of the 'breaking out'.2. It is the event-type of the associated events that determines the domains involved. For example, for divine anger, the stimulus is typically apostasy and the consequence is judgement.
\level2 1
\meaning To experience a state of extreme displeasure and an associated excitement of the mind
\frame Anger
\stem Qal, Niphal|nc|n* (3x) or Hiphil|nd|n* (1x)
\eventfr Experiencer:      God or humanTarget:               human = individual(s) or a nation, Israel or another|ne|n*Stimulus:           eventConsequence:  event
\level3 a
\context To experience rightly a state of extreme displeasure and an associated excitement of the mind
\domainc Apostasy and Judgement, Opposition to Israel
\eventfrc Experiencer     = |hל|h* or genitive with |hאַף|h*:                          GodTarget              = |hבּ|h* or rarely |hעַל|h* or implicit:                    humanStimulus          = consecutive or |hעַל|h* (once) or implicit:  eventConsequence = consecutive or implicit:                        event
\keyref "!$!.!!
\keyverse The anger of Yahweh |bburned |b*against Moses and he said ... (KTBH)
\eval Some translations, such as NIV, preserve the Hebrew idiom, 'then the Lord's anger burned against Moses' (similarly RSV, NRS, NJB). This works well in English and could be followed in other languages where the idiom is natural. Other translations, such as NJV remove the imagery of burning and simplify the expression, 'the LORD became angry with Moses (similarly TEV, CEV, REB).The same options may be used whether the |hאַף|h* is explicit or implicit  in the Hebrew. (See the discussion of the two constructions above.)

\paradigc 
\not (a) A third construction with |hבְּעֵינֵי|h* also occurs, but only twice, both times in Genesis (31:35; 45:5) and both negated.
\not (b) Joüon and Muraoka note that impersonal constructions with |hל|h* occur with a 3ms verb, especially when they express an emotion. |hחָרָה לֹו|h* 'he is angry', |hחַם לֹו|h* 'he is hot', |hצַר לֹו|h* 'he is distressed', |hמַר לֹו|h* 'he is unhappy' and others (§152.d). See also gkc §144.b and ibhs §22.7.b.
\not (c) There are only three instances of the Niphal of |hחרה|h*. They show a different syntax in that the Experiencer is subject, but the target is still introduced by |hבּ|h*. Some commentators attribute reflexive significance to the Niphal form: thus KD renders it by 'burn inwardly' at Sng 1:6 and TOTC by 'keep themselves incensed' at Isa 45:24, but there is insufficient evidence to support a distinct meaning. Thus KTBH includes the Niphal with the more common Qal.BHS proposes repointing both instances of the Niphal in Isaiah as a form of |hנחר|h* 'snort [with indignation]' but this is 'unnecessary here, since MT is neither impossible nor improbable' (NICOT on 41:11).
\not (d) In Job 19:11, MT points as the Hiphil, but BHS and HALOT recommend repointing as the Qal. If MT is retained, the construction with the Hiphil may either be the same as the impersonal construction with the Qal, with |hאַפֹּו|h* as subject 'his anger burned', or there may be a causative nuance, 'he has kindled his anger', with |hאַפֹּו|h* as object.
\not (e) At Deu 29:26, the target is 'that land' but by metonymy it refers to the people of the land, the Egyptians
\not (f) Unusually, the target is more extensive than the agent of the stimulus.
\not (g) The syntax of Hab 3:8 seems to be unique. If MT is accepted, then |hיהוה|h* must be either the personal subject of |hחרה|h* or a vocative with elision of the |hלְךָ|h* normally expected with |hחרה|h*. Most commentaries and translations assume the latter, except for Robertson (NICOT 1990, 229) who appears to assume the former without comment. For detailed discussion, including defence of MT, see Andersen (AB 2001, 316-17).
\not (h) In this instance, self-anger is probably implied, which is rare in HB. The same construction, with |hבְּעֵנֵי|h*, occurs at Gen 31:35 but there Experiencer and Target are distinct.
\not (i) This may be an unusual instance of an inanimate target, but it is more likely that |hעַל|h* expresses the stimulus, 'about the plant', as the translations all agree and Price and Nida make explicit, 'It is important not to imply that Jonah was angry at the plant itself but because the plant had withered and died' (UBS).
\not (j) This gloss is taken from BDB. HALOT and BHS both recommend emendation to some form of |hיחל|h* 'to wait'. The normal meaning of the Hithpolel of |hחיל|h* hardly fits the context: 'to writhe with fear (HALOT) or 'to whirl, writhe' (BDB).
\not (k) This word appears to be a Hiphil form from |hחרה|h*, but it is difficult to assign a meaning to such a form in this context. The word is absent from LXX, probably either because it was missing from their |iVorlage |i*or because they did not understand it either. Those who retain MT generally translate 'eagerly, zealously' or the like, but, as Williamson notes, 'this is not a legitimate meaning of this word' (WBC). NICOT translates in brackets and WBC and AB omit. In the context of the present work in KTBH, the sense 'anger' is absent.

\heb חָרוֹן
\strong 02740
\pos ncm
\gloss burning [of anger]
\freq 41
\sub חרה
\intro 1. All but one instance of the word refers to the burning of anger, Meaning 1.2. When the word refers to ‘anger’, there is a further distinction in meaning between God being angry with someone, Meaning 1a, and someone being angry with someone else, Meaning 1b. 3. God’s anger is always righteous. It is directed against a person or people who are wrong. It should not be translated by words which can only mean sinful anger.4. In Psa 58:10, the meaning is very uncertain, Meaning 2.
\hebraist For discussion of the likely original sense 'burning' of the root see the cognate verb |hחרה|h*. Although the term is never used of literal burning,|na|n* it is used in contexts in which literal burning is a consequence of God's anger (eg Lam 4:11).In a similar manner to the verb, there are two constructions, the more common in which |hאַף|h* 'anger' is explicit, God's |hחֲרֹון אַף|h*, and the less common in which it is implicit, God's |hחָרֹון|h*. There appears to be little or no difference in meaning between the two constructions.
\comp 1 Focus: response; response: anger	1a Experiencer: God	1b Experiencer: human2 Meaning uncertain	2a Meaning uncertain
\dfnotes The domain, Apostasy / repentance, includes many sub-domains: Disobedience / obedience, Injustice, Law-breaking, The ban (|hחֶרֶם|h*). For further comments see other synonyms.

\keyverse Pour out your wrath on them and let your |bburning |b*anger overtake them. (KTBH)
\eval Most retain the 'burning' imagery and understand the construct pair |hחֲרֹון אַף|h* as an intensifying construction, 'burning anger' (RSV, NRS, REB, NJB) or 'blazing anger' (NJV). This works well in English and could be followed in other languages where it is natural. NIV mutes the imagery, but is otherwise similar, 'fierce anger'. It may be appropriate to use an intensifying adjective with a basic word for 'anger' in this way. Others simplify the expression to 'indignation' (TEV) or 'be furious' (CEV).

\eval NJB 'owing to the devastating fury', and NJV 'because of the oppressive wrath' translate MT and appear to understand |hהַיֹּונָה |h*as 'oppressing', and take it to qualify |hחֲרֹון|h*.  Most others accept emendation of |hחֲרֹון |h*to |hחֶרֶב|h*, and understand |hהַיֹּונָה |h*as a collective 'oppressors' eg NIV 'because of the sword of the oppressor' (Similarly RSV, NRS, REB, and probably TEV). CEV omits the entire second half of the verse.Depending whether or not the textual emendation is accepted, either NIV or NJV would be good models to follow.

\paradigc 
\not (a) With the possible exception of the very doubtful Psa 58:10 (see below).
\not (b) Though the difficult Jer 25:38a may be an exception (see note f).
\not (c) Only here in the plural. Tate takes it as an intensive or plural of amplification, citing gkc 124.e (WBC 1990). BDB suggests 'bursts of burning anger' probably assuming a plural of intensity.
\not (d) This assumes that the 3ms suffix refers to Yahweh (cf the similar phrase in v. 37) and not |hהַיֹּונָה|h*, understood as 'the oppressor'. However the commentaries handle the difficulties of the preceding colon, they agree that it is the anger of Yahweh that is in view.
\not (e) The Experiencer is ambiguous in this verse, but essentially the same phrase, differing only in an explicit 1cs suffix, refers to Yahweh's anger in v. 14, so that is likely to be the case in v. 12 too. There is, however, textual uncertainty in the sequence  |hחָרֹון|h* (v. 12), |hחָזֹון|h* (v. 13) and |hחֲרֹונִי|h* (v. 14), which looks suspicious and is missing from LXX. See Allen (WBC 1994).
\not (f) This assignment makes two assumptions:1) MT is correct, though several Hebrew mss, supported by LXX and Tg, read |hחֶרֶב|h* 'sword' for |hחָרֹון|h* 'burning [anger]' and2) the following genitive |hהַיֹּונָה|h* should be understood as 'the oppressor'.A good case can be made for accepting an emendation based on LXX if |hחָרֹון|h* was read by dittography from the colon that follows. Most translations and commentaries do so.If MT is retained, the next word |hהַיֹּונָה |h*is generally understood either as 'oppressor(s)' though it may also be an abstract noun 'oppression' (WBC).
\not (g) The meaning of v. 10, especially the second half is very uncertain. See, for example, Tate (WBC 1990). KTBH shares the view of Bratcher and Reyburn that, 'It would be of little practical use to list the many ways in which the text has been handled. No two translations agree completely, and all (including even NIV) have textual footnotes. Whatever course a translator takes, a note should indicate that the Hebrew makes little sense' (UBS, 1991).

\heb כעס
\level1 1
\strong 03707
\alt כעשׂ
\pos v
\gloss to be angry, provoke anger
\freq 55
\sub כַּעַס
\intro 1. The key meaning distinction is between someone being angry with someone else, Meaning 1, and someone making someone else angry, Meaning 2.2. There is a further distinction in meaning between God being angry with someone, Meanings 1a, 2a, and someone being angry with someone else, Meanings 1b, 2b. 3. God’s anger is always righteous. It is directed against a person or people who are wrong. It should not be translated by words which can only mean sinful anger.4. Meaning 3 is distinguished in Ezk 32:9, where the response in view is fear or horror. Elsewhere it is anger.
\hebraist 1. The two instances of the Piel both collocate closely with the Hiphil. Either they describe the same sequence of events (1Sa 1:6-7) or they connect two similar events (a cause and its effect; Deu 32:21). Both stems signal a causative meaning. The distinction is stylistic, not semantic (TDOT: 'the very rare piel appears to represent a stylistically motivated variant to a nearby hiphil').2. The cognate noun (see |hכַּעַס|h*) collocates with the verb, either in a simple cognate accusative construction (1Sa 1:6) or in a more complex relative construction eg |hבְּכַעְסוֹ אֲשֶׁר הִכְעִיס אֶת־יְהוָה |h*‘because of the provocation with which he provoked Yahweh’ (1Ki 15:30 cf 1Ki 21:22, 2Ki 23:26).3. The majority of instances of this root are spelled with a |hס|h*, but in the book of Job, it is spelled consistently with |hשׂ|h*. There appears to be no difference in meaning, so KTBH treats the two forms in the same entry as alternative spellings. TDOT and NIDOTTE suggest that the spelling with |hשׂ |h*may indicate a relationship to an Arabic cognate.
\comp 1 Stem: Qal; agent: null; experiencer: subject; target: human; focus: response; response: anger	1a Experiencer: God	1b Experiencer: human2 Stem: Piel and Hiphil; agent: subject; experiencer: object; target: = agent; focus: provocation; response: anger	2a Agent: human; experiencer: God	2b Agent: God or human; experiencer: human3 Stem: Hiphil; agent: subject; experiencer: object; focus: provocation; response: fear or horror: frame: horror	3a Agent: God; experiencer: human
\dfnotes With one exception (Ezk 32:9) the meanings of |hכעס|h* all relate to the same frame, labelled simply ‘anger’ in which the elements are: 	the anger event itself	an experiencer of the anger	an event which is the stimulus for the anger	an agent who instigates that event and may be described as the agent which causes the anger	a target of the anger who is normally the same as the agent	consequence event(s) of the anger having an impact on the targetMeaning 1 points to the anger event itself, while for Meaning 2 the focus is the event which is the stimulus for the anger.Subsequent research might show that it would be better to deal with two sub-frames, divine anger and human anger, which would relate to different domains in different ways. Alternatively, since, at least some of the time, the anger of a man of God is expected to match that of God, the sub-frames might be righteous and unrighteous anger.Perhaps because anger is such a common human experience, the anger frame is evoked in many contexts so that the frame is understood against a background of many domains. Thus there are no core domains, only non-core. For example:	Wickedness: God's anger towards the target/agent is a response to the sin of the agent	Idolatry: God's anger is provoked by idolatry	Injustice: God's anger towards the target/agent is a response to the sin of the agent	Unbelief: God's anger towards the target/agent is a response to the unbelief of Israel in the wilderness	Prophecy: a prophet provoked the anger of an ungodly king	Judgement: God's anger against the target was expected to have punishment as a consequence
\addinfo 1. With only one exception (Ezk 32:9), all the instances of the verb describe anger as the response to a stimulus. Although this instance is unique for the verb, the cognate noun (see |hכַּעַס|h*) does have a wider semantic range and refers to several responses other than anger (cf TDOT).

\keyverse |bI will disturb |b*the heart of many nations when I bring about your destruction among the nations in lands which you have not known. (KTBH)
\eval It is important to distinguish the response from anger. Most translations appropriately use a generic translation, eg 'I will trouble' (NIV, RSV, NRS, TEV) or 'I shall cause disquiet' (REB). CEV's 'nations will be shocked' may capture more precisely the nature of the response which God provokes, as indicated in v. 10. However, its use of a passive, perhaps for stylistic reasons, reduces the force of nineteen first person singular verbs in twelve verses in the Hebrew, which emphasise what Yahweh will do. NJV's use of ‘vex’ (its normal equivalent for |hכעס|h*) implies anger, which is out of place here. Similarly, 'grieve' (NJB) slightly misreads the response.
