\heb חרם
\level1 1
\pos v
\inc חֵרֶם[2]‏
\incpos ncm
\sub חָרִים, חֵרֶם‏, חֳרֵם, חָרְמָה, חֶרְמוֹן
\tnotes It will not be necessary to reflect every aspect of meaning in every verse. However, particularly when חרמ occurs for the first time in a passage, it would be wise to bring out implicit meanings of the word. Take for example Num 21:2: Then Israel made a vow to YHWH and said, “If you will indeed give this people into our hands, then we will utterly destroy their towns.”  This translation (NRSV) neglects one important implicit meaning of חרמ here, viz. that YHWH is the benefactive of this action. In other words it is not clear why destroying the towns would please YHWH. This is a vow: the Israelites offer to do something (make the towns herem) in return for YHWH’s help (the granting of victory). Put more crudely, this is a contract with God: if he does his bit, we will do ours. In order to bring out this implicit information we would need to say: Then Israel made a vow to YHWH and said, “If you will indeed give this people into our hands, then we will devote their towns to you by utterly destroying them.”  In the footnote for Horma in Num 21:3 we will need to explain this name as "place devoted to destruction". 
\hebraist The verb is used only in the causative stems Hiphil (48 times) and Hophal (3 times)
\dfnotes The Hebrew trilateral root חרמ is a complex idea which is alien to 21st century western thought, but which occurs relatively frequently in the Jewish scriptures. It is one of the most difficult Hebrew terms to translate adequately. As we examine the verses in which חרמ is used, we are able to identify the various facets of the term.                                                                                                                                                                                                               Philip Stern observes that חרמ has “long been considered as a singular ‘consecration-to-destruction of a designated enemy”, but “may equally well (or even better) be described as a ‘consecration-through-destruction’” and also “a destruction through consecration”. Niditch notes that its wide range of meanings includes “to become sacred, inviolable”, “to be accursed”, “to be consecrated for destruction” and a possible association with burning, though Num 18:14-15 specifically excludes human sacrifice. However, I would disagree with Niditch about including an element of curse in חרמ. In my view that is imposing our own cultural understanding on the Hebrew term. A good example of what I mean is the city of Jericho, which was deemed herem and duly ‘devoted to destruction’ (Jos 6:21). However, it is only after Jericho had been reduced to ashes that Joshua (6:26) pronounced a curse (Hebrew אָרוּר) – a concept which is clearly separate from חרמ. The term חרמ contains no more idea of ‘curse’ than עלה (=whole burnt offering). Both terms indicate what is, or should be, totally devoted to YHWH. It was perfectly possible for a place to be חרמ without becoming accursed.	The concept of חרמ is pretty gruesome and has caused serious ethical problems in the minds of many Christians. This leads some of them to try and water down or explain away the term. However, it is not the business of Bible translators to try and sugar the pill of unpalatable, but correct, exegesis. The need is to translate the term accurately and leave the theological implications to preachers.	It is certainly a fact that the concept of חרמ was not exclusively Israelite. As we see from the Mesha inscription, the Moabites also practised it. However, it should be noted that Israel only practised חרמ against selected inhabitants of the Promised Land. Deuteronomy (chap. 7 and 20) lists nations to undergo the herem. Yet Deu 7:2 shows חרמ was never intended as the sole mode of operation (i.e. genocide). חרמ can also be seen as the triumph of created order over chaos (enemies).                                                                   The first instance of חרמ is in Ex 22:19. In other verses which deal with the need to be exclusively loyal to YHWH  (Lev 20:2, Deu 13:5, Jdg 6:21), the penalty for disloyalty is clearly death. Banishment is not an option. There is little doubt that the Torah prescribes the death penalty for Israelites who offer sacrifices to any god other than YHWH. When applied to human beings חרמ entails death; when applied to things it entails destruction. In both cases death/ destruction is generally completed by means of fire. Thus after putting every living thing in Jericho to the sword, “they burned down the city and everything in it; only (the non-combustible) silver and gold, and the vessels of bronze and iron, they put into the treasury of the house of YHWH” (Jos 6:24). There is a very close connexion in thought with the holocaust or whole burnt offering (עלה). People who offer an animal as a whole burnt offering to YHWH are not allowed to eat any of the meat. The animal is totally destroyed. Similarly a town which is herem has to be totally destroyed. No one is allowed to benefit personally from its conquest by taking booty for their own use. Such action is deemed stealing as the goods belong to YHWH. Everything that breathes is put to the sword and then, together with all property taken from the enemy, burned to ashes.חרמ is not only total in its application, it is also irrevocable. There is no special ‘get out’ clause, no exception. Rahab and her household escaped the destruction of Jericho because of their faith in YHWH and identification with his people. This saved them from being destroyed along with the rest of the city because they had ceased to be subject to the law of חרמ. Lev 27:28-29 makes the irrevocable nature of חרמ crystal clear: “Nothing that a person owns that has been devoted to destruction (herem) for YHWH, be it human or animal, or inherited landholding, may be sold or redeemed; every devoted thing is most holy to YHWH. No human beings who have been devoted to destruction can be ransomed; they shall be put to death.” In other words there is no way something or someone that is חרמ can be “deheremised”. It is like becoming exposed to radioactive contamination. It is not a condition from which you can divest yourself.The recipient of people or things which are devoted to destruction is YHWH. They belong to him and are his property. Thus if anyone appropriates articles which are חרמ, they are stealing. As Jos 7:11 puts it, “Israel has sinned; they have transgressed my covenant that I imposed on them. They have taken some of the devoted things; they have stolen, they have acted deceitfully, and they have put them among their own belongings.” From this we see that taking something which is חרמ is not only stealing, it is also deceitful and treacherous; it breaks the covenant God has made with his people. He no longer feels obliged to protect them from harm. One individual’s transgression of the חרמ law thus has very serious consequences for the whole nation. His ‘radioactivity’ contaminates the people around him. This brings us to the next element of meaning.There is a grievous penalty for transgressing the law regarding חרמ. Articles or human beings that are חרמ inevitably bring punishment on those who disobey YHWH by taking them for their own profit: they themselves become חרמ. In other words they in turn forfeit their own lives and become liable for destruction. More than that, by taking something חרמ into their home they make every member of their household and indeed their whole nation herem. As Jos 6:18 warns, “As for you, keep away from the things devoted to destruction, so as not to covet and take any of the devoted things and make the camp of Israel an object for destruction, bringing trouble upon it.” Unfortunately, a man called Achan disobeyed this command and pilfered some of the loot he found in Jericho, causing the loss not only of his own life but that of his whole family and all their property, not to mention thousands of ordinary Israelites, before his sin was discovered.  King Saul forfeited his kingship when he disobeyed God’s command through the prophet Samuel to annihilate (חרמ) the Amalekites (1Sa 15:3,9,18-19,26). Many Christians are puzzled that God should be so harsh with Saul for what seems a relatively trivial sin, whereas King David literally gets away with murder (after also committing adultery). This is because they do not appreciate how irrevocable the ban (חרמ) is and how contagious it is. The 'herem'ness of the Amalekites and their property rubbed off onto Saul and permanently contaminated him rather like radioactive waste: once it gets into you, you cannot get rid of it and ultimately it will destroy you.In Hebrew thought holiness (קדשׁ) is also contagious (cf. Eze 44:19). The main difference  between קדשׁ and חרמ is that קדשׁ is usually a setting apart for life or use in the service of YHWH, whereas חרמ is a setting apart for death or destruction.                                                                                                                ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------                                                                                                        1 Philip D. Stern, The Biblical Herem, p1.2  Susan Niditch, War in the Hebrew Bible, p29.3  Stern, p24  Stern, p19,186. The fact that herem does not occur in Kings shows that the laws of Deu 20 are restricted to the aboriginal peoples of Canaan.5 Stern, p96.
\addinfo USES IN COGNATE LANGUAGES (Arabic and Syriac) - It is easy to see the transition in thought from the Hebrew חרמ to the Arabic حرم (haram). Both are very absolute terms. חרמ involves things which are ‘off limits’, things which are totally forbidden to human beings. Something or someone which is חרמ is outside the saving protection of YHWH. The sixth commandment is “Thou shalt not kill”, but people who, for example, trespass in the temple put themselves outside the protection of YHWH and can be put to death without transgressing the sixth commandment because it is forbidden for anyone except a priest to enter those places. The idea of forbiddenness is very strong also in the Arabic haram. Thus for a Muslim the eating of pork is haram. Eating a very small piece of pork or highly processed and refined pork or just an occasional bit of pork will not absolve you from committing what in Islam is considered a very serious sin. It is absolutely forbidden to eat pork in any shape or form and there are no extenuating circumstances. Not even ignorance makes it excusable. It is incumbent on a Muslim to know what he is eating. This is why committed Muslims, like Jews, will go to great lengths to avoid contaminating themselves and thus incurring the wrath of God. Westerners are bemused by Turkish biscuit packets which assure potential consumers that the biscuits contain no pork fat in any form. It seems like a lot of fuss about a non-issue. Westerners would be more concerned about E numbers! One can only understand the Muslim perspective if one bears in mind just how strong and absolute the concept of haram is.	One other interesting development in the use of the h-r-m root is in Syriac, where the causative form is used in ecclesiastical parlance to mean excommunicate. The offender is handed ‘over to satan for the destruction of the flesh, so that his spirit may be saved in the day of the Lord’ (1Co 5:5). The person is considered dead to the community. Obviously, this is how the Syriac church applied the concept of חרמ to their own time and community, but no doubt many modern Christians would practise something similar in their church discipline.
\level2 1
\meaning Devote to YHWH by destruction
\frame Utter destruction
\domain Items to be destroyed, people to be killed
\stem חרמ
\eventfr Agent = people, Target = things (often places) or people, Benefactive (often unexpressed) = YHWH, instrument (often, but not always) = the weapons of war followed by burning with fire
\level3 a
\context Execution as a penalty for disobeying the Torah
\eventfrc Agent = Faithful Israelites, Target = Israelite who has disobeyed the Torah and committed a capital offence
\syntagcc חרמ  as a punishment is irrevocable. It cannot be commuted. The offender cannot save or redeem his life by undergoing a lesser punishment.
\keyref "!6!3#!
\keyverse Whoever sacrifices to any God, other than the LORD alone, shall be devoted to destruction
\eval NRSV "shall be devoted to destruction" well captures the finality of חרמ, and hints that the benefactive is YHWH, but fails to convey the context, viz. it is a command to faithful Israelites to execute any idol-worshippers among them. It sounds as if it is simply the natural fate of an idol-worshipper. The expression "devoted to" could also be miscontrued by an ignorant reader to mean that an idol-worshipper is a fervent supporter of destruction, he is devoted to destroying things. NIV "must be destroyed" conveys the idea of a command, but there is little or no idea that the benefactive is YHWH. It tries to solve this problem by adding a long footnote: "The Hebrew term refers to the irrevocable giving over of things or persons to the LORD, often by totally destroying them". This may well be the best solution. TEV "Condemn to death" is a clear command and the fate of the offender is expressed unequivocally. However, it is very secular in tone and conveys no idea that the benefactive is YHWH. The same remarks apply to CEV: "Death is the punishment".
\trans The New Turkish Bible has "ölüm cezasına çarptırılacaktır" (=will be made to incur the death penalty) - very similar to the TEV, CEV. This rendering is not completely satisfactory (a footnote like that in the NIV would have been good), but it is better than the Azerbaijani "tamamilə məhv olunsun" (=let [him] be completely destroyed), which sounds like a curse rather than a judicial death sentence.
\ref "!6!3#! %!-!0(! '!5!+)!
\paradigc 
\colc |eThere are aspects of the root קדשׁ  (=holy) which are parallel to חרמ.|e*
\level3 b
\context To devote an enemy city or land to YHWH by killing all the inhabitants and their animals, burning everything combustible and putting into YHWH's sanctuary all non-combustible items (e.g. artefacts made of gold, silver and other metals)
\framec Total irrevocable loss (destruction)
\domainc 1. Enemies and their animals are killed - none are spared. 2. Combustible items captured from the enemy are burned to ashes. 3. Non-combustible items like gold and silver are put in the sanctuary. 4. Land comes under the control of the sanctuary and is administered by the priests.
\eventfrc Agent = victors in war, Target = vanquished enemy and his animals and property, Benefactive (often unexpressed) = YHWH
\keyref &!&!2$"
\keyverse (Including v17) The city and all that is in it shall be devoted to YHWH for destruction. Only Rahab the prostitute and all who are with her in her house shall live because she hid the messengers we sent. 18 As for you, keep away from the things devoted to destruction, so as not to covet [fr 6.18 Gk: Heb "devote to destruction". Compare 7.21] and take any of the devoted things and make the camp of Israel an object for destruction, bringing trouble upon it.
\eval The NRSV "shall be devoted to the LORD for destruction" is supposed to be understood as "shall be offered (like a whole burnt offering) to the LORD by being destroyed". However, there are two big problems: 1) There is an ambiguity in the English. 'Devote' generally has a positive meaning and "shall be devoted" could be understood by an ignorant reader as "the city shall become fervent devotees of the LORD for the purpose of destroying things"; 2) "shall be" could be understood as the natural fate of the city as opposed to a specific command to destroy it. The NIV "are to be devoted to the LORD" is even worse since the element of destruction is totally absent and the danger of misunderstanding "are to be devoted" is even more acute. The TEV "must be totally destroyed as an offering to the LORD" is an excellent translation model which successfully addresses both of these problems. The CEV "you must destroy it and everything in it, to show that it belongs to the LORD makes the agent clearer than the TEV, but the second half of the sentence is less natural. I would recommend translating this verse as follows: "You must totally destroy the city and everything that is in it as an offering to YHWH".
\trans Turkish "RAB'be koşulsuz adanmıştır" (=has been devoted unconditionally to the LORD) is unsatisfactory since it is not clear that the city is to suffer total destruction. Thus the Turkish translation could be greatly improved by adding the phrase "for total destruction". The element koşulsuz (=unconditional), whilst eminently defensible, is not so important and could be omitted. The Azerbaijani "məhv edilməyə həsr olunmuşdur və onda olan hər şey Rəbbə təqdim olunmalıdır" (=has been devoted to-being-destroyed and everything in it must be offered to the Lord) is much better. Though it is a bit long-winded, every element of meaning in חרמ has been accounted for.
\ref $!5!"+" $!5!#'" %!"!B%" %!#!&!" %!'!"'! %!-!0(! %!4!1!" &!"!*6! &!&!2$" &!&!5!" &!(!:)! &!*!!)" &!*!<)! &!*!C+! &!*!E," &!*!G&" &!*!H/! &!+!+%! &!+!,(! &!+!4+! &!+!52! '!!!1)" )!/!#%" )!/!(&! )!/!)-! )!/!/0! )!/!2&" )!/!41! -!$!I." 7!+!/!" 7!B!"'! A!$!--"
\paradigc 
\level2 2
\meaning To relinquish ownership of persons and things and permanently devote them to the service of YHWH
\frame Irrevocable loss (often, but not always, followed by destruction) due to being offered to YHWH
\domain Items or persons offered to YHWH
\stem חרמ
\eventfr Agent = Israelite worshipper, Content = item or person, Benefactive = YHWH
\syntagc In this meaning חרמ somewhat overlaps in meaning with קדשׁ (=consecrate, make holy). However, whereas קדשׁ focuses on the object or person's end condition of being consecrated to the service of YHWH, חרמ concentrates on the permanent irrevocable loss of that object or person to the giver.
\level3 a
\context Irrevocable loss of ownership
\framec Permanently dedicating things or persons to YHWH
\domainc Items for destruction or permanent dedication to the sanctuary, persons to be killed
\eventfrc Agent = Israelite worshipper, Content = item or person, Benefactive = YHWH
\keyref #!;!<#! #!;!=#!
\keyverse 28 Nothing that a person owns that has been devoted to destruction for YHWH, be it human or animal, or inherited landholding, may be sold or redeemed; every devoted thing is most holy to YHWH. 29 No human beings who have been devoted to destruction can be ransomed; they shall be put to death.
\eval One point which comes out of this verse is that, despite the NRSV rendering "has been devoted to destruction", חרמ does not always involve destruction. The basic idea is irrevocable loss. In the case of material objects, combustible items are destroyed by fire, but articles of gold, silver, bronze etc are put in the sanctuary. In the case of people, they are put to death. In the case of real estate ('inherited landholding'), ownership passes permanently and irrevocably  to the sanctuary and the property is administered by the priests. The TEV avoids this difficulty by rendering "what he has unconditionally dedicated", but then spoils it by adding a footnote saying that such things had to be destroyed, forgetting that metal items and real estate were not in fact destroyed, but simply remained the permanent property of the sanctuary.
\trans The Turkish "RAB'be koşulsuz adanan" (=which has been unconditionally devoted to the LORD) works well here - better than the NRSV. Introducing the element of destruction is unhelpful here because one of the targets ('inherited landholding'), unlike humans and animals, cannot be destroyed.
\ref #!;!<#! #!;!=#! &!&!2$"
\paradigc 
\level2 3
\meaning (Simply) to destroy utterly, annihilate (with no reference to YHWH)
\frame Utter destruction
\domain Killing the whole population in a locality and destroying their property (with no idea of the benefactive being YHWH)
\stem חרמ
\eventfr Agent = non-Israelites, Target = other non-Israelites
\level3 a
\context Non-Israelites destroying others (with no idea of the benefactive being YHWH)
\framec Utter destruction
\domainc Genocide - complete destruction of one nation by another
\eventfrc Agent = |iAmmonites and Moab, Target = the inhabitants of Mount Seir|i*
\syntagcc Even in relatively early Hebrew (e.g. Isa 11:15) some of the original connotations of חרמ were being lost and the word sometimes meant no more than simply |ito destroy|i*. In later Hebrew, such as 2Ch 20:23, 32:14 this tendency becomes even more pronounced.
\keyref .!4!7'"
\keyverse For the Ammonites and Moab attacked the inhabitants of Mount Seir, destroying them utterly (חרמ ); and when they had made an end of the inhabitants of Seir, they all helped to destroy (משׁח) one another.
\eval Here there is no problem for the NRSV "destroying them utterly" because there is no idea of devoting them as an offering to YHWH. The NIV, TEV and CEV are similarly OK here.
\ref .!4!7'" .!@!.&! 7!E!+)" 8!R!5(" 8!R!:'" 8!S!#*! ;!+!L)#
\paradigc 
\level3 b
\context YHWH using non-Israelites to destroy Judah as a punishment
\syntagcc Here obviously חרמ has an inbuilt element of exaggeration. King Nebuchadrezzar did not inflict genocide on Judah. He permanently destroyed the state of Judah and took the population captive to Babylon. God could not allow his people the Jews to be totally wiped out without reneging on his covenant.
\keyref 8!9!)2"
\keyverse I am going to send for all the tribes of the north, says YHWH, even for King Nebuchadrezzar of Babylon, my servant, and I will bring them against this land and its inhabitants, and against all these nations around; I will utterly destroy them, and make them an object of horror and of hissing, and an everlasting disgrace. [+ fr 25.9  Gk Compare Syr: Heb "and everlasting desolations"]. 
\eval Where YHWH is the agent, there are no problems with translating חרמ as simply "utterly destroy". However, in Jer 25:9 there is a slight problem with this rendering in that the people of Judah were not and never have been "utterly destroyed". It was the existence of an independent state of Judah which was permanently terminated. It might therefore be better to suppress the word "utterly" here and say simply "destroy".
\ref 8!9!)2"
\paradigc 
\level2 4
\meaning To be confiscated, suffer forfeiture
\frame Total irretrievable loss
\domain Confiscation of property
\stem חרמ
\eventfr Agent: authorities, Target: people who do not comply with the authorities
\syntagc As Stern notes, there is a distinct paucity of material on herem in the Kethuvim and by Ezra’s time (6th century B.C.) we also notice a distinct watering-down of the term. In Ezr 10:8 it is used of the forfeiture of goods as punishment for failure to attend a key meeting in Jerusalem. In Dan 11:44 it is used as a synonym for ruin with the element of being devoted to YHWH no longer in focus. Obviously by this time the Jews were no longer capable of making their enemies חרמ. They were in exile and subject from time to time to persecution by tyrannous rulers. Not surprisingly the laws of herem became a dead letter. In Malachi 4:6 the term is rendered curse in the NRSV. It would be better to translate as the REB and say: “lest I come and put the land under a ban to destroy it”. Malachi is unusual in his use of herem in that the subject of the verb is YHWH. It is normally human beings who devote things to YHWH for destruction. Here herem seems to have lost the element of being devoted and simply means doom to destruction.	Besides occurring in proper names like Hermon, Hormah, Horem and Harim (with the general idea of places and people being devoted to YHWH), the root חרמ  also occurs in the word חֵרֶם meaning a net (Ecc 7:26; Ezk 26:5, 26:14, 32:3, 47:10; Mic 7:2; Hab 1:15,16,17). This is the same word which elsewhere means thing devoted to destruction. Unless it is a coincidence, which seems unlikely, the extension of meaning to net seems a piece of rather grim, black humour. The net is the place where a fish becomes herem (devoted to destruction), ending its life there and later getting cooked on the barbecue! The irrevocability of the ban could indeed be likened to a trap or net and the fish’s appointment with the fire of a barbecue resembles the fate of people and property that are herem.
\level3 a
\context Permanent irretrievable loss of property.
\domainc |bPermanent irretrievable loss of property as a punishment for non-attendance of an important meeting|b*
\eventfrc Target = property of non-attenders, Agent (unexpressed) = leaders of the people
\syntagcc Here we see a further watering-down of the meaning of חרמ. Here it means no more than the confiscation of property as a legal penalty.

\keyverse (Including v7) They made a proclamation throughout Judah and Jerusalem to all the returned exiles that they should assemble at Jerusalem, 8 and that if any did not come within three days, by order of the officials and the elders all their property should be forfeited, and they themselves banned from the congregation of the exiles.

\heb ענשׁ
\strong 6064
\pos v
\gloss to fine, to impose levy, to punish
\freq 9
\sub עֹנֶשׁ
\intro This verb expresses the action that someone with authority or power imposes payment in either monetary or other forms to another.  1. In legal context, it is to make someone pay fine or penalty compensating the loss or damage caused by wrong doings of the person.  Similar legal conducts can become oppressive when it is done unjustly by the authority.2.  A sovereign country demands payment to the vassal.  3. When it is used in the context of Moral teaching, the meaning is simply to punish rather than to impose payment.  The metaphorical point may be having to pay the cost of own action or behaviour.  In this use, often Niphal form is used and AGENT (subject) "pays cost" in figurative sense.  Implicit AGENT may be God.
\dfnotes The prominent domains can be shifted according to the point of views.From the viewpoint of Agent 1: Authority, Power, Punishment, Legal, JusticeFrom the viewpoint of Agent 2: Oppression, Suffer, Wisdom, FollyFrom the viewpoint of Theme: Payment, Oppression
\level2 1
\meaning To impose payment to someone as a consequence of wrong doings
\frame Punishment
\domain Authority, Power, Wrong doing, Payment
\stem Qal, Niphal
\eventfr Agent 1: humanAgent 2: humanTheme: money or goods
\level3 a
\context To impose payment to someone as a consequence of wrong doings
\framec Punishment
\domainc Legal, Payment
\eventfrc Agent 1: humanAgent 2: humanTheme: money or goods
\keyref %!6!3!"
\keyverse They |bshall fine |b*him one hundred shekels of silver (which they shall give to the young woman's father) because he has slandered a virgin of Israel. She shall remain his wife; he shall not be permitted to divorce her as long as he lives. (NRS)
\eval Most translations translate 'to fine,' which is fine.  CEV explains the meaning and renders 'have to pay'.
\ref "!5!6+! %!6!3!" 4!1!:"! >!"!('!|na|n*

\frame Sovereign, Vassal
\domain Authority, Power, Payment

\context To impose payment to another country
\framec Sovereign, Vassal

\eventfrc Agent 1: human, countryAgent 2: people group, country, landTheme: money or goods

\level2 3
\meaning To punish someone
\frame punishment
\domain Authority, Power, Wrong doing
\level3 a
\context To punish someone
\domainc Wisdom, Folly, Wrong doing
\keyref 4!6!#("
\keyverse Sensible people will see trouble coming and avoid it, but an unthinking person will walk right into it and |bsuffer|b* for it. (KTBH)
\eval This verse is expressed passive form 'to be punished'. NJPS, RSV NRS translate as 'pay penalty' and NIV, RSV, NRS explain the meaning as 'suffer for it' and either way is fine.  CEV is an effort to explain the communicative meaning: 'When you see trouble coming, don't be stupid and walk right into it— be clever and hide'.  However the meaning seems to be shifted from 'be wise because ...' to 'do this when ...'.  GNT seems to use 'regret it later' expressing incurred meaning of 'be punished,' but since the meaning of 'to be punished' is not clearly expressed, it is not satisfactory.
\trans Translation approach may be depends on the literary tradition of the target language.  The Hebrew style used in the Proverbs allows logical gaps in the sentence, for some meanings are incurred.  Explaining the communicative meaning may be preferable if the target language does not allow such logical gap.  If the language allows to have incurred meaning and compact style in teaching, it may be used quite effectively.

\not (a) This is a difficult verse.  The context of Amos 2:8 clearly shows strong dominance of Oppression and Injustice domains.  Another option is to arrange MEANING 1 into two CONTEXUAT USEs.  The second CONTEXTUAL USE would be 'To impose payment in an unjust manner'.  The Contextual Frame of this would be Payment, Oppression, and Injustice.However, in the text, the things which were acquired by this legal action were consumed by the authority, and it was probably not for the intended use.  The action of 'consuming' was not right.  However, it is not clear whether the legal act 'imposing payment' was done right or not. Therefore, my preference is not to separate Amos 2:8 from the rest of Meaning 1.

\heb עֹנֶשׁ
\strong 6066
\pos ncm
\gloss fine, levy, tribute, penalty, punishment
\freq 2
\sub ענשׁ
\intro This noun occurs two times in the Old Testament.  It is used in both concrete and metaphorical meaning, (1) referring to a kind of payment imposed by authority, and (2) referring to a negative consequence, and the person who experiences this consequence is responsible of the prior cause of this consequence.
\dfnotes The intrinsic domains of this noun include Payment, Authority, Power, and Legal.  It also triggers either Justice or Oppression domain, according to the proportion of the payment.  When the payment is imposed to someone in legal setting in appropriate proportion, it is likely that justice was done.  On the other hand, large amount of payment imposed by authority can result in Oppression as such was the case in sovereign-vassal relationship.  Thus, in MEANING 1, domain Oppression is present.
\level2 1
\meaning A kind of payment imposed by superior power or authority
\frame Sovereign, Vassal
\domain Payment, Authority, Power, Oppression
\level3 a
\context A kind of payment imposed by superior power or authority
\framec Sovereign, Vassal
\domainc Payment, Power, Authority, Oppression
\keyref ,!7!A*#
\keyverse Pharaoh Neco confined him at Riblah in the land of Hamath, so that he might not reign in Jerusalem, and imposed |btribute |b*on the land of one hundred talents of silver and a talent of gold. (NRS)
\eval Most translations do not include the sense of Punishment and simply refers to 'tribute' or 'levy' either using noun form or verb form, which is a good model.  CEV explains this meaning 'to force to pay as taxes'.  However REB and NJB translate as 'impose indemnity' which include the sense of compensation of damage or loss.  However, 2Kings 23:35 gives more detail description of the payment: 'Jehoiakim gave Pharaoh the silver and the gold, and he made an assessment on the land to pay the money demanded by Pharaoh. He exacted from the people of the land the silver and gold to be paid Pharaoh Neco, according to each man’s assessment.' (NJPS).  This additional information supports that this payment was a kind of normal taxation in the Ancient Near Eastern practice.  Therefore, our preference goes to the majority interpretation.
\ref ,!7!A*#

\level2 2
\meaning Punishment
\frame Wisdom, Folly, Teaching
\domain Punishment, Authority, Power, Wrong doing
\level3 a
\context Punishment
\framec Wisdom, Folly, Teaching
\domainc Punishment, Authority, Power, Wrong doing
\keyref 4!3!3$!
\keyverse If someone has a hot temper, let him |btake the consequences|b*. If you get him out of trouble once, you will have to do it again. (GNT)
\eval English versions are divided into two interpretations whether it involves payment or not: 'penalty' (NJB, NIV, RSV, NRS) and 'punishment' (NJPS, ) or 'bear/take the consequences' (REB, GNT), 'in trouble' (CEV).  Here 'punishment' is preferred to 'penalty', because the immediate context of Pro 19 indicate that the prominent domain is 'wisdom/folly, unless 'penalty' is used in figurative sense.  More explicitly, since 'punishment' can be paraphrased as 'bear/take the consequences' (REB, GNT), GNT is a good model for translating this verse.

\heb בּוֹר
\strong 953
\level1 1
\pos ncms
\gloss cistern
\freq 64
\sub בֹר
\intro 1. The primary meaning of |hבּוֹר|h* is a cistern, a hole dug in the ground to hold water. The hole or pit was sometimes used as place to imprison people. |hבּוֹר|h* also means prison. However, the prison is not clearly underground so it is not necessarily a dungeon. 2. A figurative meaning is the underworld which is underground, the place where the dead continue exististence, like |hשְׁאוֹל|h*. It is not a grave where dead bodies are buried. The darkness, depth, and unpleasantness of an old, unused cistern could have evoked the place where dead people were. |hבּוֹר|h* is used primarily in a phrase 'those going down to the underworld' as a metonymy for death. All the occurrances are a metaphorical way of referring to death.
\tnotes When it means the underworld, this word is used almost exclusively in the phrase |hיֹורְדֵי בֹור|h* 'those going down to the underworld'. The whole phrase is a metonymy for dying. In the other occurrances, |hבּוֹר|h* itself is the metonymy for death.There is no sense of punishment in this underworld, so words for underworld which involve punishment are not appropriate translations, such as hell. Words for grave could be used when they are a metonymy for death, as they are in English.
\hebraist |hבּוֹר|h* is used primarily in the phrase |hיֹורְדֵי בֹור|h*. This phrase is introduced by the object marker |hאֶת|h* or by the prepositions |hעִם|h*, |hמִן|h*, or |hכּ|h*. |hבּוֹר|h* is always the goal of the verb. When meaning the underworld, the term is used soley in the poetic genre.
\altint Many translations gloss |hבּוֹר|h* with either ‘death’ or ‘grave’. BDB, Raymond, Even-Shoshan give the gloss of 'grave'. IVPBBC gives 'underworld' and 'grave'. Note that all of the occurrances of |hבּוֹר|h* as the underworld are in poetic genre and are a metonymy for death. In English 'grave' is also a metonymy for death.
\evalev The term |hבּוֹר|h* is used almost exclusively with the verb |hירד|h*. This verb is also used with other words for the underworld, but never with the words for grave,  |hקְבוּרָה|h* and |hקֶבֶר|h*, and only once with |h‫מָוֶת‬|h*. The meaning of |hבּוֹר|h* is thus most likely 'underworld' rather than 'grave'. Words for grave, underworld, and death are used in similar contexts since they all have to do with death. A person goes down to the underworld at death, and the body is buried at that time. |hבּוֹר|h*  and |h‫מָוֶת‬|h* are used in parallel only three times, and one of these is also with |hשְׁאוֹל|h*. Thus their juxtaposition does not necessarily imply that |hבּוֹר|h* means grave. A menonymous use of |hבּוֹר|h* is a more likely explanation for the evocation of death.
\comp 1. The literal meaning   Frame: Water: Water storage2. A metaphorical extention of Meaning 1  Frame: Imprisonment3. A metaphorical extention of Meaning 1  Frame: Death
\dfnotes The primary meaning of |hבּוֹר|h* is a cistern, a hole dug in the ground or rock to hold water. After the cistern was no longer used or usable for water storage, it was sometimes used as a place of imprisonment. By figurative extension, the same word was used for prison or dungeon. Another figurative use was as a name for the underworld, or place where dead people were. The underworld was seen as underground, and dark, like the inside of a cistern. In this meaning |hבּוֹר|h* does not seem to be a place of punishment. Death is not a positive thing, and dying is the punishment. The idea of imprisonment is associated with two other words for the underworld, |hשְׁאוֹל|h* and |hשַׁחַת|h*.The psalmists speak of |hבּוֹר|h* in relation to life and rescue from death and from enemies. The prophets Isaiah and Ezekiel both speak of |hבּוֹר|h* in the context of punishment. Part of the punishment of God on the surrounding nations is to send their leaders to |hבּוֹר|h*, often used in parallel with |hשְׁאוֹל|h*. Death itself is the punishment because it is a humiliation of those who were leaders and in high position.The most important characteristics of the underworld for the Hebrews were that it was a dark and distanct place underground where the dead people continued their existence. Hebrew uses five terms to refer to it: |hשְׁאוֹל|h*, |hאֲבַדּוֹן|h*, |hשַׁחַת|h*, |hבְּאֵר|h* and |hבּוֹר|h*, and two phrases: |h‫אֶרֶץ נְשִׁיָּֽה |h* (Psa 88:13) and |h‫אֶרֶץ תַּחְתִּיֹּ֖ות‬|h* (Ezk 32:18). It had some characteristics of a prison, like bars and gates, but it was not seen as a place of punishment.
\level2 1
\meaning A cistern for holding water
\frame Water
\domain Water storage
\level3 a
\context A hole (dug) in the ground used to store water
\keyref #!+!D#!
\keyverse - - but a spring or a |bcistern of water |b*will both be ceremonially clean, although anything else which touches their carcasses will be ceremonially unclean. (KTBH)
\eval Evlauation: All transaltions referred to convey the correct meaning.
\ref "!5!A#! "!5!A'! "!5!B"" #!+!D#" %!&!+&" )!-!&/$ )!3!6%" *!7!/'" *!7!0'" ,!*!.'" ,!2!?2" -!+!1(" -!+!2&" -!+!61" .!:!*%! 0!)!9)! 3!'!0!! 3!x!'"! 4!%!/"" 4!<!1$"|na|n* 5!,!&/# 7!.!3+#|nb|n* 7!D!03" 7!S!!+! 8!"!-,! 8!"!--! 8!&!'"! 8!I!')" 8!I!)!#

\level3 b
\context An empty cistern used as a place of imprisonment
\domainc Imprisonment
\keyref !!E!8$!
\keyverse and they threw him into an |bempty cistern |b*that had no water in it. (KTBH)
\eval The NIV and REB make it clear that Joseph was imprisoned in an empty water storage cistern. Several other transaltions use the term 'well', which is not as good but acceptable since a well can be dug for the purpose of holding water. The RSV, NRSV, and NJPS use the generic word 'pit'. It is not clear that the pit was normally used to store water.  A word referring to a cistern is the best translation.
\trans Possible translations: a hole dug in the ground to hold water
\ref !!E!4&" !!E!6'# !!E!8$" !!E!8%# !!E!<(# !!E!=## !!E!=&# *!7!43" 3!H!#"" 8!F!&%# 8!F!&.$ 8!F!'+# 8!F!)-# 8!F!*/# 8!F!+2# 8!F!-&# 9!#!U"# F!)!+%"
\paradigc 
\level2 2
\meaning A place of imprisonment (Metaphorical extention of Meaning 1)
\frame Imprisonment
\syntagc Most English translations and Biblical dictionaries understand the word |hבֹּור|h* to be a place underground, both for the literal and concrete meaning of cistern, and for the extended meaning of prison. When it refers to a place of imprisonment, none of the references give a clear indications that the prison is underground.
\level3 a
\context A place where people are imprisoned
\keyref !!H!/*!
\keyverse ...and even here in Egypt I didn't do anything to deserve being put in |bprison|b*. (TEV)
\eval Most English translations use 'dungeon' which implies an underground prison, but a term for prison that does not imply an underground location is certainly an acceptable translation, as in the TEV and the CEV.
\ref !!H!/*# !!I!.&# "!,!=2" 7!8!6$" 8!E!0%"
\paradigc 
\level2 3
\meaning The underworld as a metonymy for death (Metaphorical extention of Meaning 1)
\frame Death
\domain Judgement; Rescue
\eventfr 
\level3 a
\context The underworld as a metonymy for death
\syntagcc All references for |hבּוֹר|h* as the underworld are in the poetic genre. Although the word refers to the underworld as a place, it is used in the context of death or possible death of the speaker or the person referred to. Often there is a comparison of the troubles of the speaker with one who is dead or 'has gone down to the underworld'. In all but four occurrances, |hבּוֹר|h*  is used within a structured phrase: |hיֹורְדֵי בֹֽור|h* 'those who go down to the underworld'. A phrase which is a metonymy for death in the target language is a possible translations of this phrase. The word 'death' can also be used.
\keyref 3"U!'+!
\keyverse ...Don't turn away and leave me here to die. (CEV)
\eval Most translations consulted use the term 'the pit', which does not convey the emotion of the psalmist. CEV and NJBE convey the emotion well by using the phrases 'leave me here to die' and 'sink into oblivion'. TEV refers directly to the world of the dead, which also evokes the negative emotions associated with death and the underworld, but to a lesser degree.

\not (a) Possible meaning. Some translations take |hבּוֹר|h* to mean 'pit' or 'hole' in Prov 28:17, not the underworld. The TOB translates it as 'prison'. Both are possible meanings. KTBH prefers the meaning underworld as a metonymy for death, as in the NRSV, TEV, and FLC among others. The preposition |hעַד |h*implies a a physical distance, which fit into the idea of the underworld being far away, as well as a temporal distance, that is until death. This metonymous use of underworld fits with the other occurances of |hבּוֹר|h* as underworld that are metonomies for death.

\notxx (a) NOTE
\not (b) In Isa 14:19 |hבּוֹר |h* is taken by some translations and lexicons to be the underworld. However, it is also translated as a cistern wthere dead bodies are thrown instead of being buried. |hבּוֹר |h* is repeated four times in this section. This occurrance of |hבּוֹר |h* comes in the midst of Isaiah's prophecy about the defeat and punishment of the surrounding nations. The leaders will all end up in the underworld.This verse appears to use a play on words of the several meanings of |hבּוֹר |h*. Isaiah is making a unique point about the king of Babylonia. He is no better than any of the other kings. His fate is even worse than that of the surrounding nations. Not only will he die and meet the other kings in the underworld, he will not be buried appropriately in a grave like other dead leaders.  His body will simply be thorwn into a pit with other corpses. This was a very shameful thing to have happen to him. The more lavish a funeral and burial rites, the more important a leader was considered to be. His burial reflected how much honor he had among the poeple.

\notsp (b) NOTE
\notpt (b) NOTE
\notxx (b) NOTE

\not (d) Most translations take |hבּוֹר|h* to be the pit or hole the writer was thrown into. This is a possible translation, since just a few verses before he describes being thrown into a pit with water in it. The phrase is 'mi bor taxtiot' [|hמִבֹּור מִבֹּור|h*], a similar phrase to other underworld words, particularly |hשַׁחַת|h* where deliverance or rescue from the underworld is a common theme. The writer could be playaing on the word for pit by refering to it first as the literal hole in the ground, and the second time as a metonymy for death.
\notfr (d) NOTE
\notsp (d) NOTE
\notpt (d) NOTE
\notxx (d) NOTE
\not (e) In both chapters 28 and 32, Ezekial gives prophecies against the nations that conquered Israel, depicting their downfall and humiliation. The image is one where the graves of the dead kings and their warriors are laid out in the underworld, just like they would be in a regular burial place on earth. God is punishing them by taking away their power and authority on earth, and sending them down to the underworld just like everyone else. Even worse, he is sening them to the most remote part of the underworld. One could infer from this usage that |hבּוֹר|h* means 'death', but |hבּוֹר|h* not 'grave', in this passage. The most likely translation is 'underworld', where the dead are. Ezekiel is describing a scenario typical of the earth to make his point more powerfully.

\not (a) The term |hנֵצֶר|h* 'branch' here in Isa 14:19 probably refers to an unwanted or rejected branch of the family (see DOTTE). The song about the king of Babylon rejoices in his dishonor. \heb שְׁאוֹל
\strong 7585
\pos ncb
\gloss underworld
\freq 65
\sub שׁאה
\intro 1. |hשְׁאוֹל |h*is the name of the place where the dead are. It is part of the universe, under the earth, inaccessible to living humans, yet God can see into |hשְׁאוֹל |h*and knows all about it.2. Death is a negative thing because it separates one from society, family, and from God. So |hשְׁאוֹל |h*is also a negative place since one who is there is separated from society and from God. It is not a place of judgement, nor of punishment.3. The phrase 'go down to the underworld' [|hירד שְׁאוֹל‏|h*] is a metonymy for death, or possibly a euphemism for death. As a metonymy, it is used primarily in the speech of individuals in narrative portions, and in poetry. It is often used in parallel with other words for the underworld, such as |hשַׁחַת|h* and |hבּוּר|h*, and with the word |hמָוֶת|h*.4. In Proverbs, |hשְׁאוֹל |h*is the underworld, and is used mainly as a metaphor for the consquences of a wasteful or evil life or the living of an evil life which leads to death. 5. The use of the term |hשְׁאוֹל |h*in poetic and prophetic sections is varied. Sometimes it is personified, sometimes it refers specifically to the place where the dead are, sometimes it refers to death.
\tnotes 1. |hשְׁאוֹל |h*was a literal place within the Hebrew cosmos. It is underground, the place where the dead are located after death. The Hebrews believed that the dead continued to exist in this underworld, but had no further contact with the surface of the earth or those still living. There was no intermediate place where the dead spent time. |hשְׁאוֹל |h*was the only location of those who were dead.2. |hשְׁאוֹל |h*was not a place of judgement or punishment for the Hebrews. Any word in the target language which evokes these ideas, e.g. 'hell' or |ienfer|i* should be avoided. Some of the prophets and psalmists viewed death itself as part of God's punishment, as in Psalm 9:18, Isaiah 14 and Ezekiel 31. The punishment is in the loss of honor and importance in the world rather than anything that takes place in the underworld after death.3. The place of the dead and death itself are very closely tied together in the Hebrew text. The implicit domain for |hשְׁאוֹל |h*is Death. |hשְׁאוֹל |h*is often used with the word |hמַוֶת|h*, and with other metonymous phrases for death, such as רִמָּה. In the poetic and prophetic passages, the use of the terms for death and the place of the dead are found in parallel and often used as synonyms.  4. The Hebrews used the expression   |hירד שְׁאוֹל |h*to refer to the death of a person. It is possibly a euphemism. The names of the underworld itself, including |hשְׁאוֹל|h*, were used as metonymies for death. Some translations, e.g. CEV, NLT, and NIV, always take |hשְׁאוֹל |h*as a figurative expression for death or the grave. Only where |hשְׁאוֹל |h*is a metonymy for death is this a good alternative. Metonymies in the target languages could also be used. Where |hשְׁאוֹל |h*is referring to the literal place where the dead are within the cosmos, a term for the underworld in the target language is a preferable, if the term is a place underground. If not, a phrase like 'world of the dead' is appropriate. 5. Ezekiel and Isaiah describe the underworld in several prophecies. The kings of the nations which surround Israel are described in the underworld with their honour and importance gone. Because these are prophetic passages, they should not be taken as literal descriptions of what |hשְׁאוֹל |h*is like. The prophecies are descriptions of God's punishment of these evil nations, using the images of the underworld to emphasize how they will loose all their glory and importance when they die.
\hebraist The verb |hירד|h* is used with |hשְׁאוֹל |h*as goal or location to describe the death event. Thus to be saved from death, or to live, uses the verb |hצלה|h*, to go up. Synonyms for |hשְׁאוֹל|h*, such as |h‫שָׁחַת|h* and |h‫בּוּר‬ |h*both also use these verbs. The words |h‫קבֶרֶ‬|h*, |h‫קְבוּרָה‬ |h*or  |h‫מָוֶת‬|h* never use these verbs.
\altint Some commentators equate |hשְׁאוֹל|h* with the grave, as do the NIV. NIDOTTE gives the meaning as both the grave and the underworld because |hשְׁאוֹל|h* collocates with |hאֲבַדֹּון|h* and is used in parallel with |hבּוּר|h* and |hמָוֶת|h*. Although in English 'the grave' can be a euphemism for death, this is not the case in Hebrew. The grave is associated with death because the dead body is put in the grave. But the part of the person that continues existing goes to |hשְׁאוֹל|h*, the underworld. The verb |hירד|h* is used with words for the underworld, but not with words for the grave. Neither are terms for grave used as synonyms for the underworld. Some resemblance between the structure of graves and of |hשְׁאוֹל|h* in implied in various passages. Amos 9:2 implies that one can dig into |hשְׁאוֹל|h*, but the focus here is that the underworld is part of the universe which God knows all about. Words for grave are never used to name parts of the universe.
\comp 1. The place Frame: Universe2. Metonymous use of Meaning 1 Frame: Death
\dfnotes 1. In Meaning 1, the focus is on the location of the underworld in relation to other places in the universe. It is part of the cosmos, evoking the universe domain. All occurrances are in poetry or prophecy.2. In Meaning 2, the word for the underworld refers to the event of death. One dies when one goes down [|hירד|h*] to the underworld. God or a human can send someone down, and a human can go down. Someone who is in the underworld has already died. The people in the underworld are insubstantial, they fade away as a cloud, they are shades. It is a dark place, underground, yet God can see into it and knows all about it. No one can hide from God in the underworld. It is contrasted with both heaven and the earth, the land of the living.3. The underworld is also a metaphor for the danger and fear of death. The Psalmists beg God to deliver them from |hשְׁאוֹל|h*, that is, from death.  The danger of living an evil life is compared to death. Thus, the death event is used as a metaphor for a live of evil.
\level2 1
\meaning The underworld as part of the universe
\frame Universe
\domain Death
\level3 a
\context The underworld where the dead are, contrasted with other parts of the universe
\keyref 3"Q!(&!
\keyverse If I went up to heaven, you would be there; if I lay down in |bthe world of the dead|b*, you would be there.  (TEV)
\eval TEV and CEV are the most clear translations, the only ones that uses the phrase 'world of the dead'. Other translations use Sheol, the name of the underworld, which is a transliteration of the Hebrew name, but has no meaning to a non-Hebrew audience. NIV uses the term 'the depths' but it is unclear what this term is referring to in the verse.

\context The underworld as a metonymy or euphemism for death
\syntagcc The phrase |hירד שְׁאוֹל|h* is a metonymy for death, and possibly a euphemism for death. The phrase is modified in several ways: go down in sorrow, go down in peace, go down in blood, go down alive. The name for the underworld is also used as a metomony for the death event in the absence of the verb.
\keyref 3!x!$%"
\keyverse My life is so full of misery that I'm about to |bdie|b*. (KTBH)
\eval The use of 'death' for |hשְׁאוֹל|h* in various translations maintains the meaning, since |hשְׁאוֹל|h* is a metonomy for death. NIV uses 'grave', which is also acceptable in this context since 'the grave' is an English metonymy for death. TOB uses 'enfers', not an acceptable translation, since the Hebrew underworld was not a place of punishment as it is in French.

\context The underworld as a metaphore for death described as a prison or place of entrapment
\domainc Imprisonment; Rescue
\syntagcc |hשְׁאוֹל|h* as a place of imprisonment is used exclusively in poetry. There is some personification within this use, but the focus is on imprisonment or entrapment.
\keyref 3!y!Q(#
\keyverse What man can live and not see death, can save himself from the clutches of |bSheol|b*? (Tanak)
\eval All the translations consulted are adequate. Most maintain the term 'Sheol', which should have an accompanying note to explain that it is the name of the underworld. The NIV, TEV, and CEV use the term 'grave' rather the name Sheol. This is acceptable in English where 'the grave' is a euphemism for death. The TOB uses the French name for the underworld, |ienfers|i*, which is not advised since it conveys the idea of punishment.

\not (a) Psalm 55:16 uses the same phrase as Num 16:30, 33, 'go down to Sheol alive' [|hירד שְׁאוֹל חַיִּים|h*]. These three verses are the only places this phrase is used. In Numbers, some members of the clan of Kohath of the tribe of Levi challenged the leadership of Moses. They wanted to offer sacrifices just as the priests did. To restore the authority of Moses, God gave the sign that the ground opened up and the families who had challenged Moses 'went down alive to Sheol' [|hיָרְדוּ חַיִּים שְׁאֹלָה|h*]. In Psalm 55, the psalmist complains to God about his companions and close friends who betrayed him, just as the Levites who were the same tribe as Moses betrayed Moses by challenging his authority. So the psalmist asks God to justify him by causing his betrayers to 'go down to S alive' [|hיֵרְדוּ שְׁאֹול חַיִּים|h*] in the same way that God acted for Moses.

\heb שַׁחַת
\strong 7845
\pos ncf
\gloss pit, underworld
\freq 23
\sub שׁוח
\intro 1. The primary meaning of שַׁחַת is a hole in the ground which is used for a trap.2. Used metaphorically, שַׁחַת means the underworld, a place no one can leave, so a kind of trap. 3. When used metaphorically, the concept of the underworld is often a metonomy for death. Either the word itself or a whole phrase such as תִּקְרַב לַשַּׁחַת "it drew near to shaxat" can be the metonomy.
\tnotes The netherworld, the place of the dead, was seen as underground by the Hebews. It is not the same as the grave where dead bodies are put. It is also not a place of punishment, so words like "hell" where punishment in the underworld takes place are not appropriate.All the words for underworld are used metaphorically to refer to the event of death. S is used often with the words hya (life) and nephesh (soul), in phrases like "abandon my life to שַׁחַת". The whole phrase is a metonomy for death.
\evalev The words for the underworld use the verb ירד, as opposed to words for a burial place which do not use this verb.
\comp 1. The literal object.   Frame: danger2. Metaphorical use of Meaning 1.   Frame: death
\dfnotes 1. The literal object is a hole in the ground, a pit used as a trap. 2. A metaphorical extention of meaning 1 is the underworld, the place underground where the dead are, a trap from which one must escape in order to continue living. שַׁחַת by itself or phrases using שַׁחַת are a euphemism for death. One can be redeemed or ransomed from שַׁחַת, or brought up from שַׁחַת, kept back from שַׁחַת, or not see שַׁחַת, thus one does not experience death.
\level2 1
\meaning A hole in the ground used as a trap
\frame Danger
\level3 a
\context A hole in the ground used as a trap
\domainc Agression
\keyref :!3!$$"
\keyverse The nations heard about him, and he was trapped in their pit. They led him with hooks to the land of Egypt. (NIV)
\ref 2!)!?"# 3!'!0%" 3!)!0#" 3!C!'#! 4!:!;!" :!3!$$" :!3!()"
\paradigc 
\level2 2
\meaning The underworld, the place where people go after death (Metaphorical extention of Meaning 1)
\frame Death
\gram The term שַׁחַת and other terms for underworld all use the verb yrd in at least some instances. The terms for grave or burial place do not use this verb.
\level3 a
\context The underworld, the place one is rescued from when one is rescued from death
\domainc Rescue, Danger
\keyref 3"-!$""
\keyverse He keeps me from the grave and blesses me with love and mercy. (TEV)
\eval The CEV and TEV both translate the euphemism and make death explicit, but make no reference to the underworld. This gives a clear understanding of the meaning of the verse. REB includes the term 'pit', which is an alternative if the target culture has an understanding of the netherworld as being underground. The other translations are not very clear, as they don't specify what or where the 'pit' or 'abyss' is, or how it relates to death.

\heb כלם
\pos v
\gloss humiliation, reproach
\sub כּלִמָּה, כּלִמּוּת
\dfnotes In the domain of violence directed against persons, various elements are present, e.g., beard pulling, which results in shame, stripping of clothing, which results in nakedness, and thus shame, and spitting, which results in uncleanness, which contributes to shame.In the domain of violence directed against nations, humiliation is effected by force of arms, in that the obligation to defend oneself, one's family and clan, city state or sovereign is not fulfilled.
\level2 1
\meaning to humiliate by physical attack
\frame shame
\domain |bphysical violence|b*
\stem NUM 12:14      ni.ipf2SA  25:7        ni. ptc1CH 19:5         ni.ptcPSA 40:15	ni.ipfPSA 70:3	ni.ipfISA 50:7	neg + ni.pf1SA 25:7         hi.pf1SA 25:15       ho.pf
\level3 a
\context to humiliate a specific person by physical attack
\domainc |bincluding one or more of the following: beard pulling, uncleanness, nakedness, verbal insult, persecution, weapons|b*
\eventfrc agent>patientstimulus>experiencer
\keyref 7!R!'&!
\keyverse But the sovereign Lord helps me, so I am not |bhumiliated|b* (NET)
\eval RSV, NLT translate as "confounded", "dismayed" respectively. This is dispreferred because the frame "expectation", "hope" is not in focus. REB, NJB, TEV render as “no insult can wound me”, “insult has not touched me”, “insults cannot hurt me” respectivelyNRS, NJPS, NIV, CEV rendered as "disgraced." In line with this last set of English translations, NET translates as  "humiliate". We view "humiliate" as the preferred translation, since the intent to humiliate by means of assault is in view. The phrases "no insult can wound/touch/harm me" are also acceptable.

\heb חבל
\level1 1
\strong 2254
\pos v
\gloss to take as a pledge
\freq 27
\sub חֶבֶל[1], חֹבֵל, חִבֵּל, חֹבְלִים
\intro 1. The verb |hחבל|h* 'to takes as a pledge' is used to refer to an event in which a person takes an item as a security or pledge for a loan or debt.2. The one taking the item as a pledge was expected to show mercy to the poor.3. Standard lexicons list up to three different roots for the verb |hחבל|h*. Other meanings of the verb that are not related to the Commercial Transaction frame are listed in Meanings 2-5.
\comp Meaning 1 is the only meaning relevant for the Commercial Transaction frame. Meanings 2-5 are other meanings from a variety of domains that have not been thoroughly analyzed.Meaning 1.The basic event of a person taking an item as a security or pledge for a loan or debt Meaning 2. The event of acting wickedlyMeaning 3. The event of destroying thingsMeaning 4. Metaphorical extension of meaning 3 Meaning 5 The event of a woman laboring to give birth to a child. One occurrence in this meaning category is metaphorical in nature.
\level2 1
\meaning To take an item as a security or pledge for a loan or debt
\frame Commercial Transaction: loan
\domain Pledge
\stem Qal
\eventfr Agent (Creditor): human Patient: human, animal or thing Source (Debtor): human
\level3 a
\context To take an item as a security or pledge for a loan or debt
\keyref "!6!9!"
\keyverse If you |btake |b*your neighbor’s garment |bin pledge|b*, you must return it to him before the sun sets; (NJV)
\eval This is an acceptable translation and a similar translation is used in several versions (NJB, RSV, NIV, TEV). Other translations substitute a more modern term "in pawn" (NRS, REB) or a more explanatory phrase is used, "as security for a loan" (CEV). In some cultures, longer explanatory phrases will be necessary due to the lack of specific terms for this kind of a commercial transaction.
\trans to take (an item) as a security for a loan

\heb מור
\strong 4171
\level1 1
\pos v
\gloss to exchange
\freq 14
\sub תְּמוּרָה
\intro 1. The verb |hמור |h* 'to exchange' is used to refer to exchanging one item or attribute for another item or attribute. 2. Money is not mentioned in any context with this type of exchange. 3. Items exchanged are technically equivalent, but often an item of lesser quality or value is exchanged for one of greater quality or value. 4. In addition to external exchanges, the verb can refer to an internal change of nature in an item.
\comp Meaning 1 refers to the basic event of exchanging one item or attribute for another item or attribute. Meaning 2 refers to the basic event of an internal change that occurs in the nature of the item.
\level2 1
\meaning To exchange one item for another item
\frame Commercial Transaction
\domain Various (according to the nature of the items exchanged)
\stem Hiphil, Niphal
\gram There is only one occurrence of the Niphal.
\eventfr Agent:  human Patient: animal, thing, or attributePatient (second Patient that is replaced by first Patient): animal, thing, or attribute
\syntagc Items exchanged may be viewed as equivalent in value by one party in the exchange, but in many examples an item of lesser value or quality is substituted for an item of greater value or quality.
\level3 a
\context To exchange one item or attribute for another item or attribute
\eventfrc Agent:  human Patient: animal, thing, or attribute Instrument (Item replacing Patient): animal, thing, or attribute

\heb מְחִיר
\level1 1
\strong 4242, 4243
\pos ncm
\gloss price
\freq 16
\intro 1. The term |hמְחִיר  |h*'price' is used to refer to money or other items that are used in a commercial transaction (Meaning 1). The price most frequently refers to an amount of money that has been or will be given in exchange for the purpose of acquiring ownership of an item. 2. Meaning 2 is a proper name for a male.
\comp Meaning 1.The the amount of money used in a commercial transactionMeaning 2: A proper name
\level2 1
\meaning Money or other items that are given in exchange for the purpose of transferring ownership of an item or obtaining a service considered to be equal in value
\frame Commercial Transaction
\domain Various (according to the item being sold)
\level3 a
\context Money or other items that are given in exchange for the purpose of transferring ownership of an item or obtaining a service considered to be equal in value
\keyref 7!W!!/!
\keyverse "Come, buy wine and milk without silver and without a |bprice|b*."
\eval NRS, RSV, and REB all use the generic noun 'price'. Other translations use the term 'cost' (NIV, TEV) or the specific phrase 'paying a cent' (CEV). All of these are acceptable translations, though the TEV and CEV collapse the translation of parallel phrases into a single phrase.

\heb מכר
\strong 4376
\level1 1
\pos v
\gloss to sell
\freq 80
\sub מֶכֶר, מִמְכָּר, מִמְכֶּרֶת
\intro 1.  The verb |hמכר|h* ‘to sell’ is used most frequently to refer to a commercial transaction that involves the sale of an item (including humans) to another human for a price. 2.  Selling an item involves a change of ownership and control. 3.  These elements of ownership and control allow for the metaphorical usage of the verb |hמכר|h* ‘to sell’ to refer to God's causing Israel to be defeated in battle (Meaning 2a) and his sending them into exile (Meaning 2b). In addition, the verb |hמכר|h* ‘to sell’  can be used metaphorically for the rejection of a positive moral value (Meaning 4) and for submitting oneself totally to doing evil (Meaning 3).The final metaphorical meaning (Meaning 5) is uncertain, but seems to refer to the person who is enslaving or exploiting others by a variety of means.
\comp The five meanings of |hמכר|h* ‘to sell’ can be basically divided into two groups. Those occurrences that refer to a literal commercial transaction (Meaning 1) and those that are used metaphorically (Meanings 2-5). Meaning 1.  The basic event (focus: the commercial transaction) Meaning 2.  Metaphorical use of 1 (focus: ownership) Meaning 3.  Metaphorical use of 1 (focus: control) Meaning 4.  Metaphorical use of 1 (focus: ownership) Meaning 5.  Metaphorical and metonymic use of 1 (focus: control)
\dfnotes 1. In Meaning 1 humans sell various items including other humans. This usage triggers the Commercial Transaction frame and the sell sub-frame. The Commercial Transaction frame is used against the background of a variety of domains depending on the item being sold. This includes the Slavery domain and one occurrence that is best understood against the background of the Marriage domain. 2. In Meaning 2, YHWH is selling His people and this use of the verb triggers the Ownership frame and the disown sub-frame. One of the background cognitive domains necessary to make this correct interpretation is the 'YHWH is a king' metaphor that is a recurring theme throughout the Old Testament. YHWH is like a king who has made a treaty with the people of Israel - one that obligates both parties to fulfill certain requirements. When Israel breaks this agreement, by not fulfilling their obligations, then YHWH has the right to respond accordingly. In the context of a king's responsibilities towards his subjects, ‘disown’ means ‘relieve oneself of the responsibility to protect’ which in turn means ‘allow their enemies to defeat them’. The purpose is to punish them for their rebellion. 3. In Meaning 3, a person is selling himself to the control of evil. This use of the verb triggers the Control frame and the submit sub-frame. This meaning is best understood against the background of the domains of Pagan Religions, Evil, and Sin.  A person submits to the control of evil by participating in pagan activities such as divination, child sacrifice, and augury. All of these activities are viewed as evil by God. 4. In Meaning 4, a person is encouraged to not sell truth. This metaphorical use of the verb triggers the Ownership frame and the disown sub-frame. This usage is best understood against the background of the Wisdom/Folly domain. Wise people are those who buy (acquire possession of) truth and do not sell (disown or reject) it. 5. Meaning 5 is uncertain, but it seems to refer to an entity that is enslaving or exploiting another by the use of various kinds of evil activities. This metaphorical use of the participle triggers the Control frame and the submit sub-frame and focuses on the agent of the activity. This metaphorical meaning is best understood against the background of the domains of Exploitation, Slavery, and Evil. An entity (the city of Nineveh who is spoken of metaphorically as a harlot) causes the Israelites to submit to her control (i.e., sells them) by means of various evil activities.
\level2 1

\frame Commercial Transaction
\domain Various (according to the item being sold)
\stem Qal, Niphal, Hithpael (1x)
\eventfr Agent (Seller): human Patient (Item): human, animal, or thing Recipient (Buyer): human Instrument (Price): silver, gold, or other valued item
\level3 a
\context To sell (a thing); To transfer ownership of a thing to another human in exchange for money or other items considered equal in value
\eventfrc Agent (Seller): human Patient (Item): animal or thing Recipient (Buyer): human Instrument (Price): silver, gold, or other valued item

\keyref B!#!$(!
\keyverse "Because of the countless debaucheries of the prostitute, gracefully alluring, mistress of sorcery, who |benslaves |b*nations through her debaucheries, and peoples through her sorcery," (NRS)
\eval NRS is an acceptable translation of a verse whose specific meaning is uncertain.  Versions vary widely in their translation of this verse, but none of the 8 translations consulted translated |hמכר|h* ‘to sell’ literally. The main grammatical difficulty in translating this verse is determining the semantic functions of |hבִּזְנוּנֶיהָ|h* ‘through her debaucheries’ and |hבִּכְשָׁפֶיהָ|h* ‘through her sorcery’. In most verses with |hמכר|h* ‘to sell’ the preposition |hבּ|h* ‘with’ is used to refer to the instrument for which an item is sold, e.g., ‘for twenty pieces of silver’ (Gen 37:28). In at least one verse, the preposition |hבּ|h* ‘with’ indicates the reason the sale was made, i.e., ‘for your sins’ (Isa 50:1). If the first interpretation is chosen, then she was selling the nations so that she would be able to commit adultery and magic as a result of the sale. If the second interpretation is chosen, then she was selling the nations because of her adultery and magic. Most translations follow neither of these interpretations. Instead, the phrases are interpreted as the means by which the nations are enslaved or trapped, as in CEV and other versions -- ‘You were nothing more than a prostitute using your magical charms and witchcraft to attract and trap nations’.
\trans to enslave; to exploit

\heb מִקְנָה
\strong 4736
\level1 1
\pos ncf
\gloss purchased item
\freq 15
\sub קנה
\intro 1. The noun |hמִקְנָה|h* ‘purchased item’ is used most frequently to refer to an item (including humans) that has been acquired and is under the ownership of another human as a result of an exchange of money or other items considered to be equal in value. 2. When there is a transfer of ownership, the noun |hמִקְנָה|h* ‘purchased item’ can be used to refer to the money given in exchange for the item. 3. In addition, the completed event can also be referred to by the same term, |hמִקְנָה|h* ‘purchased item’.
\comp Meaning 1 refers to the patient of the event of acquisition. Meaning 2 refers to the instrument (money) of the event of acquisition. Meaning 3 refers to the completed event.
\level2 1
\meaning An item that has been acquired and is under the ownership of another human as a result of an exchange of money or other items considered to be equal in value
\frame Commercial Transaction
\eventfr Agent (Buyer): human Patient (Item): human, thing Source (Seller): human Instrument (Price): silver, gold, or other valued item
\level3 a
\context A piece of land that has been acquired and is under the ownership of another human as a result of an exchange of money or other items considered to be equal in value
\eventfrc |b|b*Agent (Buyer): human Patient (Item): land Source (Seller): human Instrument (Price): silver, gold, or other valued item
\keyref !!7!2"!
\keyverse to Abraham as his |bproperty |b*in the presence of all the Hittites who had come to the gate of the city. (NIV)
\eval '|blegal possession |b*of Abraham, in the presence of all the Hittites who had assembled at the city gate.' (REB) Most English translations use either 'possession' or 'property'. Both are acceptable choices, but property is probably the more commonly used term for land.  REB uses the phrase ‘legal possession,’ which is probably more a translation of the context than of the word |hמִקְנָה|h* ‘purchased item’.
\ref !!7!2"! #!;!6#!

\context A human that has been acquired and is under the ownership of another human as a result of an exchange of money or other items considered to be equal in value; a slave
\domainc Slavery
\eventfrc Agent (Buyer): human Patient (Item): human Source (Seller): human Instrument (Price): silver, gold, or other valued items.
\keyref !!1!7)!
\keyverse Then Abraham took his son Ishmael and all the slaves born in his house or |bbought |b*with his money, every male among the men of Abraham's house, and he circumcised the flesh of their foreskins that very day, as God had said to him. (NRS)
\eval Most English translations use a verbal phrase ('he had bought') with or without a literal translation of the word 'its silver'. CEV's translation 'and so were all other men and boys in his household, including his servants and slaves' seems to equate 'servants' with ones born in his household and 'slaves' as ones bought with his money. These would not, however, be the naturally understood equivalents for these terms.  Because of this it would be best not to follow CEV's example.

\eventfr Agent (Buyer): human Patient (Item): human, thing Source (Seller): human Instrument (Price): silver, gold, or other valued item  |b |b*
\level3 a
\context The amount of money given in order to acquire ownership of an item
\keyref #!9!S(!
\keyverse If many years remain, he must pay for his redemption a larger share of the |bprice |b*paid for him. (NIV)
\eval 'The longer the time until then, the more you will have to pay.' (CEV) English translations generally use the term 'price' to translate this meaning of the term. The difference between this meaning and Meaning 1b, may be seen in a comparison of the meaning and the translation of the construct chain |hמִקְנַת כַּסְפֶּךָ|h* 'purchased item of your silver' in Gen 17.12 and the construct chain |hכֶּסֶף מִקְנָתוֹ|h* 'silver of his purchased item' in Lev 25:51. In the first, the focus is on the fact that the slave (Item) was purchased with money. In the second, the focus is on the amount of money (Price) involved in the purchase.  In Lev 25:51 the term |hמִקְנָה|h* ‘purchased item’ is translated by terms such as ‘(price) paid for him,’ (NIV, RSV) ‘pay’ (CEV), ‘purchase (price)’ (NRS, TEV, NJPS) ‘sale-price’ (NJB), ‘(sum) for which he sold himself’ (REB). All of these translations are basically adequate.

\heb נשׁא
\strong 5377, 5378, 5383
\level1 1
\pos v
\gloss to deceive
\freq 20
\sub מַשָּׁא, מַשָּׁאוֹן, מַשּׁוּאוֹת, נְשִׁי
\intro 1. One meaning of the verb |hנשׁא |h*is 'to collect interest on a loan.' This meaning only occurs 3 times. 2. The most frequent meaning of the verb is 'to deceive'. 3. Another infrequent meaning of the verb is 'to compel'.4. In one occurrence, most translations seemed to be based on a textual variant that is translated  'to lift up', rather than a meaning related to this verb root. 5. All occurrences of the verb seem to contain an element of "compulsion", i.e. being forced, compelled, or persuaded to do something that the patient would not otherwise have done.
\comp Meaning 1 refers to an event that is a type of commercial transaction. Meanings 2-4 refer to other domains and have not been thoroughly analyzed.Meaning 2 refers to the event of forcing something upon someone or compelling someone to do something. Mearning 3 refers to the event of deceiving someone or causing someone to have an inaccurate view of the truth. Meaning 4 includes one reference, the meaning of which is uncertain due to textual variants and other problems of interpretation.
\level2 1
\meaning To collect interest on a loan
\frame Commercial Transaction: loan
\stem Qal
\gram All examples are participles.
\level3 a
\context To collect interest on a loan
\keyref 0!%!',!
\keyverse After thinking it over, I brought charges against the nobles and the officials; I said to them, “You are all |btaking interest |b*from your own people.” And I called a great assembly to deal with them, . . . (NRS)
\eval This is an acceptable translation. Many translations focus on the negative impact of collecting interest and translate the term in a variety of ways, e.g., 'oppressing' (TEV), 'imposing a burden' (REB) and 'pressing claims on loans' (NJV).
\trans to collect interest on a loan
\ref )!6!"'! 0!%!',! 7!8!".!
\level1 3
\paradigc Agent_focus
\pgloss creditor
\refsc )!6!"'! 7!8!".!

\heb סחר
\strong 5503
\level1 1
\pos v
\gloss to travel in order to trade
\freq 21
\sub מִסְחָר ,סַחַר ,סְחֹרָה ,סֹחֵרָה ,סֹחֶרֶת
\intro 1.  The verb |hסחר|h* ‘to travel in order to trade’ is used most frequently to refer to the activity of traveling for the purpose of buying and selling items (including humans). 2. Movement (Travelling) is one of the central components of the meaning of this verb. 3. This element of movement allows for the metaphorical usage of the verb in which the heart is described as moving rapidly using a variant of the Piel verb form.  This unique verb form is used to refer to a state of the mind.
\comp Meaning 1. The basic event   (focus: movement and commercial transaction)Meaning 2. Metaphorical use of meaning 1 (focus: Concern)
\level2 1
\meaning To travel around for the purpose of engaging in the buying and selling of items
\frame Commercial Transaction
\domain Travelling
\stem Qal
\eventfr Agent (Seller):         human Patient (Item):          thing Recipient (Buyer):   human Instrument (Price):  silver, gold, or other valued item
\level3 a
\context To travel around for the purpose of engaging in the buying and selling of items
\keyref !!J!B1!
\keyverse . . . Then I will release your brother to you, and you may |btrade |b*in the land. (NRS)
\eval NRS '... you may trade in the land' focuses on the economic aspect of the meaning of the verb. While it is true that trading is an aspect of the meaning of the verb, it fails to distinguish between this verb and |hרכל |h*. The NJB 'and you will be free to move about the country.' and NJV ' you shall be free to move about in the land,' correctly recognize the movement that is indicated by this verb root, but fail to include the economic activity. A better translation would include both activities, i.e. 'you may travel and trade in the land'.

\keyref 8!.!2-!
\keyverse If I go out to the country— Lo, the slain of the sword. If I enter the city— Lo, those who are sick with famine. Both priest and prophet |broam |b*the land, They know not where. (NJPS)
\eval The NJPS is an acceptable translation that reflects the interpretation of KTBH (See footnote (a).) Other translations that follow this interpretation include NJB 'roam' and REB 'wander without rest'. NIV appears to follow this interpretation, but their translation choice, 'have gone (to the land)', fails to reflect the continuous nature of the activity. Other translations seem to assign this reference to meaning 1 and have translations that reflect that choice, e.g. ' ply their trade' (RSV, NRSV), 'carry on their work' (TEV), 'go about their business' (CEV).  The translations that have chosen meaning 1 have left the nature of the business of the priest and prophet unstated. If the translator chooses meaning 1 instead of meaning 2, then they should make sure that the translation does not imply that the priests and prophets are not traveling around trading goods.

KTBH has assigned these three occurrences to meaning 1 due to the overall context, but Jeremaiah 14:18 has been assigned to meaning 2 because of the fact that the agents are priests and prophets.
