\heb ערב
\level1 1
\strong 6148; 6149;6150
\pos v
\gloss to mortgage
\freq 33
\sub מַעֲרָב, עֲרֻבָּה, עֶרֶב, עֵרֶב, עָרֵב, עָרֹב, עֵרָבוֹן, תַּעֲרוּבָה
\intro 1. The verb |hערב|h* 'to mortgage' is used to refer to several different types of commercial transactions. These include 'to mortgage', 'to guarantee the repayment of the debt of another' and 'to trade'.  These types of activities are used metaphorically to refer to events in other domains. 2. The event of mortgaging items or guaranteeing the repayment of the debt of another both have negative connotations. 3. The verb |hערב |h*also may be used for several other meanings including 'to mix with', 'to be pleasing', and 'to become evening'. There are also meanings that are metaphorical extensions of these basic meanings.
\comp Meaning 1. The basic event of mortgaging an item Meaning 2. The basic event of guaranteeing the repayment of someone's debt Meaning 3. Metaphorical extension of meaning 2. Rather than guaranteeing the repayment, the agent is guaranteeing the safety of another. Meaning 4. Metaphorical extension of meaning 2. Rather than guaranteeing the repayment, the idiom with heart refers to the eagerness about doing an event. Meaning 5. Another type of economic event -- 'to trade' Meaning 6. A metaphorical extension of meaning 5. Instead of actually trade, it refers to the wager about a potential trade. Meaning 7. A non-economic event -- 'to mix with' Meaning 8. A metaphorical extension of meaning 7 -- 'to share' Meaning 9. A non-economic event -- 'to be pleasing' Meaning 10. A non-economic event -- 'to become evening' Meaning 11. A metaphorical extension of meaning 10 -- 'to become dark'
\level2 1
\meaning To mortgage; To place an item under the control of another person in order to obtain something one is lacking
\frame Commercial Transaction: loan
\stem Qal
\eventfr Agent: human Patient: thing or human
\syntagc Job 17:3 has been interpreted in a variety of ways.
\level3 a
\context To mortgage; To place an item under the control of another person in order to obtain something one is lacking
\keyref 0!%!#(!
\keyverse Others were saying, “We |bare mortgaging|b* our fields, our vineyards and our homes to get grain during the famine.” (NIV)
\eval Most translations use the term 'to mortgage', a technical term that is known in countries with developed banking systems.  In languages that lack such technical terminology the translator may use a more generic translation, such as 'to pledge' (NRS) or a much longer explanation may have to be made to explain the idea of borrowing money and having to give someone land if the money is not returned by a certain date.

\heb קנה
\strong 7069
\level1 1
\pos v
\gloss to buy
\freq 85
\sub מִקְנֶה ,מִקְנָה ,קִנְיָן
\intro 1.  The verb |hקנה|h* ‘to buy’ is used most frequently to refer to a commercial transaction that involves the acquisition of an item (including humans) by another human for a price. 2.  Buying an item involves a change of ownership and control. 3. These elements of ownership and control allow for the metaphorical usage of the verb in which items are acquired not through the exchange of money or other items, but through the effort of the person acquiring the item. The effort expended may be that of childbirth (Meaning 2a), God's effort in redeeming people from slavery or in acquiring something by means of warfare (Meaning 2b), and the effort spent on the acquisition of abstract qualities (e.g. wisdom) by a variety of means (Meaning 2c). 4. The verb |hקנה|h* can also mean 'to create' (Meaning 3) a concept related to the issue of ownership and control.
\altint In most major English translations, the five occurrences of |hקנה |h*‘to buy/create’ discussed in Meaning 3 have been consistently translated ‘to create’ or ‘creator’.  The UBS Handbooks on Genesis, Deuteronomy, and Proverbs discuss the positive and negative factors about choosing to translate this verb as ‘to create’ versus ‘to acquire’. (Reyburn and Fry 1997: 324; Reyburn and Fry 2000; Bratcher and Hatton 2000) The NIV includes an alternate translation in four of these verses. In Gen 14:19, 22, NIV lists ‘possessor’ as the alternate translation for ‘creator’ and in Deu 32:6, the alternate translation is ‘who bought you’.  In Pro 8:22, the NIV note suggests the translation ‘possessed me’.  If this interpretation is chosen, then the meanings of these four occurrences would be similar to Meaning 1b. The presence of synonyms in Deu 32:6 that are not economic in nature and the use of the translation equivalent κτ|gί|g*ζω ‘to create’ in the LXX in three of the occurrences, however, provides evidence that these five occurrences probably refer to the creation of items. This does not, however, necessitate the assigning of these meanings to a separate root, as is suggested in some lexicons. Rather, the possibility of alternate interpretations points to the related nature of the concepts of acquisition and creation, i.e. created items are the possessions/acquisitions of the one who created them. This is another example that shows that Hebrew words often have a greater range of meaning than their equivalents in English and other languages. In Pro 8:22 |hקנה|h* ‘to buy/create’ refers to the creation of wisdom using a metaphorical extension of Meaning 2a. The metaphorical nature of this occurrence can be seen by the use of the term |hחִיל |h*‘to bring forth’ in Pro 8:24-25 to describe the same event. The interpretation/translation of Pro 8:22 has varied through the years as a result of the use of this verse in theological debates.  These theological debates may have had some affect on the early revisions of the LXX, which revised the Old Greek from |gἔκτισε |g*‘he created’ to |gἐκτήσατο ‘he acquired’. As a metaphorical extension of Meaning 2a, this usage of |g*|hקנה|h* ‘to buy/create’ in Pro 8:22 has been assigned to Meaning 3 ‘to create.’ Because of collocational clashes inherent in the image of God giving birth, it is likely that |hקנה |h*will need to be translated as ‘to create’ rather than 'to produce'.  Similes will also probably need to be used in Pro 8:24-25 to communicate the similar concept of  |hחִיל |h*‘to bring forth’, that is used in Pro 8:24-25.
\evalev A brief examination of the translation of the phrase |hהִקְנַ֥נִי אָדָ֖ם|h* ‘a man has acquired me’ in Zec 13:5 will quickly illustrate the fact that the MT has been emended and read in a variety of ways in major English translations.  Among the translations examined for KTBH, the most frequent translation was based on the emended text |hאדמה קניני|h* ‘ground is my possession’, as may be seen in the following translations: ‘the land has been my possession’ (RSV, NRS, REB) ‘the land has been my livelihood’ (NIV), ‘the land has been my living’ (NJB), ‘I have farmed the land’ (TEV). In the CEV this phrase is apparently viewed as a parallelism that could be collapsed and hence there is no specific translation of the phrase. The NJV has a rather unique translation that evidently reads the word |hאדם|h* ‘man’ as being connected with the root meaning ‘red’ and therefore translates it as follows: ‘I was plied with the red stuff’ (NJPS). In addition, the NEB has read the root as being from the verb |hקנא|h* ‘to be jealous’ and has translated it accordingly as ‘who has been schooled in lust’ (NEB). (Smith 2002: 280) While the majority of the major English translations followed an emended text that leads to a very smooth reading, HOTTP suggests translating it as ‘a man has acquired me <as a slave>’, which follows the MT (Barthélemy, Prophetical Books II 1980: 422). The LXX translates the verb by the term |gἄνθρωπος ἐγέννησέν με |g*‘a man has given birth to me’, which seems to indicate that the translator was reading a text similar to the MT, though he did not use the unmarked translation equivalent |gκτάομαι |g*‘to acquire’ for the translation of the verb |hקנה|h* ‘to buy’. Some translations, such as the Tok Pisin Bible, reflect an interpretation based on the MT. This translation reads as follows: Mi stap wokboi nating bilong wanpela man ‘I am the slave of one man.’ The Tok Pisin translation reinforces the preceding statement, i.e. he is just a slave and therefore he could not be a prophet. In an ideal world, KTBH recommends that the MT and the recommendations of the HOTTP be followed. KTBH recognizes, however, that in order for a translation to be accepted the emended text that is used by most major translations in English may need to be followed.
\comp The three meanings of |hקנה|h* ‘to buy’ can be basically divided into three groups.  Those that refer to a literal commercial transaction (Meaning 1), those in which 'buy' is used metaphorically for a type of acquisition (Meaning 2), and those that refer to the event of creation (Meaning 3). Meaning 1. The basic event (focus: the commercial transaction); perspective: buyerMeaning 1a. Patient: thingMeaning 1b. Patient: humanMeaning 1c: Patient: brideMeaning 2. Metaphorical use of Meaning 1 (focus: control and ownership) Meaning 2a: To acquire a human being through the process of childbirth |b|b*Meaning 2b: To acquire authority over a people group or a piece of land by means of a demonstration of power |b|b*Meaning 2c: To acquire (a moral value)Meaning 3. The event of creation
\level2 1
\meaning To buy; To acquire ownership of an item from another human in exchange for money or other items considered to be equal in value
\frame Commercial Transaction
\stem Qal, Niphal, Hiphil (1x)|na|n*
\eventfr Agent (Buyer): 	human Patient (Item): 		human, thing Source (Seller): 	human Instrument (Price):	silver, gold, or other valued item
\level3 a
\context To buy (a thing); To acquire ownership of a thing from another human in exchange for money or other items considered to be equal in value
\domainc Various (according to item being acquired)
\eventfrc Agent (Buyer): 	           human Patient (Item): 		thing Source (Seller): 	           human Instrument (Price):	silver, gold, or other valued item
\keyref *!8!8%!
\keyverse But the king said to Araunah, “No, but I |bwill buy |b*them from you for a price; I will not offer burnt offerings to the Lord my God that cost me nothing.” So David |bbought |b*the threshing floor and the oxen for fifty shekels of silver. (NRSV)
\eval Many English translations (NRSV, RSV, NJV, REB) use the term 'buy', which properly focuses on the acquisition of the item, to translate both occurrences of |hקנה|h* in this verse. Some translations, however, use the term 'pay' (NIV, TEV, CEV) or an even more generic 'give ... a price' (NJB). While both 'pay' and 'give ... a price' are still in the Economic Domain, they focus more on the transfer of money than on the acquisition of an item, which is a major component of the meaning of |hקנה|h* 'to buy'.

\eval CEV and TEV were the only English translations of Ruth 4:10 examined that clearly chose translation equivalents from the Marriage Domain -- 'agreed to marry' (CEV) and 'becomes my wife' (TEV).  Most English translations chose to either translate generically -- 'acquire' (NIV, NRS, NJB, NJV) 'taken over' (REB) --  or to use a term from the Economic Domain -- 'bought' (RSV).  If the receptor culture has terminology for this type of buying of wives, then those terms should be used. If not, then terms that evoke the Marriage Domain may be more appropriate.

\level3 a
\context To acquire a human being through the process of childbirth
\domainc Childbirth
\eventfrc Agent:      woman Patient:    child
\keyref !!$!!)!
\keyverse . . . and she gave birth to Cain and said, "I have |bacquired |b*a male child with the help of YHWH." (KTBH)
\eval 'Now the man knew his wife Eve, and she conceived and bore Cain, saying, "I have produced a man with the help of the LORD."' (NRS) The NRS translation 'produced' focuses on the immediate context of childbirth and translates accordingly. Other English translations of a similar nature include  'brought forth' (NIV) and 'brought into being' (REB). If this interpretation is followed, then this reference might fit better with Meaning 3, i.e. 'to create'. Because of the similarities between Meanings 2a and 2b, however, it seems better to translate the term using more generic terms for the acquisition of something, e.g., 'got' (CEV), 'have gotten' (RSV, TEV), 'acquired' (NJB) and 'gained' (NJV). In many languages, these kinds of generic terms for acquisition are the natural way of euphemistically referring to childbirth.
\ref !!$!!)!
\paradigc Similar
\colc ילד
\pgloss to give birth to
\refsc !!$!!)!
\level3 b
\context To acquire authority over a people group or a piece of land by means of a demonstration of power
\domainc Warfare; Slavery
\eventfrc Agent:      God Patient:    human, land
\syntagcc Three of these references occur in contexts that refer to the use of God's power, i.e. 'your arm' (EXO 15:16), 'his right hand' (PSA 78:54) or 'his hand' (ISA 11:11).
\keyref "!/!0.!
\keyverse Terror and dread fell upon them; by the might of your arm, they became still as a stone until your people, O Lord, passed by, until the people whom you |bacquired |b*passed by. (NRS)
\eval Many translations maintain the use of terminology from the commerce domain using terms such as 'bought' (NIV) or 'purchased' (RSV, NJB). This leads to the false idea that God was physically purchasing people for enslavement. Other translations use terminology from the slavery or exile frames, such as 'set free from slavery' (TEV), 'rescued for your very own' (CEV) and 'You have ransomed' (NJV). A few translations use neutral terminology such as 'acquired' (NRS) and 'made your own' (REB). Either of these last two approaches is an improvement over the use of terminology from the Commerce Domain.

\paradigc Similar
\colc גאל
\pgloss to redeem
\refsc 3!j!"#!
\level3 c
\context To acquire (a moral value)
\domainc Wisdom
\eventfrc Agent:     human Patient:   abstract quality
\keyref 4!0!0$!
\keyverse How much better to |bget |b*wisdom than gold! To |bget |b*understanding is to be chosen rather than silver. (NRS)
\eval Most translations use a general term for acquiring an item -- 'get' (NIV, NRS, REB, RSV), 'acquire' (NJV) 'gain' (NJB). If a language does not speak naturally about acquiring abstract qualities, then a better non-literal translation would be as follows: "It's much better to spend effort on becoming wise than on getting gold and on becoming sensible than on getting silver." This non-literal translation maintains the emphasis on the process that is indicated by the use of |hקנה|h* 'to buy'. CEV is an example of a non-literal translation, but it incorrectly shifts the emphasis from the acquiring of the attributes to the state of the person after the acquisition of these abstract qualities. "It's much better to be wise and sensible than to be rich." (CEV) In addition, CEV collapses the parallel lines, which is not an approach recommended by KTBH.

\frame Commercial Transaction
\domain Economic
\eventfr |b Agent (Buyer): human Patient (Item): human, thing Source (Seller): human Instrument (Price) silver, gold, or other valued item  |b*
\level3 a
\context An item that has been acquired and is under the ownership of another human as a result of an exchange of money or other items considered to be equal in value
\eventfrc |b |b*Agent (Buyer): human Patient (Item): human, thing Source (Seller): human Instrument (Price) silver, gold, or other valued item
\keyref !!D!&+!
\keyverse Esau took his wives, his sons and daughters and all the members of his household, his livestock, all the animals, and all the possessions he had acquired in Canaan, and went to the district of Seir out of the way of his brother Jacob, . . . (REB)
\eval Esau took his wives and sons and daughters and all the members of his household, as well as his livestock and all his other animals and all the goods he had acquired in Canaan, and moved to a land some distance from his brother Jacob. (NIV) English translations use terms such as goods (NIV, NJB ), property (NRS, RSV, NJPS), and possessions (CEV, TEV, REB). Referentially these are all accurate.  Difficulties in understanding, however, arise due to the level of language and the fact that the more common usages of some terms are too limited, i.e. "goods" is used more frequnetly for merchandise and "property" is used more frequently for land. All examples in Meaning 1a refer to moveable items.

\eval However, any slave that you own, including those born into your household, may eat this food. (CEV) The CEV's translation 'that you own' fails to communicate that these items were purchase by that priest, rather than being someone that he owned as a result of inheritance. Other English translations such as 'buy/bought' (REB, TEV, NIV) or "acquired . . . by purchase" (NRS) are acceptable.

\heb רְכוּשׁ
\strong 7399
\level1 1
\pos ncm
\gloss possessions
\freq 28
\sub רכשׁ
\intro The term |hרְכוּשׁ|h* 'possessions' refers to items that have been acquired by one of a variety of means.  These items are then under the ownership of another human. |h   |h*
\comp There is only one meaning for |hרְכוּשׁ |h* 'possessions'.
\dfnotes In 2 Chronicles 35:7, |hרְכוּשׁ |h* is used as a summary term that includes all kinds of animals in herds and flocks as well as other possessions such as silver, gold, other costly items, and storage buildings with grain, wine, and other produce .
\level2 1
\meaning An item that has been acquired and is under the ownership of another human
\frame Commercial Transaction
\eventfr Event: to acquireAgent: humanPatient: thing
\syntagc The word |hרְכוּשׁ  |h*does not appear to include human beings (Gen 14:16, 21 ). It does, however, sometimes include animals (Gen 36:7). At other times, |hרְכוּשׁ|h*   appears to refer to items in addition to or other than certain types of animals that are listed separately (Gen 46.6). Hence |hמִקְנֶה|h* can be in both a part-whole relationship with |hרְכוּשׁ|h*, i.e., possessions including livestock, and it can also be the opposite of  |hרְכוּשׁ|h*, i.e. livestock and NOT possessions. Some examples may be interpreted in at least two different ways. For instance, the phrase |h לִבְהֶמְתָּם וְלִרְכֻשָׁם וּלְכֹל חַיָּתָֽם׃|h* may be interpreted as referring to two coordinated items that are exclusive of each other and both of these two items are included in the all living creatures category. A second interpretation is that each of these three terms refer to distinct referents that are exclusive of the other two items. In this interpretation, the term |hחַיָּה|h* would refer to other animals that are are neither cattle nor possessions, i.e., wild animals.
\level3 a
\context An item that has been acquired and is under the ownership of another human
\keyref !!.!0##
\keyverse He brought back all the |bpossessions|b*; he also brought back his kinsman Lot and his |bpossessions|b*, and the women and the rest of the people. (NJV)
\eval English translations included terms such as 'possessions' (TEV, CEV, NIV),  'goods,' (NRS, RSV),  and 'flocks and herds' (REB). Most of these terms are acceptable contextual translations, but the phrase 'flocks and herds' may be too restrictive since other items were probably included.
\trans possessions

\heb רכל
\strong 7402
\level1 1
\pos v
\gloss to trade
\freq 17
\sub מַרְכֹּלֶת ,רָכִיל ,רְכֻלָּה
\intro 1. The root |hרכל|h* ‘to trade’ is used to refer to a commercial transaction that involves the exchange of items with another entity for the purpose of earning a living. 2. The items that may be exchanged include both humans and non-humans.
\comp There is only one meaning of |hרכל|h* ‘to trade’.
\level2 1
\meaning To engage in the exchange of items with other entities for the purpose of earning a living (an income)
\frame Commercial Transaction
\stem Qal
\eventfr Agent (Seller):              humans, cities Patient (Item):              things, humans Recipient (Buyer):        humans, cities Instrument (Payment): things
\level3 a
\context To engage in the exchange of items with other entities for the purpose of earning a living (an income)
\keyref :!;!1%!
\keyverse Judah and the land of Israel |btraded |b*with you; they exchanged for your merchandise wheat from Minnith, millet, honey, oil, and balm. (NRS)
\eval Judah and Israel paid for your goods with wheat, honey, olive oil, and spices. (TEV) TEV has collapsed two parallel phrases and used 'paid' for both verbs rather than first describing the generic trading relationship and then giving the specifics of the items that are traded, i.e. agricultural goods in exchange for merchandise. NRS and other translations that do maintain the parallelism use the verb 'to trade'.

\heb שָׂכִיר
\level1 1
\strong 7916, 7917
\pos a
\gloss hired
\freq 18
\sub שׂכר
\intro 1. The term |hשָׂכִיר |h* is used most frequently to refer to a person that has been given money or other valuable items in order to perform a particular service or work.2. The hired person may be either a general worker or a soldier.3. The term may also be used to refer to animals whose owners are given money for the use of their services. 4. The term is used metaphorically ('hired razor') for someone that God uses to perform a task.
\comp Meaning 1. Refers to the receipient of money who is also the agent performing an acitvityMeaning 2. Refers to the agent performing an activity whose owner has been given moneyMeaning 3. Refers to an entity used by God to perform an activity.
\level2 1
\meaning A person that has been given money or other valuable items in order to perform a particular service or work
\frame Commercial Transaction: hire
\level3 a
\context A person that has been given money or other valuable items in order to perform a particular service or work
\keyref #!3!-'!
\keyverse You are not to oppress your neighbour or rob him. Do not keep back a |bhired man's |b*wages till next morning. (REB)
\eval The REB is an acceptable translation. Translations of this term vary greatly depending on the context. In this context,  the fact that the person is being paid for his labor is explicit.  Because of this, translations such as 'laborer' (NRS, NJB, NJV) and 'workers' (CEV) are acceptable, but in other contexts it will be necessary to make explicit the fact that the worker has been hired.  This has been done by means of phrases in some translations, e.g. 'someone you have hired' (TEV),  'hired servant' (RSV), and 'hired man' (NIV, REB).
\trans hired man

\level3 b
\context mercenary; a foreign soldier hired to go to war on behalf of another country
\domainc Warfare
\keyref 8!N!5!"
\keyverse Even her |bmercenaries |b*in her midst are like fatted calves; they too have turned and fled together, they did not stand; for the day of their calamity has come upon them, the time of their punishment. (NRS)
\eval Most English translations use the term 'mercenaries'. A few versions use less technical phrases such as 'foreign soldiers' (CEV) or 'hired soldiers' (RSV, TEV). If a language does not have technical terminology for this concept, then it is best to use a phrase of this nature.
\trans mercenary

\level2 2

\eval All of the English translations examined used the term 'to hire' with the exception of the CEV 'to lease'. Either of these is an acceptable translation.

\paradigc 
\level2 3
\meaning A thing (razor) whose services are used (This is a metaphorical usage of meaning 2 that in the larger context refers to the using of a foreign king and his army to accomplish God's purposes.)
\frame Commercial Transaction: hire
\domain Warfare
\level3 a
\context A thing (razor) whose services are used (This is a metaphorical usage of meaning 2 that in the larger context refers to the using of a foreign king and his army to accomplish God's purposes.)
\keyref 7!'!4&"
\keyverse That day the Lord will shave, with a razor |bhired |b*from the other side of the River (with the king of Assyria), the head and the hair of the leg, and take off the beard, too. (NJB)
\eval Most translations retain a literal translation of this phrase due to the extended nature of the metaphor or shaving with a razor. As is is true with many metaphors, some aspects of the normal meaning of the word are not easily interpreted.  When an item is hired, the payment is usually made to the owner of the item.  God obviously is not making a monetary payment to a person for the use of the 'razor', though the CEV does translate in that way, 'CEVUK - The Lord will pay the king of Assyria to bring a razor from across the River Euphrates and shave your head and every hair on your body, including your beard.' The TEV translates in a similar manner, 'the Lord will hire a barber from across the Euphrates'. The CEV and TEV are not recommended models. Because of the difficulty of understanding to whom the payment is made, some translations choose not to make explicit the the fact that God 'hired' the razor.  For instance, in the Tok Pisin translation (PNG), God simply 'gets' the king of Assyria and uses him like a razor.  If the metaphor 'hired razor' is not understand properly, then an approach similar to that used in Tok Pisin is recommended.
\trans hired
\ref 7!'!4&"
\paradigc

\heb שׂכר
\level1 1
\strong 7936, 7937
\pos v
\gloss to hire
\freq 20
\sub מַשְׂכֹּרֶת, שֶׂכֶר, שָׂכָר, שָׂכִיר
\intro 1. There is only one meaning of |hשׂכר|h* 'to hire'. 2. In the Qal the person who gives money in order to obtain service is in focus. 3. In the Niphal and Hithpael, the person being hired is the one who is in focus. This person is the one who initiates an activitity so that he is hired by another person. 4. This term is often associated with the hiring of mercenaries.
\comp There is only one meaning of  |hשׂכר |h* 'to hire'.
\level2 1
\meaning To give someone money or other valued items for the purpose of attaining the service of that person
\frame Commercial Transaction: hire
\stem Qal, Niphal, Hithpael
\eventfr Agent: human Patient: human Instrument (Pay): money or other valued items
\level3 a
\context To give someone money or other valued items for the purpose of attaining the service of that person
\eventfrc Agent: human Patient: human Instrument (Pay): money or other valued items

\heb שָׂכָר
\level1 1
\strong 7939, 7940
\pos ncm
\gloss wages
\freq 30
\sub שׂכר
\intro 1. |hשָׂכָר |h*'wages' is used to refer to the money or other valuable items that are given as payment for one's work or service. 2. The wages may be given to either the person or to the owner of the hired entity (either an animate or inanimate item). 3. When God is the agent giving the   "wages",  |hשָׂכָר |h* is a metaphorical extension that refers to a reward, i.e. a positive benefit that is not earned. 4. |h שָׂכָר |h* is also a proper name.
\comp Meaning 1 is a literal payment given in exchange for a person's work or the service of a hired animal or thing. Meaning 2 is a metaphorical extension of meaning 1 that refers to a variety of benefits that are received from God. Meaning 3 is a proper noun.
\level2 1
\meaning Money or other valuable items given as payment for one's work or service
\frame Commercial Transaction: hire
\level3 a
\context Money or other valuable items given as payment for one's work or service
\keyref %!8!/#!
\keyverse You shall pay them their |bwages |b*daily before sunset, because they are poor and their livelihood depends on them; otherwise they might cry to the Lord against you, and you would incur guilt.  (NRS)
\eval 'Wages' is a good translation of |hשָׂכָר|h* in this verse. Most English translations examined used this translation though some use the term 'hire' or simply the verbal expression 'to pay'.
\trans wages
\ref !!>!<#! !!>!@/! !!>!A&" !!?!(%! !!?!(-! "!"!)+" "!6!.(" $!2!?&" %!/!2)! %!8!/#! +!%!4)" 5!$!)&! :!=!2-" :!=!33! F!(!*%! F!+!,&! F!+!,*" G!#!%,!
\paradigc 
\level3 b
\context Money given to the owner of an animal for the hiring of its services
\domainc Animal Husbandry
\keyref F!(!*)"
\keyverse For before that time, the earnings of men were nil, and |bprofits |b*from beasts were nothing. It was not safe to go about one’s business on account of enemies; and I set all men against one another. (NJPS)
\eval Several translations (NRS, NIV, RSV) maintain the same translation of |hשָׂכָר |h*for both humans and animals, i. e., 'wages'.  Wages for animals, however, are generally given to the owner of the animal. Because of this, several English translations differentiate between the two, e.g., 'men were not paid their wages and nothing was paid for the animals either' (NJB) and the NJPS above. Other translations shift to a more generic terminology that can be used for both people and animals, e.g., 'neither people nor animals were rewarded for their work' (CEV) and 'no one could afford to hire either men or animals' (TEV). If a language differentiates between wages for animals and people, translators are encouraged to follow the model in NJPS or NJB.
\trans money (gained by hiring out the services of an animal); profit
\ref F!(!*)"

\level3 c

\domainc Sailing

\eval Most translations use 'fare', which is a good translation of this usage of the word |hשָׂכָר |h*'wages'. CEV uses 'ticket', which is also an acceptable translation.
\trans fare
\ref @!!!#.!

\level2 2

\frame Reward
\syntagc Most lexicons treat the 'wages' received from God as being different from a 'real wage'. While this can be justified on the basis of the connection of these terms with different domains, such as Salvation and Prosperity, it is also possible that the analysis is affected by theology, i.e., the belief that works are not directly connected with salavation and other positive aspects of life.
\level3 a
\context Reward; Something given to a person as a reward that may or may not be tied to any behavior that merits a reward (metaphorical extension of Meaning 1)

\eval 'Reward' is a good translation of this meaning of the noun |hשָׂכָר|h*. All English translations use either 'reward' or a verbal transaltion, 'to reward', for this noun.

\heb שׁבר
\level1 1
\strong 7665, 7666
\pos v
\gloss to buy (grain)
\freq 169
\sub מַשְׁבֵּר ,מִשְׁבָּר ,שָׁבוּר ,שֶׁבֶר ,שִׁבָּרוֹן ,שְׁבָרִים
\intro 1.  The verb |hשׁבר|h* ‘to buy (grain)’ generally refers to a commercial transaction that involves the purchase of grain and other food items from another human for a price. 2. In the Hiphil,  the verb |hשׁבר|h* ‘to buy (grain)’ refers to a commercial transaction that involves the sale of grain to another human for a price. 3. The verb |hשׁבר|h* is, however, more frequently used to refer to a variety of other meanings such as "to shatter; to break, etc."
\comp Meaning 1.  Commercial transaction as seen from the perspective of the buyer Meaning 2. Commercial transaction as seen from the perspective of the seller
\dfnotes Unlike other terms in the Commerce Domain, |hשׁבר|h*  'to buy (grain)' seems to be limited to the sale/purchase of grain and food, when the patient is specified.
\level2 1
\meaning To buy (grain); To acquire ownership of grain and other food items in exchange for money or other items considered to be equal in value
\frame Commercial Transaction
\stem Qal,
\eventfr Agent (Buyer): 	            human Patient (Item): 		grain Source (Seller): 	human Instrument (Price):	silver, gold, or other valued item
\level3 a
\context To buy (grain); To acquire ownership of grain and other food items in exchange for money or other items considered to be equal in value
\keyref !!K!"-!
\keyverse And when they had eaten up the grain that they had brought from Egypt, their father said to them, “Go again, |bbuy |b*us a little more food.” (NRS)
\eval All translations use the term 'buy', which is an acceptable translation.

\level2 2
\meaning To sell (grain); To transfer ownership of grain and other food items to another human in exchange for money or other items considered to be equal in value
\frame Commercial Transaction
\stem Qal, Hiphil
\gram There is only one occurrence of this meaning with the Qal (Gen 41:56).
\eventfr Agent (Buyer): 	            human Patient (Item): 		grain Source (Seller): 	human Instrument (Price):	silver, gold, or other valued item
\level3 a
\context To sell (grain); To transfer ownership of grain and other food items to another human in exchange for money or other items considered to be equal in value
\keyref %!"!<#!
\keyverse You shall |bsell |b*me food for money, so that I may eat, and supply me water for money, so that I may drink. Only allow me to pass through on foot-- (NRS)
\eval Many English translations translate |hשׁבר |h*with 'to sell' (NRS, RSV, NIV, CEV). Other English translations shift the focus from the perspective of the seller to the perspective of the buyer and use the translation 'to pay for' ( REB, TEV) or 'to supply for money' (NJPS). This may be due to the social setting and the fact that the use of a term from the seller's perspective may seem unnatural when used by the buyer because it sounds too demanding. CEV makes it sound less like a demand by saying, 'You can even sell us ...'. NJB uses a similar strategy by saying 'I shall eat and pay for the food you choose to sell me'.  The translator is encouraged to compare the terminology used in Deuteronomy 2.28 and 2.6.  The reaction of the king to the request in Deuteronomy 2.28 suggests that the request may have been viewed as a demand rather than as a request, but this may have been due to other factors in the social setting and not due to the choice of terminology in the request.  The use of |hנתן|h* 'give' for the sale of water is similar to terminology used for the negotiation of a commercial transaction in Genesis 23. Because of this it is best to translate the verse as a polite request and not as a demand if the receptor language distinguishes between these two types of speech.

\level3 b

\keyref 3!S!3$!
\keyverse True sacrifice to God is a contrite spirit; God, You will not despise a contrite and crushed heart. (NJPS)
\eval Most English translations have chosen a literal translation 'broken spirit' (NRS, RSV, NIV, ). The NJPS translation is better in that it has tried to actually translate the term, i.e., a contrite spirit.  Because there is only one occurrence of this phrase in the Hebrew Bible, it is almost impossible to determine the exact meaning.  The word  'spirit' is used to refer to one's character. The TH recommends the use of the term 'humble', which is the meaning of the idiom 'broken spirit' in English.  In Hebrew, however, there are other idioms that clearly refer to humility, e.g.   'lowly spirit'. Because of the context in Psalms 51.19, i.e., sacrifices that generally refer to the removal of sin or the affects of sin, it is best to choose a translation that will fit with the attitude or character of a person who is seeking forgiveness, e.g. a contrite attitude.
\trans to be contrite; to be repentant

\heb תקע
\strong 8628
\pos v
\gloss to blow (a trumpet)
\freq 70
\sub תֵּקַע, תֹּקְעִים, תָּקוֹע
\intro 1. The verb |hתקע|h* most frequently refers to the blowing of a musical instrument (trumpet or ram's horn) to produce a sound. 2. The verb |hתקע |h*may be used in a more generic sense to refer to the wind blowing an item in a certain direction. 3. The verb |hתקע|h* may also be used to refer to the driving/pounding of a stake into another item for the purpose of fastening an item in place.  This may be used both for the overall task of setting up a tent and for the fastening of one item to another item, i.e., driving a stake through someone's head to fasten it to the ground. 4. Finally the verb |hתקע |h*may be used for the clapping of hands. Clapping of hands may be used to signify joy or to signify that one will keep a promise to repay the debts of another.
\comp Meaning 1. The event of blowing a musical instrument as done by a human Meaning 2. The event of the wind blowing an item Meaning 3. The event of driving one item into another for the purpose of fastening an item to a specific place Meaning 4. The event of setting up a tent by driving stakes into the groundMeaning 5. The event of clapping hands to signify that one will keep his promises Meaning 6. The event of clapping hands to signify joy
\level2 1
\meaning Other meanings: To blow (a trumpet)
\level3 a
\context Other meanings: To blow (a trumpet)
\ref $!*!#!" $!*!$"! $!*!%!" $!*!&!" $!*!&)! $!*!'#! $!*!($! $!*!*&" &!&!$0! &!&!(." &!&!)%! &!&!)&! &!&!)-" &!&!-," &!&!-8" &!&!0$! &!&!4#" '!#!;#" '!&!B%" '!'!2!" '!'!2&" '!'!3." '!'!4!" \level2 5
\meaning To clap (hands as a guarantee that one will repay the debts of another)
\frame Commercial Transaction: loan
\stem Qal, Niphal
\eventfr Agent: human Patient: hands Benefactive (Debtor): human
\syntagc This could be viewed as a metaphorical extension of meaning 6 without the physical event, but it is likely that the physical act was performed as a part of the process of guaranteeing that something would happen.
\level3 a
\context To clap (hands as a guarantee that one will repay the debts of another)
\eventfrc Agent: human, God Patient: (hands) Beneficiary: human
\keyref 4!1!2#!
\keyverse A man lacking in judgment |bstrikes hands in pledge|b* and puts up security for his neighbor. (NIV)
\eval The NIV retains both the physical action and the meaning of the physical action in their translation. If the physical action of clapping hands has a different meaning in the receptor language and the inclusion of the physical act causes confusion, then it would be best to leave out the physical action and focus on the meaning of the action in the translation. A model of this approach to the translation of the idiom 'clapping of hands' can be seen in the following translations:  'to give a pledge' (NRS), 'gives a guarantee' (REB), and 'offers guarantees' (NJB). The NJV's translation 'gives his hand' should probably not be followed because it is ambiguous as to the exact meaning of the action of giving a hand. TEV and CEV both collapse the parallelism in the verse and use a phrase to translate both terms from the Economic domain. This approach should only be used if there are no other options due to lack of vocabulary for economic activities in the receptor language.

\heb כהן
\strong 3547
\pos v
\gloss to serve as priest
\freq 23
\sub כֹּהֵן, כְּהֻנָּה
\intro 1. This word most commonly refers to the service of priests of the order of Aaron in the tabernacle and the temple.2. It is used once of the service of Levites at local shrines.3. In a few cases the focus is on adornment as a priest.4. Once the word is used of a bridegroom dressing himself like a priest.5. Once this word is used metaphorically of Israel's special relationship with God.
\hebraist This verb is used only in the Piel stem. It is generally considered to be derived from the noun |hכֹּהֵן|h*. Alternatively, the noun may be understood as the participle of the otherwise unknown Qal form of the verb.
\comp |h|h*1 Frame: Priesthood2 Metaphorical extension of Meaning 1; Frame: Service of God3 Metaphorical extension of Meaning 1; Frame: Adornment4 Metaphorical extension of Meaning 1; Frame: Special relationship
\dfnotes See the notes under |hכֹּהֵן|h*.
\level2 1
\meaning To serve as priest of God, regularly to offer sacrifices to God as an intermediary between people and God

\stem Piel

\level3 a
\context To serve as priest of the order of Aaron
\domainc Tabernacle; Temple; Special clothing; Special head covering; High Priest
\eventfrc Agent          = subject:                                human  = Aaron or descendantRecipient    = |hלְ|h* or implicit (or in context):  GodBeneficiary  = implicit:                                human  = Israelite
\syntagcc The focus in Exodus 31:10; 35:19; 39:41 is not on priestly activities or on God as beneficiary, but on proper adornment. In Exodus 28:3, 4, 41; 40:13 this focus is combined with a wider focus on priestly activities. In each case the garments to be worn are described in the immediate context.

\keyverse The Levites abandoned their common land and their own property and moved to Judah and Jerusalem, because Jeroboam and his successors would not let them |bserve|b* the LORD. (KTBH)
\eval Most translations read 'serve as priests' or similar. But the passage distinguishes priests from Levites, and this verse seems to refer to the latter. If so, the verb |hכהן|h* must refer to their service as Levites rather than as priests. An alternative interpretation would be to understand vv.13-14 as chiastic, so that v.14a refers to Levites and v.14b refers to priests.

\ver 0
\dat 2004-04-02 peterk

\heb כֹּהֵן
\strong 3548, 3549 (Aramaic)
\alt כָּהֵן (Aramaic)
\pos ncm
\gloss priest
\freq 758
\sub כהן
\intro 1. This word refers primarily to priests serving God in the Tabernacle and the Temple.2.  It is also used for other priests of God and for priests of pagan gods.3.  In a few cases it refers to a special relationship with God.4.  In three places where the meaning is sometimes understood as 'royal adviser', KTBH prefers to understand a reference to an unofficial priesthood.
\tnotes 1. The role of a |hכֹּהֵן|h* is very different from that of the leaders of Christian congregations (see 'New Testament' below). Therefore it is inappropriate to translate this word with a term which is used primarily for Christian leaders.2. Uses 1b and 1d refer to the single High Priest, who had special functions. But this role was not clearly defined during the early monarchy period. In translation, a special word or phrase for 'High Priest' may be used when the Hebrew word is qualified by |hגָּדֹול|h* or |hרֹאשׁ|h*. When the Hebrew is simply |hכֹּהֵן|h* but the reference is to someone elsewhere identified as High Priest, the word or phrase for 'High Priest' may be appropriate, but other prominent named priests should not be assumed to be the High Priest.3. The leaders (plural) of the priests mentioned at 2 Chronicles 36:14 and Ezra 8:24, 29; 10:5 should be distinguished from the singular High Priest; a rendering like 'chief priests' may be misleading.4. |hכֹּהֵן|h*, and Aramaic |hכָּהֵן|h*, is related to the Arabic word |ikāhin|i*, which has been borrowed into other languages. The meaning in Arabic is rather different from the Hebrew, more like 'diviner', but in some other languages this word is used in the sense 'priest' and so may be an appropriate rendering of |hכֹּהֵן|h*.
\hebraist The noun |hכֹּהֵן|h* may be understood as the participle of the otherwise unknown Qal form of the verb |hכהן|h*.
\nt The Greek equivalents of |hכֹּהֵן|h*, |gἱερεύς|g*, also |gἀρχιερεύς|g* 'High Priest, chief priest', are used in the New Testament almost entirely of Jewish priests, and only once of pagan priests (Acts 14:13). They are used of Jesus Christ only in Hebrews. They are also used three times in Revelation (1:6; 5:10; 20:6; cf. |gἱεράτευμα|g* 'priesthood' 1 Peter 2:5,9) of all Christian believers; these uses allude to Exodus 19:6, and so relate to Meaning 3 below and bring out the idea of a priest as an intermediary between people and God but with no sacrificial function. But they are nowhere used as a specific term for Christian leaders, who did not offer sacrifices; their relationship with other Christians should not be compared to that of Jewish priests with other Jews.
\comp 1 Frame: Priesthood; Core domain: Worship of God    1a Domains: Tabernacle; Temple; Agent: any descendant of Aaron    1b Domains: Tabernacle; Temple; High Priest; Agent: Aaron or most senior descendant    1c Domains: Tabernacle; Temple; Agent: Aaron or descendant, named    1d Domains: Tabernacle; Temple; High Priest; Agent: Aaron or most senior descendant, named    1e Domains: Local shrines; Northern kingdom of Israel    1f Domain: Non-Israelite people    1g Domain: Prophetic promise2 Frame: Priesthood; Core domain: Worship of pagan gods    2a Domain: Israelites; Recipient: Baal    2b Domain: Non-Israelite people3 Metaphorical extension of Meaning 1; Frame: Special relationship    3a Domain: Israelites    3b Domain: Non-Israelite people
\dfnotes |hכֹּהֵן|h* is a cultic term. The great majority of its uses refer to those charged with leading worship in the Tabernacle and the Temple. A smaller number relate to other worship of God and to worship of pagan gods. Just a few times the word is used to indicate a special relationship with God.The analysis of this word and of its cognates is based on an understanding that in Hebrew thinking there were several distinct domains of worship. These are distinguished by different settings, regulations, associations, and sequences of events, and by the biblical authors' different attitudes towards them. Therefore a distinction is made here between Worship in the Tabernacle and the Temple, Worship at local shrines (i.e. authorised worship led by Levites), Unofficial Israelite worship of God (including worship not authorised by the Law of Moses), Non-Israelite worship of God, and Worship of pagan gods. The precise distinctions between these domains are unclear, and sometimes depend on the different perspectives and evaluations of different biblical authors.The main duties of a priest in the Tabernacle and the Temple were to offer sacrifices. Subsidiary roles included teaching and prayer, and seeking guidance from God. Certain sacrifices could be offered only by the single High Priest. Priests at local shrines also offered some sacrifices, although this is not permitted by the law of Moses; this is why Josiah closed down the local shrines (2 Kings 23:8, 9). The main roles of non-Israelite priests also seem to have been sacrificial.
\level2 1
\meaning Priest of God, one who offers sacrifices to God as an intermediary between people and God
\frame Priesthood
\domain Sacrifice; Service of God; Hereditary role
\eventfr Event:                               to sacrificeAgent          = headword:  human        = priestRecipient:                         GodBeneficiary:                      human        = worshipper
\level3 a
\context Priest of the order of Aaron (generic), one who offers sacrifices to God in the Tabernacle or Temple as an intermediary between Israelite people and God
\domainc Tabernacle; Temple; Instruction
\eventfrc Event           = mostly implicit or in wider context:  to sacrificeAgent           = headword:                                      human        = any descendant of AaronRecipient     = mostly implicit or in wider context:  GodBeneficiary  = mostly implicit or in wider context:  human        = Israelite

\not (a) Aramaic references.
\not (b) In this verse |hכֹּהֵן|h* is used in parallel with |hכֹּמֶר|h*, in a way suggesting that two different groups are referred to. The latter word surely refers to the priests of Baal, as in 2 Kings 23:5, and so it seems likely that the former refers to apostate priests of the Temple of God, cf. 3:4. Therefore this occurrence of |hכֹּהֵן|h* is listed under this contextual use.
\not (c) Aramaic |hעֲבִידָה|h*.
\not (d) The references listed here are only those in which the priests relate to the instruction or law as guardians or instructors.
\not (e) The High Priest is only sometimes distinguished by the qualifying terms |hמָשִׁיחַ, גָּדֹול, רֹאשׁ|h*. More often he is distinguished from other priests by longer expressions or from the context. Hilkiah seems to have been the first priest given the title |hהַכֹּהֵן הַגָּדֹול|h*, but some earlier priests were known as |hכֹּהֵן הָרֹאשׁ|h*.
\not (f) These named priests are not specifically called High Priest, although some of them seem to have served as such. In the early monarchy period there does not seem to have been a single High Priest; during David's reign Zadok and Abiathar seem to have shared the leading role.Included here are the following references to priests by name:|hאֶלְעָזָר |h*Eleazar (before the death of Aaron): NUM 3:32; 4:16; 17:2,4; 19:3,4|hאִיתָמָר |h*Ithamar: EXO 38:21; NUM 4:28,33; 7:8|hפִּינְחָס |h*Phinehas, son of Eleazar: NUM 25:7,11; 31:6; JOS 22:13,30,31,32|hעֵלִי |h*Eli: 1SA 1:9; 2:11; 14:3|hחָפְנִי |h*Hophni and |hפִּינְחָס |h*Phinehas: 1SA 1:3|hאֲחִימֶלֶךְ |h*Ahimelech: 1SA 21:2,3; 22:11; 2SA 8:17; 1CH 18:16|hאֶבְיָתָר |h*Abiathar: 1SA 23:9; 30:7; 2SA 15:35,35; 17:15; 19:12; 20:25; 1KI 1:7,19,25,42; 2:22,26,27; 4:4; 1CH 15:11|hצָדֹוק |h*Zadok: 2SA 8:17; 15:27,35,35; 17:15; 19:12; 20:25; 1KI 1:8,26,32,34,38,39,44,45; 2:35; 4:4; 1CH 15:11; 16:39; 18:16; 24:6; 29:22|hעֲזַרְיָהוּ |h*Azariah: 1KI 4:2|hיְהֹויָדָע |h*Jehoiada: 2KI 11:9,9,15; 12:3,8,10; 2CH 22:11; 23:8,8,9,14; 24:2,20,25; and an earlier Jehoiada: 1CH 27:5; and a later Jehoiada: JER 21:26|hאוּרִיָּה |h*Uriah: 2KI 16:10,11,11,15,16|hפַּשְׁחוּר |h*Pashhur: JER 20:1|hצְפַנְיָהוּ |h*Zephaniah: 2KI 25:18; JER 21:1,25,29; 37:3; 52:24|hיְהֶזְקֵאל|h* Ezekiel: EZK 1:3|hעֶזְרָא  |h*Ezra: EZR 7:11,12,21; 10:10,16; NEH 8:2,9; 12:26|hמְרֵמֹות |h*Meremoth: EZR 8:33
\not (g) |hכֹּהֵן|h* and |hסֹופֵר|h* are used in parallel only of Ezra. In the Aramaic references Ezra 7:12,21 the parallel is between |hכָּהֵן|h* and |hסָפֵר|h*.
\not (h) Included here are the following references to High Priests by name:|hאַהֲרֹן |h*Aaron: EXO 31:10; 35:19; 39:41; LEV 1:7; 7:34; 13:2; 21:21; NUM 3:6; 18:28; 26:64; 33:38; JOS 21:4,13; EZR 7:5|hאֶלְעָזָר |h*Eleazar (after the death of Aaron): 26:1,3,63; 27:2,19,21,22; 31:12,13,21,26,29,31,41,51,54; 32:2,28; 34:17; JOS 14:1; 17:4; 19:51; 21:1|hאֲמַרְיָהוּ |h*Amariah: 2CH 19:11|hעֲזַרְיָהוּ |h*Azariah: 2CH 26:17,20; and another Azariah: 2CH 31:10|hחִלְקִיָּהוּ, חִלְקִיָּה |h*Hilkiah: 2KI 22:4,8,10,12,14; 23:4,24; 2CH 34:9,14,18|hשְׂרָיָה |h*Seraiah: 2KI 25:18; JER 52:24|hאֶלְיָשִׁיב |h*Eliashib: NEH 3:1,20; 13:4,28|hיְהֹושֻׁעַ |h*Joshua: HAG 1:1,12,14; 2:2,4; ZEC 3:1,8; 6:11
\not (i) These references seem to be to a priesthood in Israel predating and separate from the order of Aaron - including in Psalm 99:6 where Moses as well as Aaron is named as a priest.
\not (j) 2 Samuel 8:18 seems to state clearly that David's sons were priests; compare also 2 Samuel 20:26 and 1 Kings 4:5. But this contradicts the law of Moses which allows only descendants of Aaron to be priests. The Levitical priests Zadok and Ahimelech are listed in verse 17. So it is often suggested that here |hכֹּהֵן|h* means not 'priest' but 'royal adviser', as in NIV; compare LXX |gαὐλάρχης |g*'chief of the court'. However, the law of Moses was not obeyed in every detail during the time of David, and the text should not be adjusted to make it appear that it was.In the parallel passage, 1 Chronicles 18:17, the text reads, in place of 'priests', literally 'first to the hand of the king'. It seems probable that the text of 2 Samuel was changed in 1 Chronicles as part of its presentation of David as an ideal king who observed the law of Moses. The LXX translator may have had similar reasons for adjusting the text.In the light of Psalm 110:4, it is possible that David appointed his sons to the priesthood of God Most High who was worshipped in pre-Israelite Jerusalem, i.e. as successors of Melchizedek. (In the light of the New Testament, these sons of David would also be precursors of the greater Son of David who was also called a priest of the order of Melchizedek, Hebrews 6:20.)
\not (k) The text of Hosea 4:4 is commonly emended e.g. to 'for with you is my contention, O priest' (RSV), which implies that the addressee Israel is here being referred to as a priest. But the text as written makes sense, and is accurately translated 'for your people are like those who bring charges against a priest' (NIV). The meaning of this in context is obscure, but the priest referred to here, as elsewhere in Hosea (cf. Amos 7:10 where this is clear), is presumably one of the non-Aaronic priests of the northern kingdom of Israel.
\not (l) In view of v.18 and 1 Kings 16:31, 32, it is assumed that the priests mentioned in 2 Kings 10:11 are priests of Baal, not non-Aaronic priests of God. But it is possible that priests of God are also being referred to here.
\ver 0
\dat 2004-04-02 peterk

\heb כְּהֻנָּה

\sub כהן
\intro 1. This word refers to the status and duties of the priests serving in the Tabernacle and the Temple.2. Once the focus is on priesthood as a source of income.3. Once the word is used as a collective term for "priests".4. Once it refers to the service of the Levites.

\dfnotes See the notes under |hכֹּהֵן|h*. Almost all the uses of |hכְּהֻנָּה|h* refer to worship in the Tabernacle and the Temple. In 1 Samuel 2:36 the focus is on the priestly office as employment and a source of income.

\heb כֹּמֶר
\strong 3649
\pos ncm
\gloss idol-priest
\freq 3
\sub כמר
\intro This pejorative word is used three times, only of priests of pagan idolatrous worship by Israelites.
\tnotes |hכֹּמֶר|h* is a pejorative term. It is used in modern Hebrew of Christian ministers, but translators are not advised to translate this word with a word used for Christian ministers.
\hebraist The verb |hכמר|h* is formally a cognate of this noun, but it is not a key term.
\dfnotes This word is used only of priests of pagan gods and only when this worship is by Israelites; contrast |hכֹּהֵן|h* which is used rather rarely in such a context, only of the priests of Baal in the northern kingdom in the time of Ahab and Jehu. It would appear that |hכֹּמֶר|h* is a pejorative word, used of those who were not recognised as proper priests, at least by the theologically orthodox in Judah.In Zephaniah 1:4 the words |hכֹּמֶר|h* and |hכֹּהֵן|h* are used in parallel, apparently of two separate groups.
\level2 1
\meaning Priest of pagan gods, one who offers sacrifices to pagan gods as an intermediary between people and pagan gods
\frame Priesthood
\domain Worship of pagan gods
\eventfr Agent:               humanRecipient:         pagan godBeneficiary:      humanCausing agent: human
\level3 a
\context Priest of pagan gods, one who offers sacrifices to pagan gods as an intermediary between Israelite people and pagan gods
\domainc Israelites
\eventfrc Agent                 = headword:                                humanRecipient           = |hלְ|h* or genitive or implicit:            pagan godBeneficiary        = implicit:                                      humanCausing agent  = implicit or subject of sentence:  human
\keyref ,!7!%"#
\keyverse And he [Josiah] deposed the |bidolatrous priests|b* whom the kings of Judah had ordained to burn incense in the high places at the cities of Judah and round about Jerusalem; those also who burned incense to Baal, to the sun, and the moon, and the constellations, and all the host of the heavens. (RSV)

\colc כֹּהֵן
\pgloss priest
\refsc D!!!$-"
\paradigc Associated
\colc קטר
\pgloss to burn (sacrifice)
\refsc ,!7!%"#

\heb לֵוִי
\strong 3878, 3879 (Aramaic), 3881
\alt לֵוָי (Aramaic)
\pos np
\gloss Levi, Levite
\freq 354
\intro 1. The most basic use of this word refers to Levi son of Jacob. This is not a key term.2. The word commonly refers to the tribe of which Levi is the ancestor. This is also not a key term.3. Most commonly, the word is used of the Levites in their role of serving in the Tabernacle and the Temple.4. In a few cases the word is used to describe the priests of the order of Aaron, who were a part of the Levites.
\hebraist Technically, there are two homonyms here: the personal name, which is Meanings 1 to 3 (Strong's number 3878), and the derived gentilic form, which is Meanings 4 to 6 (Strong's number 3881, also all of the Aramaic references). But, as is regular with personal names ending in |h־ִי|h*, the gentilic suffix |h־ִי|h* is assimilated, so that the form of the gentilic is identical to that of the personal name. As commonly happens, the gentilic may be used either as a noun (Meanings 4 and 5) or as an adjective (Meaning 6).
\comp 1 Personal name    1a Frame: Individual    1b Contextual domain: Community    1c Contextual domains: Service of God; Tabernacle; Temple2 Metaphorical extension of Use 1b: Collective name; Frame: Community3 Metaphorical extension of Use 1c: Collective name; Frame: Service of God4 Gentilic noun; Frame: Community5 Gentilic noun; Frame: Service of God6 Gentilic adjective; Frame: Service of God
\level2 1
\meaning Levi, third son of Jacob
\frame Individual
\level3 a
\context Levi, third son of Jacob, as an individual
\syntagcc This use is not a key term and so is not further analysed by KTBH.

\dat 2004-04-02 peterk

\heb נְבָלָה
\strong 05039
\pos ncf
\gloss sacrilege
\freq 13
\sub נבל
\intro This rare noun is normally used only when the most shocking crimes have been committed. For instance, four of its 13 occurrences are found in the story of the atrocity in Gibeah (Judges 19-20). It indicates a flagrant violation of one of the most revered standards of morality - the kind of shocking crime that provokes public outrage and God’s indignation. In biblical usage the noun seems to elicit two lexical meanings, but the first has two contextual uses: 1. Sacrilege1.1 A scandalous act (10 x)1.2 Sacrilegious speech against God (3 x: Isa 9:17; 32:6; Jer 29:23)2. Penalty for sacrilegious speech against God (Job 42:8)1.1 The first contextual use refers either to gross sexual sin or to grave violations of treaty agreements (according to the available corpus). It commonly forms part of a fixed expression - ‘to commit a crime that causes a scandal among the people of Israel’. This fixed expression points to the fact that such scandalous offences outrage the entire nation. People closest to the victims are especially angered, as is God. Such offences break a moral or religious code that the Israelites hold to be sacrosanct (untouchable). They bring great shame to the community, especially to those most closely associated with the doer and the victim. They call for the immediate death of those who do them, and sometimes of people closely associated with them. The people who do such things in the Bible are always destroyed. 1.2 The second contextual use refers specifically to scandalous lies about God’s character or about God’s dealings with humans, sometimes while also falsely claiming to be speaking God’s message with God’s authority. In these cases, God is more directly affected (dishonoured) as the victim, because his true nature is misrepresented when people speak incorrectly about him or about his moral standards. 2. There is one case of the term referring not to the offence itself, nor to the scandal it causes, but to the penalty that is warranted by such a serious offence. Translators should find a strongly-worded expression that emphasises both the enormity of the offence (to God and to the entire community) and the serious consequences it brings (to the doer and to the community as a whole). Only very bad people commit such very bad crimes. They are not minor mistakes that anyone might make. They are deliberate and flagrant violations that shock and horrify respectable society. For the second contextual use, translators might consider explicitly stating the nature of the crime and the identity of the victim, such as ‘shocking lies about God’. The second lexical meaning might require an expansive translation, such as ‘punish you as severely as your wrong talk about God deserves’ (Job 42:8).More details about understanding and translating the following instances are available:Deu 22:21; Jos 7:15; Jdg 19:23, 24; 20:6, 10; 1Sa 25:25; 2Sa 13:12; Job 42:8; Isa 9:17; Isa 32:6; Jer 29:23
\altint LexiconsBDB defines this noun as ‘disgraceful folly (wanton deed)’, but makes God the agent in Job 42:8 (‘i.e. deal out to you disgrace, expose you and punish you as ~ylib'n>, because of your utterances about me’). But see Is ‘folly’ an adequate translation equivalent? and Who is the agent in Job 42:8?HALOT also gives two meanings: ‘stupidity, folly’ (1Sa 25:25; Job 42:8) and ‘wilful sin, sacrilege’. HALOT’s analysis of this word is a significant improvement on BDB’s, but it still retains the idea of folly in two instances. Yet there is nothing in the context of either of these that would necessarily prevent the scandal frame from being evoked, albeit with some elements behaving as weaker implicatures. See Domain and frame relations and Is ‘folly’ adequate in 1 Samuel 25:25?.0.0.0.1	NIDOTTE and Phillips 1975The NIDOTTE entry relies heavily on Phillips (1975:237-242), who divides the biblical usages into three groups, based on the kind of offence referred to: 1. Those that refer to ‘sexual acts’: Genesis 34:7; Deuteronomy 22:21; Judges 19:23, 24; 20:6, 10; 2 Samuel 13:12; Jeremiah 29:23.2. Those that pertain to ‘nonsexual acts’: Joshua 7:15 and 1 Samuel 25:25.3. Those that apply to ‘the spoken word’ (particularly when misrepresenting God or his moral standards): Job 42:8; Isaiah 9:17; 32:6; Jeremiah 29:23.Phillips (1975: 238) claims that the word can ‘best be rendered as ‘an act of crass disorder or unruliness’ or ‘acting in an utterly disorderly or unruly fashion’.’ This seems rather weak when we consider the extreme reactions of recoil and horror that are associated with the use of the word. The radical response to Achan’s felony (Jos 7) and to the crime against the Levite (Jdg 19-20) could hardly be warranted if the crime in each case was no more than a ‘disorderly and unruly action in breaking a custom’.Perhaps Philips paid too much attention to the case of Nabal’s custom-breaking miserliness (1Sa 25:25). But this an unusual usage of the word, which can be accounted for by Abigail’s employment of two rhetorical devices: 1.	Wordplay: A wordplay is formed with Nabal’s name (lb'n' ‘unscrupulous scoundrel’).2.	Hyperbole: In order to appease David, Abigail overstates the enormity of the crime. She emphasises that David is in the right because her husband’s behaviour is so disgraceful that it evokes censure, reproach and even outrage. In a culture that values hospitality so highly, Nabal’s meanness is extremely shameful and, in this case, dangerous. It was not too much of an exaggeration to use this expression. But Nabal’s offence is still not on the same level of gravity as the other occasions when the word is used, such as Achan’s disobedience to the sacred ban. Evidence for this claim is found in David’s response (1Sa 25:39). If Nabal’s miserliness had been as serious as other ‘scandalous crimes’, then David’s vow to kill all the males in Nabal’s household (verse 22) would not have been entirely uncalled for. Yet David is relieved that God had kept him from doing this ‘wicked act’ (h['r', verse 39). By contrast, Achan commits such an outrageous violation that he infects all Israel. His defiance of the ban treaty is so contemptibly scandalous that it requires the most drastic measures to rid the nation of its contaminating effect. Not only is Achan destroyed but his whole household and all his possessions. Nabal, on the other hand, dies alone (1Sa 25:38). Despite the weaker implicatures involved in this case, the offences referred to by this expression are far more damaging and shocking than mere ‘disorderly conduct’ or ‘folly’. See Domain and frame relations and Is ‘folly’ adequate in 1 Samuel 25:25?.
\evalev A detailed investigation of some of the biblical usages follows, including discussions of Judges 19-20, 1 Samuel 25:25, Isaiah 9:17 and Job 42:8. Five issues are addressed: 1.	Evidence for extremity (Jdg 19-20)2.	Is ‘folly’ an adequate translation equivalent?3.	Translating 1 Samuel 25:254.	Understanding Isaiah 9:175.	Who is the agent in Job 42:8? God’s displeasure or the friends’ folly? 0.0.0.1	Evidence for extremity (Jdg 19-20)One of the most significant elements of meaning (domains) linked to the scandal frame is Extremity. This relates to almost every other aspect of meaning (domain) associated with the frame: not only to the extreme offensiveness of the crime (the enormity), but also to the reactions of others and the immoderate measures that it calls for. What is the evidence for this? How do we know that hl'b'n> (((‘shocking crime’) describes graver, more serious sins than its weaker or more generic synonyms?...
\dfnotes The sacrilege frame is similar to other types of sin frames. There are two main differences from the usages of most other evaluative terms for sin. The standards violated are worthy of greater respect and the effects are more extreme. 0.0.1	SynonymsOur focus term (hAlAbèn ‘scandalous crime’) is related to Hebrew words for sin, horror, disgrace, and outrage. For instance, HALOT defines hM'v; as ‘horrific, atrocious event (always referring to destruction following judgement)’. Both hAlAbèn ‘scandalous crime’  and hM'v; (‘atrocious destruction’) refer to events that cause horror. But, while hAlAbèn refers to horrific criminal acts, hM'v; (‘atrocious destruction’) refers to horrific acts of desolation, often as punishment for criminal acts. While hAlAbèn ‘scandalous crime’ refers to the kinds of events that call for swift judgment, hM'v; (‘atrocious destruction’) refers to the acts of judgment themselves that are called for by serious crimes. The causative relationships may be shown like this:hAlAbèn ‘scandalous crime’ > horror > judgment > hM'v; ‘atrocious destruction’ > more horror0.0.2	Contrasting Focus of SynonymsThe term hAlAbèn ‘scandalous crime’ perspectivises the extreme reaction of outrage to the sinful event. It refers to a crime that ought to cause public outrage and a drastic response. |bTerm 	Gloss		Focusajx	|b*		none[vp			intention of agent: deliberate (therefore, culpable)!w[				consequence for agent: culpability and punishmentlw[			agent: unexpected act by formerly righteous agent~va  			effect on agent: guilt @nx 			effect on agent: moral impurityhb'[eAT			effect on others: disgusthAlAbèn 			effect on others: outrageetc.
\level2 1
\meaning Scandalous crime: offensive and abhorrent conduct that violates one of the most sacred standards of morality, appals and outrages the public conscience, disgraces those associated with it, angers God, and normally calls for the immediate death of the perpetrator
\frame Sacrilege: scandal
\domain Sin; Outrage; Violation; Standards; Disgrace; Extremity; Wantonness; Reprehensibility; Death penalty
\eventfr 1. Combination with verb: ‘to do’ (|gעשׂה|g*) except for two cases with ‘to speak’ (|gדבר|g*).Exception: only the wordplay (‘scandal is with him’, 1Sa 25:25). 2. Agents (perpetrators): Only the most unscrupulous scoundrels. Terms used, including synonyms: lb'n" ‘scoundrel’ (3x); l[;Y:lib.-ynEB. ‘shameless rogues’ (3x);' ylKu ‘rascal’ (1x) false prophets (1x); people who had become @nEx' ‘immoral’ and [r;me  ‘evil’ (1x). 3. Targets (victims): Any human in Contextual use 1; only God in Contextual use 2. 4. Effects on participants: Public outrage. People responsible for the victims’ safety are especially angered, as is God. The agents and their close associates are tainted. In Contextual use 2, God is directly dishonoured because his true nature is misrepresented when people speak incorrectly about him or about his moral standards.5. Consequences (punishment): Such crimes call for the immediate death of the perpetrators, and sometimes of those closely associated with them. Only in one case (Job 42:8) are the perpetrators pardoned. The death penalty is decreed in the OT for almost all of the crimes referred to by this evaluative term.
\level3 a
\context Scandalous crime
\distc This is an act, as opposed to speech (cf. Lexical Meaning 2) Scandalous crime: the sin event is an act, the victim is human, and a consequence is public outrage.
\framec Sacrilege: scandal
\domainc Sin; Outrage; Violation; Standards; Disgrace; Extremity; Wantonness; Reprehensibility; Death penalty
\syntagcc 1. Fixed expression: ‘to commit a scandalous crime in Israel’     Meaning: ‘to commit such a shocking crime that it ought to outrage every law-abiding citizen throughout the whole nation of Israel’. It refers to the people of Israel, not to the land of Israel. (Cf. REB for Gen 34:7: ‘done what the Israelites hold to be an intolerable outrage’.)     Function: It emphasises two things about such crimes: (1) they offend the public conscience of the nation as a whole, causing a nation-wide outcry and (2) the whole nation is implicated in the crime and must be purged by getting rid of the perpetrators from among them (cf. Deu 22:21; Jos 7; Jdg 19-20). |nH|n*dden:}	More…    Variant forms: 2 Samuel 13:12 has two alternate forms of the fixed expression: (1) ‘Such a thing should not be done in Israel! Don’t cause such a scandal’. (2) The perpetrator is warned that he would become ‘a scandalous fool (|inabal|i*) in Israel’ (that is, the people of Israel would despise him as a scandalous fool).     Distribution: All occurrences of the first contextual use, except for the special case of hyperbolic paronomasia (1Sa 25:25). None of the second contextual use, except Jeremiah 29:23, where its double application refers not only to the lying words the false prophets spoke in God’s name, but also to their adulterous acts.2. Wordplay: ‘He lives up to his name. Scoundrel is his name, and scandal is never far from him’ (1Sa 25:25).     Meaning: This is an exceptional use of the noun. Nabal’s offence, though serious enough in a culture that values hospitality so highly, did not outrage the whole nation. The term seems somewhat excessive to describe his stinginess.     Function: But the exaggeration may be deliberate, serving to placate David’s anger, which is Abigail’s main purpose. Her wordplay on Nabal’s name also serves to appease David by making him feel that his anger is justified. 	More… on NIDOTTE and Phillips 19753. Combination with verb: ‘to do’ (hf[) except for two cases with ‘to speak’ (rbd).     Exception: only the wordplay (‘scandal is with him’, 1Sa 25:25). Participant Roles:4. Agents (perpetrators): Only the most immoral scoundrels. 5. Targets (victims): Any human. 6. Affected: God, all humans associated with the agents and targets. Comments:More on … Specific event being evaluated, Agents and Targets. |nH|n*dden:}7. Specific event being evaluated: The few occurrences found in the corpus may be classified according to the kinds of scandalous crime that they refer to: 1. Gross sexual sin (8 x) 1.1 Rape of important person’s daughter by unsuitable suitor (foreigner, Gen 34:7, or half-brother, 2Sa 13:12)1.2 Homosexual rape (Jdg 19:23, 24; 20:6, 10)1.3 Consenting pre-marital sex, of female, in her father’s home (Deu 22:21)1.4 Adultery with neighbours’ wives (Jer 29:23)2. Gross treaty violation (2 x)2.1 Theft of forbidden, devoted things placed under the ban (Jos 7:15)2.2 Non-payment of the customary tribute to a protector-a special case of hyperbolic paronomasia (1Sa 25:25)Agents (perpetrators): 1. Unsuitable suitors (a foreigner, Gen 34:7, or a half-brother, 2Sa 13:12) commit the rape of an unmarried virgin.2. The men of a town who mob together to commit homosexual rape (Jdg 19:23, 24; 20:6, 10).3. A young female who consents to pre-marital sex in her father’s home (Deu 22:21). The male accomplice seems to be irrelevant in the cultural context of the ancient Near East.4. False prophets (those who claim to speak in God’s name) commit adultery with their neighbours’ wives (Jer 29:23).Targets (victims): 1. The daughter of a patriarch (Gen 34:7) and of a king (2Sa 13:12).2. A Levite, a male guest of one of the town’s inhabitants (Jdg 19:23, 24; 20:6, 10).3. The bridegroom of the young female guilty of pre-marital sex is the victim. [Her father is also shamed because it took place in his own home while he was responsible for the purity of his daughter (Deu 22:21).] 4. Male neighbours, whose wives were willing (or unwilling) accomplices in the crime of adultery (Jer 29:23).5. God has been disobeyed in a serious way. The forbidden, devoted things that had been placed under the ban have been taken. All Israel suffers (Jos 7:15).6. David’s customary rights as Nabal’s protector have been spurned (1Sa 25:25).
\keyref %!6!5,!
\keyverse Then the elders are to bring the girl out to the entrance of her father's house and the men of her town must stone her to death. You must purge this evil from among you. After all, by prostituting herself while under her father's authority, her |boffence has caused a scandal|b* among the people of Israel! (MS)
\eval ‘…the men of the town will take the woman to the door of her father's house and stone her to death.This woman brought evil into your community by sleeping with someone before she got married, and you must get rid of that evil by killing her.’ (CEV)CEV renders the fixed expression as ‘brought evil into your community’. This conveys part of the significance of the fixed expression. But since this aspect is already represented elsewhere in the verse, it is better at this point to emphasise the extreme offensiveness of the crime - one that should cause an outrage throughout all Israel. Unlike other modern translations, however, the CEV unambiguously shows that the fixed expression properly refers to the people of Israel, not to the land of Israel.REB renders the fixed expression as ‘committed an outrage in Israel’ (cf. ESV). This accurately portrays the idea of scandal, as does a noun like ‘infamy’ (NJB and TOB). GW’s ‘godless act’ may not always evoke the idea of public scandal for all English speakers, but it is better than the following. Several other translations are only a little astray by employing adjectives such as ‘disgraceful’ (NIV, NRSV, NLT, NCV, EIN) or ‘shameful’ (TEV, NJPS). Those that use ‘folly’ (LXX, RSV, NASB) are far from signifying the horrific enormity of the offence. See Is ‘folly’ an adequate translation equivalent?
\lxx evpoi,hsen avfrosu,nhn evn ui`oi/j Israhl ‘has done a folly among the children of Israel’Comments:According to Liddell and Scott avfrosu,nh means ‘folly, thoughtlessness, senselessness’.  This rendering fails to get across the idea of scandal. That the fixed expression refers to the people of Israel, however, not to the land of Israel, is stated unambiguously here, unlike elsewhere in the Septuagint.
\ref !!B!'+! %!6!5,! &!'!/-! '!3!71! '!3!82! '!4!&+! '!4!*3! )!9!9.! *!-!,*! 8!=!7$!
\paradigl 1 Scandalous crime: offensive and abhorrent conduct that violates one of the most sacred standards of morality, appals and outrages the public conscience, disgraces those associated with it, angers God, and normally calls for the immediate death of the perpetrator
\level3 b
\context scandalous lies about God
\distc Some aspects of Job 42:8 (see Lexical meaning 2) are included here, because the penalty is caused by a scandalous sin of the same sort as this use. Sacrilegious speech against God: the sin event is a speech, the victim is God, and a consequence is divine outrage.
\framec scandal: blasphemy
\domainc Sin; Speech; Prophecy; Falsehood; Disgrace; Anger; Reprehensibility; Death penalty
\eventfrc Agents (perpetrators): 1.	An unscrupulous scoundrel (lAbAn, Isa 32:6) 2.	Job’s three friends (Job 42:8) 3.	False prophets (Jer 29:23) 4.	The degenerate Israelites in Isaiah’s time (Isa 9:17). Target (victim): God (Isa 32:6; Job 42:8; Jer 29:23; Isa 9:17).
\syntagcc 1. Collocations: The verb ‘to speak’ (rbd) is always somewhere in the verse. Combination with verb: With ‘to speak’ (rbd), where no action is involved, only scandalous speech (Isa 9:17; 32:6). With ‘to do’ (hf[) in Jeremiah 29:23, even though offensive speech is entailed, because there is scandalous action (adultery) as well. 2. Specific event being evaluated: Scandalous lies are being spread about God’s character or about God’s dealings with humans, sometimes while also falsely claiming to be speaking God’s message with God’s authority.
\keyref 7!)!0/!
\keyverse This is why YHWH shows no partiality to the young men         and no mercy even to the orphans and widows: because everyone is immoral and evil,         and every mouth speaks |bshocking lies about God|b*.    (MS)
\eval 'The LORD was angry with his people and kept punishing them, because they had turned against him. They were evil and spoke foolishly. That's why he did not have pity on their young people or on their widows and orphans.’ (CEV)Many English translations still use terms like ‘folly’ (KJV, NRSV, NAB), ‘foolishness’ (NASB, GW), ‘foolishly’ (CEV) and ‘madness’ (NJB). Exceptions include ‘impiety’ (REB, NJPS), ‘evil’ (TEV) and ‘lies’ (NLT and NCV). But the guilt of the Israelites in Isaiah’s time was far greater than foolish lies would cause. Their scandalous words were no mere slips of the tongue or insignificant blunders, made because they were ignorant and foolish. Such meanings might be signalled when English words like ‘folly’ are used. 	More on this verse.See Is ‘folly’ an adequate translation equivalent?
\lxx a;dikoj ‘evil, sinful; dishonest, unjust’ (Isa 9:17)Comments:Conveys part of the meaning in a general way, but still fails to elicit the scandalous proportions of the sin.
\ref 7!)!0/! 7!@!&#! 8!=!7$!
\paradigl 1
\level2 2
\meaning Penalty for scandalous crime
\distl The penalty for 1.2 (probably death): the outcome of the sin event.
\frame Sacrilege: penalty
\domain Death penalty; Sin; Speech; Prophecy; Falsehood; Disgrace; Anger; Reprehensibility
\eventfr Agent (punisher): Only God (in the corpus).
\syntagc Combination with verb: With the verb ‘to do’ (hf[ |i‘-s-h |i*). But this time the agent of the verb is not the same as the agent of the scandalous crime. Rather the agent of the verb is punishing the agent of the scandalous crime.
\level3 a
\context Penalty
\framec scandal: penalty
\domainc Death penalty; Sin; Speech; Prophecy; Falsehood; Disgrace; Anger; Reprehensibility
\eventfrc Agent (punisher): Only God (in the corpus). Targets (victims): Humans, those found to be guilty of sacrilege.
\keyref 2!J!(6!
\keyverse My servant Job will pray for you. And then I will accept his prayer not to punish you |bas severely as your sacrilegious talk about me deserves|b*. When you talked about me, you weren’t accurate, as my servant Job was. (MS)
\eval The set of ideas prompted by the use of this noun will often require an expansive translation similar to the above. Some elements may be omitted by simply conveying that God is not going to punish them as harshly as they deserve, as some modern versions do. A word like ‘folly’ does not render this noun’s meaning forcefully enough, despite CEV, NRSV, NCV, NIV and NASB.  See Is ‘folly’ an adequate translation equivalent?
\lxx avpw,lesa a'n u`ma/j ‘I would have destroyed you’Comments:If the Septuagint is reading the focus Hebrew noun, then it may be translating this aspect of its meaning. (But this verse differs from the Hebrew in other respects too.) Based on the other twelve uses of this word we might expect that Job’s three friends would also deserve the death penalty.
