\heb מִזְבֵּחַ
\strong 4196
\pos ncm
\gloss altar
\freq 403
\sub זבח
\intro 1. This word refers to an elevated physical structure dedicated to the worship of a deity.2. The elevated structure was a platform made of packed earth, piled stones, cut stones, wood overlaid or plated with a metal such as bronze or gold, or metal. The Israelites were not permitted to build an altar of cut stones (Exo 20:25).3. The structure was normally used as a stand for burning animal sacrifices or cereal offerings. An altar could also serve as a memorial marker, or as a place of refuge. The word also referred to a stand for burning incense.4. The structure was high enough to allow the officiator to work on it while standing at its base. In some cases, the structure was built in stages that required the officiator to mount steps to be able to work on it. The people of Israel were forbidden to build such altars (Exo 20:26).5. In the Old Testament, the word most frequently refers to the altar of burnt offering which stood in the tabernacle or temple just before the entrance to the sanctuary. This altar was eventually promoted as the only legitimate place to offer animal sacrifice and cereal offerings to the God of Israel. In the tabernacle, this altar was made of acacia wood overlaid by bronze. The temple altar is referred to as the bronze altar, but it is not clear whether it was made entirely of bronze or of wood overlaid with bronze.6. The word, especially when used in the plural, can refer to altars for pagan worship, such as for the worship of Baal.7. The word can also refer to the altar of incense which stood in the sanctuary of the tabernacle or the temple and served as a brazier for burning incense. In the tabernacle this was made of acacia wood overlaid with gold. In the temple, it is sometimes referred to as the gold altar.8. In certain contexts, an altar was built as a memorial marker, not as a platform for burning sacrifices or incense. In addition to serving as a platform for burning sacrifice or incense, it also served the function of a place of refuge from vengeance killing in cases of manslaughter.
\tnotes Care needs to taken when translating 'altar' in Tazi contexts. Any large animal killed for food is ritually 'sacrificed' and it is done on a floor set apart for that purpose. So a substantive referring to 'place of sacrifice' usually refers to a floor where a butcher works. It bears no resemblance to the physical structure of Old Testament altars and does not suggest an object used for worship.In traditional Christian contexts, churches of liturgical background may included a raised structure that is called an 'altar'. But care must be taken in these cases, as well. There are valid metaphorical comparisons between a Christian altar and the kinds of altar that were used in  Israelite worship. But in terms of actual form and function there are very significant differences. It would be helpful to the translator to look for pictures or diagrams of Old Testament altars in Bible dictionaries or handbooks before they decide on the proper description or designation of an altar in their translation.
\hebraist |hמִזְבֵּחַ|h* is a noun derived from the verb |hזבח|h* 'to slaughter, to sacrifice' using a |hם |h*preformitive . Such nouns 'are mainly abstract nouns, nouns of place and nouns of instrument' (Joüon and Muraoka 1993; §88Ld). In the vast majority of occurrences, |hמִזְבֵּח|h* is not abstract, but is referring to a concrete structure. However, an altar is a place of sacrifice.
\comp All occurrences of |hמִזְבֵּחַ|h* make reference to a physical structure. The primary function of the structure was to provide a platform for burning sacrifices and offerings. This primary function is clear in the first three meanings. Meaning 1 refers to altars that were built by the patriarchs in a variety of places, in a practice that was continued well after the development of the official cult centered around the altars in  the Tabernacle and the Temple, especially in the Northern Kingdom. A contextual use distinction was made for the altar that was built on Mount Ebal at the time the Israelites entered the land. It functions as both a place of sacrifice and as a memorial to the covenant law that had been established between God and the people while they were in the desert.Meaning 2 refers to the bronze altar that was a focus of official worship when the Tabernacle and later the Temple were established and the priesthood was instituted. Contextual uses were distinguished for the different altars in the Tabernacle and the various versions of the Temple. In addition, these altars could serve as a point of refuge and become references for expressions that refer to the activities of priesthood or congregational worship.Meaning 3 refers to altars that were dedicated to pagan gods. One contextual use is distinguished where the altar in Bethel is personified as the focus of guilt for northern kingdom's apostacy and as a result is punished.Meaning 4 refers to the altar that was built by the eastern tribes following the conquest. The western tribes interpreted its function as that of sacrifice. The eastern tribes insisted that it was built only as a reminder. The western tribes accepted that explanation which indicates that 'memorial' was an understood secondary function of a patriarchal style altar.Meaning 5 refers to the incense burner that was built for the Tabarnacle and the Temple. It did not function as a place of sacrifice, but the burning of incense was probably viewed as analagous to the burning of the animal sacrifices and cereal offerings, allowing the term 'altar' to be extended to refer to 'the altar of incense'.
\dfnotes The word 'altar' evokes the frame of Sacrifice and that frame unites all of the meanings even if only by analogy in the case of Meaning 5.  Though the frame is the same throughout, domains of patriarchal religion, priesthood, pagan religion, memorial, and purification rituals distinguish the various meanings of the word.
\level2 1
\meaning Altar, an elevated structure for sacrifice, built where significant interaction with God takes place
\frame Sacrifice
\domain Patriarchal Religion
\eventfr Event: to sacrificeAgent: humanBeneficiary: GodPatient: sacrifice or offeringLocation: headword
\syntagc The patriarchal altar was built for the purpose of offering sacrifices. Patriarchs and prophets built altars in response to interaction with God. The altars were built on the site where God appeared or revealed himself to them. These altars may had a memorial function in addition to being altars for sacrifice.

\context Altar built by an Israelite patriarch, leader, or prophet, for sacrifice and/or as a monument in response to interaction with God

\trans The French common language translation (FCL) keeps the reference to altar but explains it: 'to go up to my altar' becomes 'to offer sacrifices on my altar'.

\level3 h
\context The altar of burnt offering in the tabernacle (and presumably the temple) functioned as a site of refuge from vengeance in cases of manslaughter
\domainc Refuge, Justice
\syntagcc A man could not be killed while he grasped the altar. If the man was innocent of premeditated murder he could be protected against vengeance slaying. If he was guilty of premeditated murder he would be taken away from the altar and executed. Exodus 21:14 sets down the law and presumably refers to any altar. 1 Kings 1:50,51,53;2:28,29 provides examples of specific instances where the altar of burnt offering in the Tabernacle was used as a place of refuge. In the case of Adonijah the altar is not specifically named. In the case of Joab, it is the altar in the Tabernacle (the tent of the LORD). So, it seems likely that Adonijah also fled to the altar in the tabernacle.

\level2 4
\meaning An altar built as a memorial marker to the God of Israel, not as a platform for offering sacrifice.
\frame Sacrifice
\domain Memorial
\syntagc An altar is prototypically understood as place of sacrifice. But it could also serve as a memorial marker. Such a function is implicit in many of the contexts for Meaning 1. The altars are build on locations of significant interaction with God, often there is no mention of sacrifice being made, and one of the functions may be to remind those who follow of what happened. Exodus 20:24 refers to the altar as a "place where I cause my name to be remembered" (RSV). But the only place where an altar's function as a memorial marker is asserted and distinguished from its function as a place of sacrifice is in Joshua 22. In this passage, it is clear that the function as a place of sacrifice is what comes to mind when the western tribes hear that the eastern tribes are building their own altar. But they accept the eastern tribes assertion that the altar is only to serve as a memorial.

\level2 5
\meaning An incense burner, or the altar of incense, a raised structure used by the High Priest for burning incense to establish holiness within the sanctuary
\frame Sacrifice
\domain Ritual Purification, Tabernacle, Temple, Holiness
\eventfr Event =to burn incenseAgent =High PriestPatient =incenseBeneficiary =High PriestLocation =headword
\syntagc No sacrifice is done on the altar of incense. It is probably referred to as an altar because the burning of incense and the going up of its smoke is comparable to the burning of a sacrifice and the going up of its smoke. In addition it is comparable in shape, being square in its cross-section with upturned corners, called horns. It is likely that the burning of incense was viewed as analogous to the burning of a sacrifice.The actual function of the incense burned on the altar is not clearly described. In Exodus 30:7,8, the high priest is to offer incense on the altar when he goes into the sanctuary every morning and evening to take care of the lamps. In Leviticus 16:12-13, when the high priest enters the sanctuary to sprinkle blood on the covenant box, he is to bring in coals and incense, and burn the incense on the fire (the word 'altar' is not actually used in this instance). 'The smoke of the incense will conceal the atonement cover above the Testimony, so that he will not die' (Lev 16:13, NIV). Nielsen comments 'This incense cloud provides the high priest with cover against the divine wrath or the divine “radiation.” The incense smoke gives protection.'|nr|n* Another way to put this is to say that the incense smoke establishes holiness.

On the other hand, general references to Canaanite altars may refer to Baal worship, especially when in conjunction with references to Asherah.

\ver 0
\dat 2006-01-16 13:35:06 paul

\heb קנא
\level1 1
\strong 7065
\alt קנה
\pos v
\gloss to be jealous
\freq 34
\sub קַנָּא, קִנְאָה
\intro |hקנא|h* means to become angry in response to a challenge to one's honour.1) A person can provoke another to anger by challenging their honour;2) A person can be provoked to anger by what other people do that is perceived as a challenge to one's honour;3) A person can experience anger about someone else who represents a challenge to their honour, even though the other person does nothing to provoke the anger;4) A person can be provoked to anger in behalf of someone else whose honour has been challenged.
\tnotes Traditional English translations of |hקנא|h* use terms such as 'to be jealous' or 'to envy'. They are often misleading because they evoke the Frames of Hatred, Ownership, or Desire rather than the Frames of Anger and Honour / Shame. In some contexts such as Numbers 5, 'to be jealous' might be an appropriate translation, but in many others, English translations of 'jealousy' or 'envy' make little sense. More modern English translations use general words, like 'passion' or 'to be passionate'. In so doing they evoke the emotional intensity of |hקנא|h* but they don't help the reader to understand the reason for the intense emotion. The reason for the intense emotion would have been clear to a Hebrew speaker. |hקנא|h* is an understandable, but not always appropriate, response to an honour challenge. The OT understands this response well, but is ambivalent about whether it is always a desirable response.Because |hקנא|h* evokes two Frames and there is no comparable term in English, it is often necessary to use two or more words to carry the same semantic content. The translator would be wise to explore the semantic domain of Honour / Shame as well as Anger within the audience culture. If honour, and avoidance of shame, is a high value, then there may be a cultural equivalent of an agressive response to someone who challenges one's honour. If not, the KTBH translations offered here may provide helpful suggestions.
\hebraist The verb is found in the Piel stem (30x) and in the Hiphil stem (4x). The Hiphil stem expresses a causative relationship (someone causes someone else to become angry). In two occurrences the syntax and context indicates that the Piel stem should be understood as causative (Deu 32:21 and 1Ki 14:22).
\comp All of the meanings and uses described below evoke the same cognitive scenario: B challenges the reputation or honour of A A is threatened with shame. A |bexperiences anger|b*. A expresses that anger and attempts to reestablish his or her reputation. |hקנא|h* refers specifically to the experience of anger but brings to mind the whole scenario.There can be variations on the scenario. The challenge to honour may be attributed to the actions of a group or type of people, or it may be a more general state of affairs. The challenge to honour may be intentional or unintentional. In some cases, B does nothing to create a challenge; A simply feels threatened by B's status or reputation. Finally, B may challenge the reputation of C, but it is A that feels the anger on behalf of C. Protecting the reputation of someone else happens due to the close relationships between patrons and clients in Ancient Near Eastern social order.Different aspects of the scenario are brought into focus by how the scenario is expressed grammatically and these affect the nuance of meaning of the expression.Meaning 1: When a causative form is modified by a |hבְּ|h* prepositional phrase, B causes A to experience |hקנא|h* |iwhen|i* B does certain things. Meaning 2: When a non-causative form is intransitive and modified by a |hבְּ|h* prepositional phrase, A experiences |hקנא|h* |iwhen|i* B does certain things or |iwhen|i* certain things are done. Meaning 3: When a non-causative form is transitive and there is no |hבְּ|h* prepositional phrase, A experiences |hקנא|h* about B though B has done nothing to provoke a challenge.Meaning 4: When a non-causative form is intransitive and is modified by a |hלְ|h* prepositional phrase, A experiences |hקנא|h* on behalf of C (B's role as an Agent is left implicit).Contextual Uses are distinguished either by changes in the participants or by special contextual domains that are brought to mind. So, in Meanings 1 and 4, separate contextual uses are distinguished depending on whether God or humans are Experiencers of |hקנא|h*. Meaning 4 also has distinctions in contextual use depending on whether God or humans are Benefactives. In Meaning 3, different contextual uses are distinguished by whether the Honour challenge is in the context of rivalry for status or in the context of adultery.
\dfnotes |hקנא|h* evokes two Frames: Anger and Honour / Shame. It is used regularly in association with words for anger. The writer of Proverbs classifies it as a form of anger. Anger is an emotional response to a threat to a person's well-being. Various contexts indicate that when |hקנא|h* is used the anger is in response to a threat to a person's honour or status.
\level2 1
\meaning To provoke someone to anger by challenging their honour
\frame Anger; Honour / Shame
\domain Rivalry
\stem Hiphil and causative Piel
\gram The verb is transitive and always modified by a prepositional phrase using |hבְּ|h*. The subject encodes the Agent; the object encodes the Experiencer of the anger; the object of the preposition |hבְּ|h* encodes the Stimulus of the honour challenge.
\eventfr Agent: human or divineExperiencer: divine or humanStimulus: the context which indicates the challenge to honor
\syntagc The Agent that provokes the honour challenge is made explicit as the subject of the causative verb. The Experiencer of the emotion of anger is made explicit as the object. In the other three meanings, the Experiencer of the emotion is brought into focus as the subject of the verb.
\level3 a
\context To provoke God to anger by worshipping rival gods
\domainc Apostasy; Idolatry
\eventfrc Agent: human = people of GodExperiencer: GodStimulus: activity that threatens honour = worshipping rival gods or doing evil
\syntagcc The object of the prepositional phrase which expresses the stimulus is often a metonymy where a thing refers to an event. So 'with strange (gods)' would refer to 'by worshipping and serving foreign gods'; 'with their sins' would refer to 'by the sins that they do'.
\keyref 3!n!Z$!
\keyverse They |bchallenged the honour|b* of their God and provoked him to anger by worshipping idols and serving in their sanctuaries. (KTBH)
\eval RSV provides a traditional translation: 'For they provoked him to anger with their high places; they |bmoved|b* him |bto jealousy|b* with their graven images'. But the English word 'jealousy' does not naturally lead the reader to think of honour and shame. CEV reads: 'God |bdemanded all their love|b*, but they |bmade him angry|b* by worshiping idols. (CEV)' The CEV avoids the word 'jealousy'. Its use of the phrase 'God demanded all their love' may be meaningful for an English audience, but it evokes the domain of marriage which is only a small part of the use of the word, |hקנא|h*. It may actually be misleading for non-Western languages which have a vocabulary for honour and challenges to honour. The KTBH translation makes the frame of Honour / Shame explicit in the translation and allows the anger word in the parallel line to carry the frame of Anger. It also rearranges the parallelism to make the cause and effect order more apparent. The parallelism could be retained by translating: 'They provoked God to anger by serving in sanctuaries of other gods; they |bchallenged his honour|b* by worshipping idols'.

\level3 b
\context [Of God] To provoke his people to anger by choosing another people
\domainc Covenant
\eventfrc Agent: divine = GodExperiencer: human = people of GodStimulus: activity that threatens honour = choice of a different people as covenant people
\syntagcc The prepositional phrase 'with no-people' would be a poetic figure of speech to describe people who were not a part of the people that God had chosen to love.
\keyref %!@!5'!
\keyverse They have shamed me by worshipping what is no god; they have provoked me to anger by serving idols. So I will|b shame them|b* by choosing those who are not my people; I will provoke them to anger by loving a foolish nation. (KTBH)
\eval RSV provides a traditional translation: 'They have stirred me to jealousy with what is no god; they have provoked me with their idols. So I will |bstir|b* them |bto jealousy|b* with those who are no people; I will provoke them with a foolish nation. (RSV)'. But in modern English, jealousy evokes the frames of possession or selfishness, not that of honour. It is possible to reframe a challenge to honour as an attempt to shame. That may be more natural in many languages. The KTBH translation translates using the Honour / Shame frame and allows the frame of Anger to be expressed by the parallel line.

\level2 2
\meaning To be provoked to anger or envy by a challenge to honor
\frame Anger; Honour / Shame
\domain Rivalry
\stem Piel
\gram The verb is intransitive and modified by the prepositional phrase with |hבְּ|h*. The subject encodes the Experiencer; the object of the preposition |hבְּ|h* encodes the Stimulus of the honour challenge.
\eventfr (Agent: human, implied by the scenario)Experiencer: humanStimulus: activity that threatens honour = rivalry
\level3 a
\context To be embittered by the actions of someone or some group that challenges one's honour
\domainc Wisdom
\eventfrc (Agent: human, implied by the scenario = the wicked man)Experiencer: human = the wise personStimulus: the context which indicates the challenge to honor = the way of life of the wicked man
\syntagcc In the wisdom literature, |hקנא|h* looses some of the intensity of its anger. Anger may turn inward and become bitterness or frustration. But the sense of an honour challenge is still there. Wealth or success contributes to honour. And wicked men are often successful or wealthy. Such success challenges the honour of the wise man (Psalm 73:3). The wise man is tempted to become angry and embittered (Psalm 37:1; Proverbs 24:19). Most English translations use 'envy' to translate |hקנא|h* in these passages. It is possible that bitterness leads the wiseman to desire to imitate the 'successful' ways of the wicked (Proverbs 3:31, 24:1) and that is what is being expressed in these passages. But these would be unusual uses of the word. All of these passages make sense if |hקנא|h* is understood as an emotion leading to suppressed anger, or bitterness.
\keyref 3!i!#!!
\keyverse For I |bfelt shamed|b* by the arrogant, |bbitter|b* when I saw that their wickedness made them prosperous. (KTBH)
\eval The RSV reads: 'For I was envious of the arrogant, when I saw the prosperity of the wicked'. Arrogance is a negative quality. So, it is not at all clear why a person would be envious of the arrogant, since it would imply that the psalmist is attracted to the arrogant. But that makes no sense in the context. The KTBH translation tries to bring out the comparison the psalmist is making between his own life and that of the arrogant. They seem to have prospered and been honoured, while he has suffered and been dishonoured. He wonders whether he has lived a wise life in vain (see verse 13).

\colc חרה
\pgloss (Hithpael) to fret, become angry

\paradigc Opposite_Term
\colc אוה
\pgloss (Hithpael) to crave

\level3 b
\context To be provoked to anger by the actions or attitudes of someone specific
\eventfrc Experiencer: humanEffector: human who creates the stimulus which is the challenge to honor
\syntagcc In both of these passages the object of the prepositional phrase is a person. But the use of the preposition indicates a different realization of the scenario than if the person is the simple object of the verb (see Meaning 3). In both instances the context indicates that the Effector has definitely provoked the anger of the Experiencer by challenging the honour of the experiencer. In the case of Leah the provocation was intentional. In the case of Joseph it was probably not intentional but the result of being naïve.
\keyref !!>!!'!
\keyverse Rachel had not borne Jacob any children. She was |bshamed|b* by this and |bprovoked to anger|b* by her sister. She said to Jacob: "Give me sons or I will die!" (KTBH)
\eval RSV translates: 'When Rachel saw that she bore Jacob no children, she envied her sister; and she said to Jacob, “Give me children, or I shall die!"' The word 'envied' seems weak in light of the context. CEV translates: 'Rachel was very jealous of Leah for having children, and she said to Jacob, “I'll die if you don't give me some children!”' This translation indicates the intensity of Rachel's emotion, but 'jealousy' doesn't carry a sense of provocation. The phrase, 'She saw that she had not borne Jacob any children', hints that Rachel perceived that her infertility had become a public talking point. As a result, she was shamed and responded to her sister's provocation with anger, directed at Jacob. Jacob's reaction to Rachel's implicit slight of his honour is anger in response to anger.

\refsc !!>!!'!

\level2 3
\meaning To feel anger about someone in defense of one's honour
\frame Anger; Honour / Shame
\domain Rivalry
\stem |ipiel|i*
\gram The verb is transitive and is not governed by a prepositional phrase. The subject encodes the experiencer of the emotion, the direct object encodes the target of the emotion.
\eventfr Experiencer: humanTarget: human = focus of the emotion (without an implication of provocation)
\syntagc In this meaning, the focus is on the experience of jealousy, not on what may have provoked the feelings of jealousy (compare with meaning 2). In Numbers 5, the man has a fit of jealousy about his wife but he can not prove that she has done anything to provoke him to jealousy. The ritual is designed to decide the issue for him. Or, in the case of Isaac, he did nothing to provoke the jealousy of the Philistines. The fact that he had become more wealthy than they was sufficient challenge to their honour.
\level3 a
\context To feel anger in regard to someone because they are rivals to his reputation or honour
\domainc Status
\eventfrc Experiencer: human = individual or collectiveTarget: human = individual or collective
\keyref !!:!.'"
\keyverse |u13|u* Isaac became great. He went on becoming great until he was very great indeed. |u14|u* He had acquired such a large number of sheep and cattle, and so many servants, that the Philistines were |bshamed and became angry|b* about his |bsuccess|b*. (KTBH)
\eval Many versions translate 'great' as 'wealthy', which is a legitimate translation in this context. For an English-speaker that then makes sense of the translation of 'the Philistines envied him'. But it doesn't help explain the Philistine reaction. Rather than try to appropriate his goods, they disable his wells and ask him to leave the region (following translations like NIV, TEV, CEV). 'To be great' is 'to have honour' and the Philistines were threatened to the point of anger in comparison to him. So they shamed him and made him leave the region. KTBH uses 'success' here because it is an honour word that fits the context.

\level3 b
\context To feel jealous of one's wife because one suspects that she has committed adultery
\domainc Marriage
\eventfrc Experiencer: human = maleTarget: human = wife
\syntagcc Numbers 5 is one of the few contexts were |hקנא|h* fits well with the traditional translation of 'to be jealous'. But it should be noted that the OT concern still doesn't accord very well with the scenario that 'jealousy' arrouses for a Western person. The primary concern is still honour. The husband is concerned that the wife has defiled herself, and by extension, sullied his honour.
\keyref $!%!.$"
\keyverse ...when in such a case a fit of jealousy comes over the husband which |bmakes|b* him |bsuspect|b* his wife, whether she is defiled or not... (REB)
\eval RSV translates the passage quite literally. REB translates 'he is jealous' as 'he suspects'. REB avoids a lot of redundancy. 'He suspects' looses the emotional intensity that is a part of |hקנא|h*, but it makes up for that by translating 'a spirit of jealousy' as 'a fit of jealousy'.

\level2 4
\meaning To be provoked to anger in defense of someone else's honor, to be zealous for someone
\frame Anger; Honour / Shame
\domain Patronage
\stem Piel
\gram The verb is intransitive. It is modified by the prepositional phrase |hלְ|h*. The subject encodes the Experiencer of the emotion; the prepositional phrase with |hלְ|h* encodes the Benefactive.
\eventfr (Agent=not in focus, determined from the context)Experiencer: God or humanBenefactive: human or God
\syntagc The ancient world was characterised by patron-client relationships. Part of the responsibility of the relationship was for the client to protect the honour of the patron, because his own honour was closely bound up in the honour of the patron. In a similar way, part of the responsibility of the patron was to protect, in an appropriate way, the honour of the client. We see both of the these directions in the use of |hקנא.|h*
\level3 a
\context To be provoked to anger in defense of the honor of another person
\eventfrc Experiencer: humanBenefactive: human
\keyref $!+!=$!
\keyverse Moses answered, "Why should you be |bso upset|b* about my |bhonour|b*? I wish that the LORD would give his spirit to all his people so that they became prophets!" (KTBH)
\eval |i'Are you jealous on my account?|i*' (NJB) is an unusual use of the word 'jealous'. Jealousy is a selfish emotion in modern English. It would make more sense to use 'zealous' but it is too scholarly for modern English translations. TEV's '|iAre you concerned about my interests?|i*' comes closer to the meaning of the Hebrew word but emasculates the emotional content. The KTBH translation helps to identify Joshua's chief concern, the honour of his partron, and gives a sense of the emotional intensity of the word. Finally, a Why question is much more a rhetorical rebuke in English than is a Yes-No question. Context clearly indicates that Moses intended it as a rebuke.
\ref $!+!=$! *!5!"5!
\paradigc 
\level3 b
\context To be provoked to anger in defense of the honor of God
\eventfrc Experiencer: humanBenefactive: God
\syntagcc Zeal for God seems to nurture emphatic statements. In Numbers 25:11, Phineas literally 'zeals God's zeal'. In 1 Kings 19:10 & 14, Elijah says,'Being zealous I have been zealous for the LORD'. In either situation we could say that Phineas or Elijah were very zealous to defend God's honour.
\keyref $!9!-*!
\keyverse He and his descendants are permanently established as priests, because he |bpassionately protected my honour|b* and brought about forgiveness for the people's sin. (author's translation)
\eval NIV's '|ihe was zealous for the honor of his God'|i* is a nice translation, though it is a bit scholarly. TEV's '|ihe did not tolerate any rivals to me|i*' is a good example of a possible rendering of |hקנא|h* here as in several other places. And it does suggest the emtional intensity. However, TEV uses a sort of double negative ('to not tolerate any rivals') which actually gives a negative connotation to the expression. Since tolerance is a very high positive value in Western cultures, to suggest that God isn't tolerant may be seen negatively. The author's translation tries to states Phineas' reaction in a positive manner.

\level3 c
\context [Of God] To be provoked to anger in defense of the honour of his people
\domainc Patronage; Judgment / Compassion
\eventfrc Experiencer: GodBenefactive: human = God's people|ni|n*
\syntagcc God's zeal for his people can express itself in compassion or mercy toward them (Ezekiel 39:25, Joel 2:18), or in wrath toward their enemies (Zechariah 8:2).
\keyref =!"!2!!
\keyverse |u17b|u* “Oh, spare Your people, Lord! Do not let your people become a mockery, to be taunted by nations! Do not let other nations say, ‘Where is their God?’” |u18|u* Then the LORD became angry at the shame inflicted on the people of his land and he had compassion on them. (KTBH)
\eval The last part of verse 17 is included to show the prior context. Mockery and taunting are shaming actions. The question "Where is your God?" shames the client by casting doubts on the honor of the patron. NJPS's 'Then the Lord was roused on behalf of His land' indicates God's emotional response, but it is an intensity without content. God's response is anger toward his people's rival and compassion for his people.

\not (a) The Hiphil participle is modifying the phrase 'the idol of jealousy' and is usually translated as a relative clause 'the idol of jealousy which provokes to jealousy'. The expression is figurative. The idol isn't the Agent of provocation, since it is an inanimate object. Rather it is those who make or worship the idol that provoke God to anger. So, a possible translation would be: '...where the shameful idol stands. Those who worship it provoke God to anger. (KTBH) The advantage of such a translation is that it evokes both the frames of Anger and Honour / Shame, and it avoids the redundancy of a literal translation.

\not (b) All the English translations consulted use 'envy' here. The question is whether the writer is using similar or contrastive parallelism. If we understand the verse to be using similar parallelism the idea would be: Don't let your envy of the man of violence lead you to choose his ways. If we understand the verse to be using contrastive parallelism the idea would be: Don't let yourself become embittered by the violent man, nor should you choose to follow his ways.

\not (d) See verses 4,  5 & 8. Hatred is more closely associated with jealousy in Western thought than is anger. But in this passage, |hקנא|h* is a step beyond hatred. Jacob's favoritism toward Joseph and Joseph's naive boasting created resentment, or hatred, in the brothers. He was a disgusting pest. But when Joseph shamed their father in front of them by suggesting that they and their father would bow down to him, their honour was seriously challenged. Their anger flared, but their father kept his feelings hidden. For a Hebrew speaker, using |hקנא|h* at this point in the narrative would foreshadow the action that the brothers will eventually take in regard to Joseph.

\not (e) The syntax of |hקנא|h* in Psalm 106:16 is odd. It suggests that it should be grouped with meaning 4. But context indicates that it makes most sense here in Meaning 3a. The anomaly may be because of Aramaic influence where the preposition |hלְ|h* marks the direct object of verb. If that is the case than this occurrence fits neatly into this contextual use.

\not (f) Both Isaiah 11:13 and Ezekiel 31:9 involve figures of speech. In Isaiah, Ephraim and Judah stand for the northern and southern kingdoms. In Ezekiel, the cedar of Lebanon is a proud nation (whether Egypt or Assyria depends on the interpretation) and the other trees are the rest of the nations. But in both cases the verb is used in a non-metaphorical sense. Just as an individual can be jealous of another individual, so people of one nation can feel threatened by the success of other nations.
\notfr (f) NOTE: Both Isaiah 11:13 and Ezekiel 31:9 involve figures of speech. In Isaiah, Ephraim and Judah are personifications for the northern and southern kingdoms. In Ezekiel, the cedar of Lebanon is a proud nation (whether Egypt or Assyria depends on the interpretation) and the other trees are the rest of the nations. But in both cases the verb is used in a non-metaphorical sense. Just as an individual can be jealous of another individual, so a nation can be jealous of another nation.
\notsp (f) NOTE: Both Isaiah 11:13 and Ezekiel 31:9 involve figures of speech. In Isaiah, Ephraim and Judah are personifications for the northern and southern kingdoms. In Ezekiel, the cedar of Lebanon is a proud nation (whether Egypt or Assyria depends on the interpretation) and the other trees are the rest of the nations. But in both cases the verb is used in a non-metaphorical sense. Just as an individual can be jealous of another individual, so a nation can be jealous of another nation.

\not (i) It is not always clear in the following references that the benefactive is God's people. In Joel 2:18, God is zealous for 'his land' which is a metonymy for his people, as the parallel line indicates. In Zechariah passages, Jerusalem and Zion are metonymies for God's people. In Ezekiel 39:25 God is zealous for his name, or for his reputation. But context makes clear that it is God's reputation as a God who loves his people that is at stake. Though God's honour may be the ultimate benefactive, for that to happen God's people have to be the immediate benefactive.

\heb קַנָּא
\alt קַנּוֹא
\pos a
\pos ncm
\gloss jealous
\freq 8
\sub קנא
\intro |hקַנָּא|h* or |hקַנּוֹא|h* describe a God who will respond in anger to protect his honour against a rival.
\tnotes See the discussion under |hקנא|h*.
\hebraist In seven out of eight occurrences he adjective modifies the noun |hאֵל|h* (God). On the other occasion, in Exodus 34:14 it appears to act as a substantive representing the name of |hיְהוָה|h* (the LORD or YHWH).
\level2 1
\meaning Characteristic of being diligent to protect one's honour against any challenge

\level3 a
\context God is characterized as a God who will respond in anger to a presistent challenge to his honour on the part of his people
\keyref %!&!/#!

(RSV)...15 If you do worship other gods, the Lord's anger will come against you like fire and will destroy you completely, because the Lord your God, who is present with you, tolerates no rivals. (TEV)...because the Lord your God, who is present with you, will defend his honour. (author's translation)
\eval In this case, the TEV translation is quite good and fits the context well. However, if the translation team has found the Honour frame to be meaningful, they could follow the author's suggestion.

\dat 2006-01-10 21:10:38 paul

\heb קִנְאָה
\strong 7068
\pos ncf
\gloss jealousy, envy
\freq 43
\sub קנא
\intro |hקִנְאָה|h* refers to the experience of becoming angry or anxious in response to a challenge to one's honour.1) A person can provoke another to anger by challenging their honour;2) A person can experience anger or anxiety in response to a challenge to honor;3) A person can be provoked to anger in behalf of someone else whose honour has been challenged.
\tnotes Traditional English translations of |hקִנְאָה|h* use terms such as 'jealousy' or 'envy'. They are often misleading because they evoke the frames of ownership, possessiveness, or selfishness for an English speaker, rather than the frame of honour or reputation. In some contexts such as Number 5, 'jealousy' might be an appropriate translation, but in many others, English translations of 'jealousy' or 'envy' make little sense. More modern English translations use general words, like 'passion'. In so doing they evoke the emotional intensity of |hקִנְאָה|h* but they don't help the reader to understand the reason for the intense emotion. The reason for the intense emotion would have been clear to a Hebrew speaker.The translator would be wise to explore the semantic domain of honour/shame within the audience culture. If honour, and avoidance of shame, is a high value, then there may be a cultural equivalent of an agressive response to something that challenges one's honour. |hקִנְאָה|h* is an understandable, but not always appropriate response to an honour challenge. The OT understands this response well, but is somewhat ambivalent about whether it is always a desirable response.
\hebraist |hקִנְאָה|h* is an abstract noun formed from the verb |hקנא|h*. It expresses the event of being provoked to anger over a challenge to honour or the state of being angry due to an honour challenge. It can be absolute noun, the head of a construct phrase, or a construct noun itself. It is only modified by an adjective twice, both times in Zechariah by the adjective |hגָּדוֹל |h*(1:14, 8:2). It can serve as the object of the preposition |hב־|h* (5x), and of the preposition |hכ־|h* (1x). It can be modified by a personal possessive pronoun. In all cases the possessive pronoun refers to the experiencer of the emotion. When |hקִנְאָה |h*is the head of a construct phrase the noun in construct may refer to the experiencer (c.f., Ecclesiastes 4:4), or to the beneficiary of the emotion (c.f., Psalm 69:10). Context determines whether the pronoun should be understood as experiencer or beneficiary.  |hקִנְאָה|h* only occurs in the plural in Numbers 5 where it is a part of two different technical terms: 'offering of jealousies' or 'ritual of jealousies'.
\level2 1
\meaning What provokes someone to anger by challenging their honour
\frame Anger; Honour / Shame
\domain Idolatry
\gram |hקִנְאָה|h* serves as absolute noun in construct with |hסֶמֶל|h* (idol). The construct noun encodes the agent, the absolute noun the experience of anger. The experiencer and effector are determined by context.
\level3 a
\context God is provoked to anger by the worship of foreign idols
\eventfrc Agent=image of foreign god; metonymy for those who make and worship this foreign godExperience=emotion of angerExperiencer=God, by implicationEffector=worship of a foreign god, by implication
\keyref :!(!#:!
\keyverse He put forth the form of a hand, and took me by a lock of my head; and the Spirit lifted me up between earth and heaven, and brought me in visions of God to Jerusalem, to the entrance of the gateway of the inner court that faces north, where was the seat of the image of jealousy, which provokes to jealousy. (RSV)...where the idol which shames God stands. Those who worship it provoke God to anger. (author's translation)
\eval RSV provides a literal, traditional translation. If such a translation doesn't work well, it would be possible to unpack the image according to the author's translation. The two different occurrences of the |hקנא|h* root are translated so as to bring out the two different aspects of the root: honour/shame and anger.

\level2 2

\syntagcc |hקִנְאָה |h*is used in a general way. The participant roles are not usually very clear. Meaning is determined by context. In most cases it is fairly clear that a man's competition with his rivals for honour or status is what is in focus.
\keyref 5!$!$)!

\eval RSV's 'envy' is not a bad translation, here. But it does seem a little weak. REB's 'rivalry' fits the context much better and underlines the idea of competition for status or honour.

\level3 b

\syntagcc In both cases, the context indicates that God is preparing to act on behalf of his people. So, there is a sense in which these two occurrences could be put into meaning 3. However, in both cases |hקִנְאָה|h* is describing the image of the warrior. The picture is of a warrior who boasts of his prowess and superiority, and prepares to defend his honour in battle.
\keyref 7!J!-'!

(REB)The LORD will go forth as a warrior, a soldier roused to defend his honour in battle; he will shout, he will raise the battle cry, defying his enemies. (author's translation, c.f., BFC--la Bible en français courant)
\eval REB translates |hקִנְאָה|h* as "the fury of battle", which is nice. However, it doesn't bring out the idea of honour that is so important for warriors. The author's translation attempts to do so. In addition, it brings out an insight evident in BFC that |hגבר|h* in the |ihitpa'el |i*may refer to defying someone, see also HALOT.

\keyref $!%!/3!

\eval The RSV is quite literal and ignores the secondary sense of the |ihiphil |i*of |hזכר|h* (to remember). It can mean 'to make known' (HALOT). The offering and the ritual that goes along with it are designed to determine whether or not the wife is guilty of adultery and so to relieve the husband of his anxiety that his honour has been defiled by his wife. TEV corrects that misunderstanding and is very idiomatic.

\keyref 7!:!+'!
\keyverse O Lord, thy hand is lifted up, but they see it not. Let them see thy zeal for thy people, and be ashamed. Let the fire for thy adversaries consume them. (RSV)Your hand is raised and ready to punish them, but they don't see it. Put them to shame! Show how much you care for us and throw them into the fire intended for your enemies. (CEV)...Put them to shame and show how much you care for the honour of your people. Throw them into the fire intended for your enemies. (author's translation)
\eval The image of the hand raised to punish is made explicit in CEV. But they miss the contrast between honour for God's people and shame for his enemies that is explicit in the contrast between the noun |hקִנְאָה|h* and the verb |hבושׁ|h*. The author's translation provides an example of how that could be done.

\not (q) NOTE: The syntax is similar to Zechariah 8:2. What is interesting in Numbers 25:11 is that the pronominal reference on the infinitive has to refer to Phineas as experiencer of the emotion, while the pronominal reference on the noun must refer to God as experiencer of his own emotion. The construction seems to equate the intensity of Phineas' zeal for God's honour with the intensity of God's jealousy for his own honour. So a possible translation in English would be: 'He was as zealous to protect my honour in their midst as I was. So I did not consume the people of Israel in my zeal to protect my honour.'

\heb בין
\strong #1067
\pos v
\freq 171
\sub בַּיִן, בִּינָה, בֵּנַיִם, תְּבוּנָה
\intro The verb |hבין|h* expresses a large continuum of mental activities with respect to an object. The verb can be intransitive, transitive and causative, but the difference does not always show up in the morphology. To put it more simply, not every hif'il expresses a causative. Its meaning range is as follows, in ascending order of mental participation:1. To observe with active participation of the mind     a. to notice, to take note of    b. to consider attentively, to pay heed to    c. to pay positive and/or respectful attention to someone or something    d. to see though the outward appearance of things: to understand something, to have/gain insight into   something; to conclude    e. to |iact|i* in a way that shows insight and discernment    f. to be able to perceive and see through the outward appearance of things, to have a discerning mind    g. to be capable of performing an activity; (more specifically) to be skilled in a profession    h. to examine, to check2. (Causative) To provide someone with discernment and understanding of something; to teach, explain something to someone    a. to explain something to someone in order to convince him    b. to explain Scripture to someone    c. to provide someone with (divine) understanding (regarding something)
\hebraist According to the grammars Gesenius-Kautzsch-Cowley (73a) and Joüon-Muraoka (54f; 81d) the usual and old form is |hהבין|h* ; hence the imperfect |hיבין|h* is a Hifil. The forms that morphologically cannot be but Qal forms are probably secondary hypercorrections, since the Hifil had acquired a causative meaning, which it originally (with respect to |hבין|h*) did not have.
\evalev Evidence of deutero-canonical books has not yet been covered.
\level2 1
\meaning TO OBSERVE WITH ACTIVE PARTICIPATION OF THE MIND
\frame Mental observation
\stem Qal, Nif'al, Polel, Hitpolel, Hif'il
\syntagc The contextual meanings are listed according to the increasing degree of mental activity involved
\level3 a
\context To notice, to take note of
\domainc Wisdom, organization, cooking, punishment
\keyref 0!-!'#"
\keyverse NIV84 - [I] came back to Jerusalem. Here I learned about the evil thing Eliashib had done in providing Tobiah a room in the courts of the house of God.
\eval This use of the verb is closest to mere 'seeing'. It involves a minimal exertion of the mind. For example, if you see a herd of sheep you may |inote|i* a billy-goat among them. You were not really looking for that goat, but you simply notice it, which means that you activate your knowledge of the differences between goats and sheep. In CEV Neh 13:7 reads 'I found out'. This is less appropriate because it suggests investigation on Nehemia's part.

\keyverse REB89 - I said: ‘Here am I! Send me.’ He replied: ‘Go, tell this people: However hard you listen, you will never understand. However hard you look, you will never perceive.
\eval As in many verses quoted here, an explicit distinction is made between hearing and understanding. Translations are therefore unanimous in using 'to understand' of its equivalents. But in other passages different words may be used, as long as they do not suggest an intuitive type of wisdom, for this is not rational enough for this Hebrew verb.

\heb תְּבוּנָה
\level1 1
\strong #9312
\pos ncf
\freq 42
\sub .בינה. בין
\intro The term denotes insight and understanding that go beyond common sense,such as the special skill that uses the craftsman's techniques, but in an expert way. It can be applied to God and men. The reader be referred especially to Cassuto's explanations sub (b).
\level2 1
\meaning Understanding, insight
\frame Insight, prudence, skill
\level3 a
\context Understanding, insight
\domainc Wisdom, instruction, happiness, limitations, folly, judgement, idolatry
\keyref 4!"!"&#
\keyverse CEVUK - Keep in tune with wisdom and think what it means to have common sense.
\eval CEV's choice for 'common sense' seems attractive, but if we browse through the uses of |hתבונה|h*, it just not common sense that is meant, but insight that goes beyond it.

\level3 b
\context Skill, insight (human)
\domainc Craft, tent making, building, sculpting, trade, idolatry
\keyref "!?!#&#
\keyverse NIV84 - and I have filled [Besal'el] with the Spirit of God, with skill, ability and knowledge in all kinds of crafts.
\eval In his Exodus commentary, Cassuto very aptly paraphrases the three terms as follows (31:3):|b"with wisdom [|hחכמה|h*] - that is, expert knowledge of the techniques of workmanship and the ability to employ them;|bwith understanding [|hתבונה|h*] - that is, the capacity to deduce one thing from another and to find a way of solving every problem that might arise in the course of the work;|bwith knowledge [|hדעת|h*] - that is, the store of expertness that continues to grow relative to a basic skill as a result of practical experience." (pp. 401-402, likewise Durham, Exodus (WBC) ad loc.).From these definitions we should rather translate the terms as, respectively: knowledge, insight/understanding, expertness. It makes sense to translate |hדעת|h* as ex|hpertness, because the verb  ידע means 'to be familiar with’.  |h*
\ref "!?!#&# "!C!?&" "!D!!+" %!@!<'! +!'!.,$ :!<!$"# <!-!"("

\colc |eחָכְמָה|e*
\pgloss wisdom
\refsc "!?!#&# "!C!?&" "!D!!+" +!'!.,$ :!<!$"#

\domainc Kingship

\keyverse NIV84 - And David shepherded them with integrity of heart; with skillful hands he led them.
\eval The characteristic of the (right type of) king is here compared to that of a shepherd. Words like wisdom, tact and insight suggest themselves.

\level3 d
\context Skill, insight (divine)
\domainc Creation, divine qualities, primeval battle
\syntagcc The creation of the world is metaphorically depicted as a craft, esp. in JER 10:12; 51:15, where the sky is pitched like a tent.
\keyref 8!*!,'# 8!S!/'#
\keyverse NJPS - He made the earth by His might, Established the world by His wisdom, And by His understanding stretched out the skies.
\eval Neither 'understanding' nor 'wisdom' seem to be the right term here. In fact the same word for 'skill' is of human craftsman, but then metaphorically applied to YHWH as the divine craftsman. Therefore the rendering to be chosen should not be too far removed from notions of craftsmanship.

\not (a) The translation of תבונה in this difficult verse depends on whether and how the participant change is being resolved:   * If the final clause is changed from 3rd person to 2nd person (as in NIV and CEV), it refers to the unintelligent preparation for battle;   * if the 3rd person is maintained, the clause refers to the enemies' seemingly irrational behaviour.
